Jehudah permits this kind of sowing outright, not, as assumed here, forbidden but not punishable.</i>? Then it is difficult: what is the reason of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">He declares any illegal sowing punishable.</i>? Since he threw it out from his hand as seed, he is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse reads, “do not sow your field <i>kilaim</i>,” the act of sowing is forbidden, not its consequence in the soil. This explains why one had to state earlier that in respect of the laws of Sabbath, even R. Simeon ben Laqish requires that the seed come to rest on the ground.</i>. But did we not state that “Rebbi Jehudah says, it is not <i>kilaim</i>?” Explain it, until the moment that it comes to rest<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish states his rule according to the rabbis. He will agree that R. Jehudah requires that the seeds actually fall to the ground, since it says “do not sow <i>your field kilaim</i>;” even if he had the intention of sowing <i>kilaim</i> but the wind carried the seeds away, there is no criminal act committed. Hence, in this interpretation intent alone cannot be the determining factor.</i>. But was it not stated that Rebbi Judah permits it? Even if it came to rest, Rebbi Jehudah permits it<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">The previous interpretation is contradicted; R. Simeon ben Laqish only expresses his opinion according to the rules of the rabbis, not at all following R. Jehudah.</i>. Rebbi Illaï said, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish follows his own opinion, since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said in the name of Ḥizqiah: A vertex<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">ראש תור “ox head, trapezoid, vertex” describes the configuration in which two fields are not parallel but are at an angle to each other, so that a vertex of one field touches the other field in the middle of a side. (Musaphia wants to derive the expression from the Greek adjective τορός, ά, όν “sharp, piercing,” but probably it means ראש תור, “head of the line.”) It is spelled out in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 3:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.3.3.1">Mishnah 3:3</a> that in this case, no minimal distance has to be observed since the fields appear distinct by their neat geometrical form. Ḥizqiah adds that even if one of the fields does not have a regular shape, e. g., if it ends in a dry spot, if in the neighborhood of the other field it forms a triangle with a vertex touching the other field the leniency of ראש תור applies.<br>Cf. the discussion of ראש תור in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.5.1">Chapter 2, Note 101</a>.</i> from a dry spot is permitted. Then it also should be thus in the case of two wheat grains and one of barley<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">The one barley grain should be ראש תור between the row of two wheat grains!</i>? That is correct. But did we not state, “unless there are two wheat kernels and one barley kernel, or one wheat kernel and two barley kernels, or one each of wheat, barley, and spelt?” Explain it if there is a grain of wheat on each side, a fence on each side, and the grain of barley caught in the middle<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the configuration of ראש תור is excluded since the field represented by the barley grain enters the field represented by the wheat grains, and the minimal distances of separation will have to be observed. If the three grains are sown together in a straight line, the separation requirements are certainly violated.</i>. Rebbi Mattaniah said, that is only if there is no dry spot, but if there is a dry spot there, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">Since Ḥizqiah admitted a ראש תור pointing to a dry spot, if the three kernels were sown in the neighborhood of a dry spot, R. Jehudah will recognize the barley grain as ראש תור. But in other circumstances, ראש תור is only admitted for rectangular fields. In the absence of a dry spot, the barley grain cannot possibly count as ראש תור and is forbidden.</i>.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Every <i>seah</i> that contains a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter <i>qab</i> which is <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> <i>seah</i>. Here one speaks about seeds. Since seeds usually come mixed with weeds and other seeds, some sowing of different kinds together is unavoidable. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.1.1"><i>Baba Batra</i>, Mishnah 6:2</a>, up to a quarter <i>qab</i> of foreign material in a <i>seah</i> of grain is established as the standard accepted in any trading contract unless otherwise specified. From the Biblical requirement, it is only necessary that the farmer have no intention of sowing the undesirable seeds with his crop. But in order to avoid the appearance of intentional <i>kilaim</i>, the rabbis gave an upper limit also of one <i>qab</i> in 24 before requiring action.</i> of seeds from another kind should be diminished<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Some of the foreign seeds have to be taken out, so that the entire contamination is brought below the limit. R. Yose requires that once one has started cleaning up the seeds, all foreign seeds should be eliminated.</i>; Rebbi Yose says, he must pick it out, whether it be from one kind or from two kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Kinds of weeds and contamination with other crops. Rebbi Simeon says that each kind of contamination has its own limit, Rebbi Yose says that all those are counted together which are <i>kilaim</i> in any way; the Sages say that everything which is <i>kilaim</i> with the main crop is added together. So if wheat seed is contaminated with barley, <i>zewanin</i>, and peas, and together they are more than one in 24 but any two of them together are less than that amount, then for the Sages one does not have to act since <i>zewanin</i> are not <i>kilaim</i> with wheat, but R. Yose would require that the entire seed grain be sifted since <i>zewanin</i> are <i>kilaim</i> with peas.</i>. Rebbi Simeon said, they said it only for one kind, but the Sages say, everything that is <i>kilaim</i> with the <i>seah</i> counts together for a quarter.<br>When is this said<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">That a quarter <i>kab</i> of foreign seeds makes a <i>seah</i> of seeds unusable.</i>? Grain with grain, legumes with legumes, grain with legumes, legumes with grain<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">All these need approximately one <i>seah</i> for a field of 2500 square cubits (a <i>bet seah</i>). But if seeds need appreciably less than that, the amount which makes seed grain unusable is 1 in 24 of the amount used for the contaminant. It was already explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:1:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.1.5">Halakhah 1, Note 22</a>, that for kitchen greens whose seeds are not edible, the amount of seed used for one <i>bet</i> <i>seah</i> is only one sixth or one twelfth of a <i>seah</i>. The amount of these seeds sufficient to make a <i>seah</i> of seed grain unusable is <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> of either a <i>qab</i> or half a <i>qab</i>.</i>. In truth, they said that garden seeds which are not eaten add up together for one in 24 when they fall on a <i>bet seah</i>. Rebbi Simeon said, that which they said to restrict, they also said for leniency. Flax seeds<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the Halakhah, text following <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.11">Note 44</a>.</i> in grain add up together for one in 24 when they fall on a <i>bet seah</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> We have stated, “which contains.” Some Tannaïm state<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In a parallel <i>baraita</i>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 1:16" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).1.16">Tosephta (<i>Kilaim</i> 1:16</a>) has the same language as the Mishnah.</i>, “into which fell.” Rebbi Mana said, he who says “which contains” means one in 24<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If 24 quarters contain 1 quarter of foreign material, that is 1 in 24 of the total, 1 in 23 of useful grain.</i>, he who says “into which fell” means one in 25<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Since a <i>seah</i> is 24 quarters, if an additional quarter fell in, then the total adds up to 25 quarters, and we have 1 quarter of rejects in 24 good ones.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, both for him who says “which contains” and for him who says “into which fell,” it means one in 24<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is the commercial standard, there is no reason to change the standard here. This is also the position of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 94a" href="/Bava_Batra.94a">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 94a</a>.</i>. How is that? “Which contains,” if it is cut, “into which fell,” if it is standing<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The first case deals with grain bought as grain. In the second case, the contamination of the seed grain is detected after sowing, when the grain already is growing. This proves that the standard applies also to growing produce, not only to seed grain, supporting the position of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Batra 94a:11:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Batra.94a.11.1">Tosaphot (<i>Baba Batra</i> 94a, <i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. סאה</a>) against R. Samuel ben Meïr.</i>.
What are we talking about<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">How can the case of the Mishnah appear legitimate?</i>? If he intended it for seed<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If he recognizes the impurities in the seeds and intends to grow them for themselves, then it is <i>kilaim</i> and the standards of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.1">Mishnah 1:9</a> apply. The implication is that the yield from these impurities may not be selected out after harvest and sold separately, because that would make the entire harvest <i>kilaim</i> and forbidden.</i> then even one grain of wheat is forbidden. If to mix in<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since no farmer may sell grain with more than the permitted impurities, at harvest time he and the grain wholesalers will see to it that the impurities are not more than 1 in 24. In order to get illegitimate seed, he would have to mix in impurities; this is forbidden and we are not concerned here with intentional lawbreakers.</i>, it is forbidden to mix. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Abba bar Ḥiyya<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 93b" href="/Bava_Batra.93b">Babli (<i>Baba Batra</i> 93b</a>), the name is Rav Abba bar Ḥiyya from Ktesiphon (on the Tigris) in the name of Rabba (Rav Abba bar Naḥmani, of the third generation after Rav.) In any case, it is clear that the author here is Babylonian and not identical with Rebbi Abba bar Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba.</i> in the name of Rav: He who takes out impurities from his neighbor’s heap has to give him good grain in their stead<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the neighbor could have sold his grain with the impurities under a standard contract, the one who sifts the grain causes him to lose the value of the volume of the impurities he removes. {The word צרור translated here as “impurity” is not the biblical word (I. money pouch, II. flintstone), but is connected with Hebrew צרר II (to be hostile), more directly with the corresponding Arabic ضوّ צ̇רּ (to be noxious, pernicious).}</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that one is forbidden to mix in; if you say that one is permitted to mix them back in, why require good grain instead?
If one sold wheat as [standard] good quality and it turned out to be sifted, may he take out the value of that quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since the standard contract allows for impurities, may the seller deliver only the <sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup>/<sub>24</sub> <i>seah</i> of seeds guaranteed by the standard contract or does the buyer have to pay extra for the good grain he receives instead of the standard impurities? This problem belongs to <i>Baba Batra</i>; it is treated here because the problem comes up naturally from the statement of Rav which belongs to <i>Baba Batra</i>.</i>? The words of the rabbis say that he cannot take out the value of that quarter, for Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Abba bar Ḥiyya said in the name of Rav: He who takes out impurities from his neighbor’s grain has to give him good grain in their stead. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The buyer noticed that the grain he bought contained more impurities than those allowed by the standard contract. He has the right to return the merchandise for a full refund (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Batra</i> 6:2</a>). He chose instead to clean up the grain. Everybody agrees that he does not have to pay for the volume of the half quarter which exceeds the standard. The question is, since he started cleaning and took all impurities out, does he pay only for the remaining grain or may the seller insist to be paid also for the quarter of impurities which would have been legal and is reflected in the price charged for a unit of volume?</i> removed one and one half quarters, may he take out the value of that quarter? Rebbi Ḥinena and Rebbi Mana. Rebbi Ḥinena said, he takes it out<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">He pays only for the sifted grain, at the rate of standard grain.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, he does not take it out, because he can say to him, if you had returned it to me, I would have bundled it for the Saracens who would have bought it; what that buyer sees is what he buys<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Saracens, Arab traders without fixed residence, buy and sell “as is”, without warranties. The buyer would have inspected the grain before buying and offered a price based on his inspection.</i>.
But when they were mixed up during transport<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">After the interruption by the theme from <i>Baba Batra</i>, one returns to the original question and answers that more than one in 24 may contaminate the harvest by accidents during transport from the field to the storage facility. From here on to the end of the Halakhah one deals exclusively with seed grain.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Next Mishnah, dealing with the details of the rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>. “Garden seeds which are not eaten” are seeds of kitchen greens which in themselves are not food. They are usually very small and the resulting contamination will be more than 1 in 24 at harvest time. Any statement introduced by “in truth” is unquestioned practice.</i>: “In truth, they said that garden seeds which are not eaten add up together for one in 24 when they fall on a bet <i>seah</i>.” And we stated on that, this is a <i>qab</i> or half a <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The standard of maximal contamination for seeds of kitchen greens is a <i>qab</i> for the larger seeds and half a <i>qab</i> for the smaller seeds. Since these seeds are so much smaller than seed grain, they cannot be 1 in 24 of the full <i>seah</i> since that would overwhelm all the grain, but only 1 in 144 or 1 in 288, depending on the size of the seeds. The standard is a contamination of 1 in 24 of the harvest, not the seeds.</i>. What could one do with the twenty-fourth part of half a <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">About 0.09 liter.</i>? For that, they are worried because of the bad impression<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The entire rule is rabbinical so people should not think the contamination was intentional. One forbids only accidental weeds or strange admixtures which exceed the commercial standard.</i>. They were not worried because of the bad impression from a lesser amount.
“Should be diminished<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the Mishnah.</i>.” How does he diminish? Either he removes from the quarter or he adds to the <i>seah</i>. But did not Rebbi Joḥanan say (Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan), for all prohibitions, if one added in error, it is permitted, if intentionally, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For example, if some dairy product fell into a meat dish, if the meat dish by volume is more than 60 times that of the dairy product, the contamination is declared minimal and the dish is permitted to be eaten. (This is the rabbinical standard.) If the ratio was less at the time of the accident but later some more of the meat dish was added inadvertently or unknowingly, the measure at the end is determining. But if the addition was made in the knowledge of the prior prohibited contamination, then the measure at the time of the accident is determining and the later addition must be disregarded. How can the Mishnah here permit intentionally adding to legalize the prohibited mixture?</i>. There you add to annul a Torah prohibition, but here you add because of the bad impression.
“Rebbi Yose says, he must pick it out.” What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? Because he started with the quarter, he must finish the entire quarter. Rebbi Yose agrees that if there was less than a quarter when he started, he does not need to attend to it<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If he started to remove the foreign seeds when he did not have to he may stop in the middle, before removing all of it. We do not say that the fact of his removing impurities shows that he is bothered by their presence and, therefore, any sowing would be intentional sowing of <i>kilaim</i> and forbidden.</i>. How is that done? If there was a quarter composed of two kinds, does he pick out one kind and that is sufficient, or does he have to pick out everything<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered since practice does not follow R. Yose.</i>?
“Rebbi Simeon said, they said it only for one kind,” not two kinds. How far? Up to the greater part of the <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the total of foreign seeds is less than 50% and each single contamination is less than 1 in 24, the seeds remain permitted. (Up to 12 contaminants of maximum volume are possible.)</i>. If he added afterwards, the grain returned to its permitted status<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon accepts the earlier interpretation that adding of the main seedstock makes everything permitted again.</i>. Just as Rebbi Simeon said, two kinds do not combine for prohibition, so he says that they do not combine for permission. How is that done? If there were twenty-three and one half quarters of wheat and half a quarter of barley, and into that fell less than a quarter of lentils<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">It is supposed here that the volume of the lentils is at least <sup>23.5</sup>/<sub>24</sub> = 0.9792 quarters but less than one quarter. In that case, if the lentils were judged against the combined volume into which they fell, it would be less than 1 in 24. But since the barley is not counted at all, the contamination is at least a full twenty-fourth of the volume of wheat grain and the mixture is forbidden.</i>, even so the two kinds would not combine for permission.
We have stated about this, “three or four <i>qab</i>,” hence two <i>qab</i> go by one in 24<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The rule of the first Mishnah covers all seeds which need between four and three <i>seah</i>, i. e., between ⅔ and ½ <i>seah</i> per <i>bet</i> <i>seah</i>. Hence, seeds that need less volume are considered kitchen vegetables and go by <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup>/<sub>24</sub>. In slightly different language, this is explicitly stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 1:16" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).1.16">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 1:16</a>.</i>. “In truth;” Rebbi Eleazar said that every place where they stated “in truth,” it is practice going back to Moses on Mount Sinai.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This principle is also accepted in the Babli, <i>Baba</i> <i>Meẓia</i>‘ 60a.</i> And we stated about this, for example a <i>qab</i> or half a <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1">Halakhah 1, Notes 22–24</a></i>.Hence, two <i>qab</i> go by a quarter. Here you say, two <i>qab</i> by a quarter, and there you say, two <i>qab</i> by one in 24. Rebbi Zeïra and Rebbi Abun in the name of Rav Huna; one says, nine <i>qab</i>, the other says, eight <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">If one only needs 2 <i>qab</i>, ⅓ <i>seah</i>, for seeding, then the contamination is by a quarter, as expressed in the Tosephta, not for 1 <i>seah</i> but for 1⅓ to 1½ <i>seah</i> (8 to 9 <i>qab</i>). For smaller quantities, one goes by the <sup>1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> rule; the two statements are complementary, not contradictory.</i>.
Do nine and ten join together<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">It was stated that seeds which need 8 or 9 <i>qab</i> are contaminated by a quarter of seeds which need two <i>qab</i>. Does this rule extend to seeds that need 10 <i>qab</i>? The answer is, since 3 <i>qab</i> go by the quarter rule, and 9 <i>qab</i> go by the same rule, the difference between 9 and 10, a mere 10% from above, is not large enough to change the rules. Hence, if seeds which need 9 or 10 <i>qab</i> contaminate seeds which need one <i>seah</i>, they are measured by <sup>9</sup>/<sub>24</sub> = ⅜ <i>qab</i> per <i>seah</i> of the main seed. In the opinion of R. Simson, if half of the contamination is by seeds which need 9 <i>qab</i>, this half is measured by <sup>9</sup>/<sub>24</sub>, the other half by <sup>10</sup>/<sub>24</sub>. (Maimonides does not mention these rules in his Code.)</i>? Come and see, nine and three come together, so much more nine and ten!
Rebbi Abin and Rebbi Ḥanina both say that a split <i>seah</i> is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between the Sages and Rebbi Simeon is whether wheat grain is the standard by which everything is measured or whether every kind has its own standard. The examples given all go by the rule of R. Simeon.</i>. A quarter <i>seah</i> may prohibit three <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter <i>seah</i> is half of three <i>qab</i>, not 1 in 24. The explanation is that if some plants need 6 <i>seah</i> of seeds for a <i>bet seah</i>, then they contaminate in 6÷24 = .25 <i>seah</i>, even if the main seed is one that needs only half a <i>seah</i> to cover a bet <i>seah</i>. All other examples follow the same pattern and are really superfluous.</i>, and a quarter of three <i>qab</i> may prohibit a <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Here the contaminating seed needs 18 <i>qab</i> = 3 <i>seah</i> per <i>bet seah</i>.</i>. A quarter <i>seah</i> may prohibit nine <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The contaminating seed needs 4 <i>seah</i> per bet <i>seah</i>, the contaminated seed is standard.</i>, and a quarter of nine <i>qab</i> may prohibit a <i>seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The contaminating seed needs 6 <i>seah</i> per <i>bet seah</i>.</i>. Three quarters of flax seed prohibit a <i>seah</i>. How is this? On a plot on which one sows one quarter of wheat, one sows three quarters of flax seed.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If his field was sown with wheat and he changes his mind to sow it with barley, he should wait until it gets wormy<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The seeds, or the grain fallen to the ground, are seen to attract worms. If the grains are from the harvest in the dry season, one has to wait longer. Maimonides (<i>Kilaim</i> 2:13) applies the Mishnah only to freshly sown grain, but the mention of dry fields in the Halakhah does not seem to support that position.</i>, then<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abraham ben David (Maimonides, <i>Kilaim</i> 2:13) interprets ויופך to mean “or he turns it over.” His argument seems to be that waiting until the worms eat the grain is not mentioned in the later parts of the Mishnah.</i> he turns it over<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abraham ben David (Maimonides, <i>Kilaim</i> 2:13) interprets ויופך to mean “or he turns it over.” His argument seems to be that waiting until the worms eat the grain is not mentioned in the later parts of the Mishnah.</i> and after that he may sow. If it is sprouting, he should not say, I am going to sow and then turn it over, but he turns it over and then he sows. How deep does he have to plough? Like furrows for the rainy season<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Coarse furrows made so that the autumn rains should penetrate the soil, not the second ploughing of straight and parallel furrows for sowing.</i>. Abba Shaul says, so that there should not remain a quarter for a <i>bet seah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">He may leave <sup>1</sup>/<sub>24</sub> of the surface area unploughed, following the rule established in the first two Mishnaiot.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 1:16" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).1.16">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 1:16</a>.</i> How long can it remain sprouting? That it should remain in the ground three days in a muddy place, but in a dry place it needs more [time].” We have stated on that: Part of the day is like an entire day<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, that is an accepted principle in most cases, not only in this particular case.</i> in a muddy place, and if it started growing but he brought his animals and they browsed it clean<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical Hebrew כרסם, Arabic قرصم, קרצם.</i> it is permitted.
What is the reason of the rabbis? Because he intended to plough, even if it is not covering<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In modern Hebrew, one would translate “not continuous.” However, the image here is that of a floor tiling that does not cover the entire floor (a stone-covered floor is רצפה, “tiled.”)</i>. It was stated: Abba Shaul said, “one does not oblige him to plough finely, only like the furrows of the rainy season. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, it used to be called ‘horse tail,’ so that the end of dust of any one furrow touch that of the next one.”<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 1:17" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).1.17">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i>1:17</a>. The position of Abba Shaul here is that of the anonymous Tanna there. Rabban Gamliel notes that one has to plough only half the ground; the other half then is covered by the earth thrown up in ploughing.</i> What is the reason of Abba Shaul? Since he started, it is enough up to a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter <i>qab</i> of contamination is acceptable by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>.</i>. Does Abba Shaul hold with Rebbi Yose? He is with the rabbis, for the rabbis said, because he intended to plough, even if it is not covering. Abba Shaul says, because he started intentionally to plough, it is enough up to a quarter. Rebbi Yose said, he must finish that entire quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of סומך “to lean on” was studied by J. N. Epstein, ,מבוא לנוסח המשנה 2nd ed. (Jerusalem/Tel Aviv 1964) p. 446.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> [If the field was] sown and he decided to plant, he should not say, I shall plant and then I shall turn over, but he shall turn over and then plant. [If it was] planted and he decided to sow, he should not say, I shall sow and then I shall uproot, but he should uproot and then sow. If he wishes, he cuts down to less than a hand-breadth, plants, and then takes out the roots.<br>If his field was sown with hemp or arum<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Peak</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.4.1">Mishnah 6:9</a>.</i>, one should not sow on it because they produce all of three years<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The next paragraph (next Mishnah in the Mishnah part of the Yerushalmi) will explain that, in general, aftergrowth may be disregarded. But if a kind is known to produce commercially valuable aftergrowth, even if no new seeds were provided, then this is to be considered as a continued crop and any other kind on that field is <i>kilaim</i>. A field of hemp cannot be used for another crop the next year unless all hemp roots are taken out.</i>.<br>One is not required to weed grain in which there appeared aftergrowth of indigo, or the threshing floor on which many kinds are appearing<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">They never were sown; this is growth from the grains which remained from the different kinds that were threshed at that floor.</i>, or fenugreek in which grasses appeared<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Fenugreek is an expensive spice and medicinal plant with tiny seeds which is cultivated only on plots the size of a garden bed. Since the plants are of such high value, weeding has to be done with the utmost care.</i>. But if he weeded or cut, one says to him, uproot everything except one kind.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: One may put sheaves next<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of סומך “to lean on” was studied by J. N. Epstein, ,מבוא לנוסח המשנה 2nd ed. (Jerusalem/Tel Aviv 1964) p. 446.</i> to dried-up vines. Rebbi Yose said, that means that one may not plant a dry vine next to dry standing grain<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since only sheaves, bundles of cut grain, may lean on dry vines, it follows that dry, standing grain must be separated even from dried-up vines.</i>. May one sow next to dry vines? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.1">Mishnah 7:2</a>.</i>:“A dried-up vine is forbidden but does not sanctify<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a> declares that grain growing in a vineyard sanctifies both the grain and the vines. This means that it acquires a status similar to sacrifices; all profane use of it is forbidden. But since it is not a sacrifice, it has to be burned. In tractate <i>Kilaim</i>, “sanctified” means “it must be destroyed by burning.” <i>Kilaim</i> not involving vines are sinful but not sanctified.</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar said, this is the opinion of Rebbi Meïr, since Rebbi Meïr said<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.1">Mishnah 7:2</a>.</i>, even cotton<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Determination of Maimonides and R. Simson, see <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.1">Mishnah 7:2</a>. Cotton is not food but cottonseed is.</i> is forbidden but does not sanctify.
That<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The last sentence of the Mishnah, that one may cut down, plant, and only then uproot.</i> means that one may sow next to dry vines and “when they produce leaves again, the past is permitted and the future prohibited.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 1:18" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).1.18">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 1:18</a>. There is added: “If he reduced the stumps to less than a hand-breadth in height, but when he returned he found new vine leaves over the growing grain, the past is permitted but the future forbidden. He removes any kind he chooses and keeps the other kind.”</i>” Like that of Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the Fifth generation, student of R. Simeon ben Ioḥai, from Kefar Akko.</i> who cut down his vines and told his sharecropper, go and sow! After he had sown, they<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The stumps of the vines.</i> started to grow leaves. He said to him, go and cut<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Harvest the unripe grain for fodder.</i>! After he had cut, they rotted; he said to him, go and sow close up<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Rotting vine stumps are certain not to produce fruit; they can be disregarded.</i>. Rebbi Zeīra asked: Is that only for Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah, for he said it in full knowledge; what about common people who do not have full knowledge? He returned and said, even Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah said it only because of the bad appearance<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the vine stumps do not produce blossoms, the prohibition is purely rabbinical and not universally accepted.</i>.
It is a condition of Joshua<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Ten categories of public use of private property are attributed to Biblical Joshua, attached as liens to the original distribution of land. The detailed discussion is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 80b-82a" href="/Bava_Kamma.80b-82a">Babli <i>Baba Qama</i> 80b–82a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.1.2-7">Yerushalmi <i>Baba Batra</i>5:1</a> (fol. 15a). Since tractate <i>Neziqin</i> in the Yerushalmi was edited differently from the rest of the Yerushalmi, the text in Yerushalmi <i>Baba Batra</i> is similar to but not identical with the text here.</i> that one may collect grasses everywhere except in a field of fenugreek. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman: The Mishnah deals with him who sowed for sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">He wants to sell fenugreek in retail bundles. The buyer can take the seeds as spices anytime he wants and in the meantime store the plants so they will not be lost.</i>. But did we not state: “One is not required to weed … fenugreek in which grasses appeared?<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If he grows fenugreek for sheaves, he can use the grasses to bind the “sheaves” that in reality are thin bundles. The grasses represent a value for him, he wants them there, and the entire crop should be forbidden because of <i>kilaim</i>! How can the Mishnah exempt fenugreek from an obligation of weeding?</i>” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in his own name: “When it grew for seeds<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the grasses are really weeds; they have to be removed before the fenugreek seeds can be taken from the stems.</i>.” Rebbi Yose said, did Joshua come only to help sinners<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Why should one be prohibited to take grasses which should have been weeded and whose existence is proof of a sin?</i>? If he committed a sin, it is forbidden as robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Since then the owner is deprived of his material to bind the bundles.</i>, if he did not commit a sin it is permitted in respect to robbery. Hence, there is no difference whether he sowed for seeds or for sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah applies to both cases.</i>. But did we not state: “One is not required to weed … fenugreek in which grasses appeared?” If he intends to use the grasses, one should oblige him to weed! Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Miasha: He would be happy if they were torn out and lying before him<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The entire previous argument is untenable; even if the farmer wants to sell fenugreek in small bundles, the loss because of weeds is much larger than the money or work saved in having the grasses ready as material for tying the bundles.</i>. Rebbi Yose did not say so, but in the end he approves that they should be torn out and lying before him<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he wants to sell fenugreek in bundles, he would first have to remove the grasses from the field before harvesting, in order to have a commercially usable crop. However, he wants to preserve them in order to use them to tie the bundles; he does not want to have them treated as ownerless.</i>. But then, if somebody found <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard, they should be forbidden as robbery since in the end he desires that they should be torn out and lie before him<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">There will be some use for weeds, probably as animal feed, even though without the religious obligation of <i>kilaim</i> the vineyard must be weeded to ensure the growth of the grapes.</i>! There<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The grape harvest is transported in vessels; fenugreek bundles are tied on the “field,” i. e., the garden plot.</i>, he has the means to bring it in, here he does not have the means to bring it in. Rav Naḥman said that Rebbi Mana asked: Think about it, if he had another field of fenugreek close by, then he has the means to bring it in<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The grasses growing on one plot could also be used for the other.</i>! The words of the rabbis disagree, since Rebbi Zeriqan said in the name of the House of Yannai: All aftergrowth is forbidden except for that which appears<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Vocalization and translation follows the parallel in <i>Baba Batra</i>, עולין instead of העלין “the leaves.” There, the three cases are explained. The farmer does not care what grows on a fallow field in crop rotation since everything will be ploughed under. If a field is being prepared for ploughing, aftergrowth will be removed as a matter of priority. In a vineyard, the owner will take out everything that could make his vineyard forbidden.</i> on a fallow field, a field ready to be ploughed under, and a vineyard. He should have added, except for a field of fenugreek sown for the production of seeds! Rebbi Yose said, the words of Rebbi Joḥanan support me. It happened that somebody bought a garden plot of vegetables from a Gentile. He came and asked Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">What to do with the various garden vegetables growing together on the plot in violation of the laws of <i>kilaim</i>. R. Joḥanan told him that only the future growth is forbidden. Any possible profit from plants growing as <i>kilaim</i> is forbidden, but in this case, the buyer could sell the undesirable produce for the full market value.</i>, who said to him, go, harvest and sell at the going rate. Rebbi Abbahu objected before Rebbi Joḥanan: Did we not state: “or the threshing place on which many kinds are appearing?<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The objection is not really from this sentence but from the following, that if one starts to remove some of the growth, everything has to be treated as <i>kilaim</i> except for one (main) crop. How could R. Joḥanan declare all kinds of produce equally acceptable?</i>” He said to him, that place proves that he does not want them to exist. Could he not have answered, the one is like robbery, the other is permitted with respect to robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Yose’s argument, that R. Joḥanan would support his position. If taking grasses from the field of fenugreek were not a case of robbery, the situation here would be totally different since the garden plot certainly is protected by the laws of robbery against taking from it (not grasses, but valuable produce!)</i>? How can you object based on a scenario where it is forbidden as robbery to a decision in a case which is permitted with respect to robbery? It is as Rebbi Immi said, they were rich in answers<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Yose is invalid; if there are several possible explanations, one is never required to give an exhaustive list of all possibilities; one satisfactory answer is enough.</i>. Or it is as Rebbi Nasa said, like a person who has two reasons but he answers only with one of them.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If someone wants to make his field into separate garden beds<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Borrowed from Aramaic מישרא, parallel Arabic مَشَارَة “plant life”. According to Maimonides in his Code (<i>Kilaim</i> 3:8), משר applies only to a rectangular garden bed that is not square. A square garden bed is called ערוגה if it is isolated and קרחת if it is part of a field. The latter assertion is difficult to accept, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.7.1">Note 150</a>. One might accept that קרחת applies to congruent rectangular patches but משר to rectangular patches of differing sizes. This seems to be the position of R. Abraham ben David of Posquières.</i> of different kinds, the House of Shammai say, three furrows of the ploughshare, and the House of Hillel say, the width of a Sharon yoke. Both criteria are close to one another<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides, the criterion is that of the minimal distance between beds. According to R. Isaac Simponti, the criterion is that of the minimal size of a garden bed, with only a minimal but orderly separation. The Halakhah can be interpreted both ways; this is the position of R. Simson.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Eleazar, in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya the Elder: He makes it two by two cubits, but decreases continuously to an arbitrarily small distance<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The total area must be four square cubits, but the division into strips is left to the farmer. This interpretation seems required by the question of R. Eleazar in the next paragraph.<br>R. Simson thinks that somewhere there must be an empty space of two by two cubits, otherwise the border strips can be as narrow as one desires. Maimonides in his Commentary and <i>Hilkhot Kilaim</i> 4:6 explains: “He makes it two by two cubits, but the border can be minimal;” i. e., if the field is subdivided into garden beds, inside between any two beds must be 4 square cubits of fallow land. At the border of the entire field, a border strip must be left empty. That strip has no minimal size (but presumably it must be wide enough to allow the farmer to walk there on his own property.) This interpretation takes מצר as a noun, “border strip,” a word otherwise documented only in the Babli. The translation and R. Simson take the word as a verb, “to narrow,” a usage that is required later in the statement of Rebbi. In addition, the phrase והולך עד “doing something continuously,” is only used in conjunction with transitive verbs.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, that is, if it is subdivided into garden beds, but if all is one bed, it does not apply. This parallels what Rebbi Yannai said: I may sow my field<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The standard field understood in all these Mishnaiot, a <i>bet seah</i> of 50 by 50 cubits. Rebbi Yannay splits the field into two strips, each of length 50 and width 25-ε cubits (ε being a small positive quantity) and reserving two cubits in length for each crop and its border.</i> with fifty kinds, twenty-five here and twenty-five there; this is in two rows. If that is so, would it be permitted even between himself and his neighbor<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">This is the case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.4.1">Mishnah 6</a>.</i>? Or in a dry spot<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">This was discussed in Chapter 1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9">Halakhah 9 (Note 203</a>), as a matter of dispute between Rebbi Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>? Then it parallels what Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said in the name of Ḥizqiah: A vertex from a dry spot is permitted! Rebbi said, he starts with an area of <i>bet rova‘</i> and narrows it down to three furrows of the ploughshare. Does Rebbi not accept that between grains one needs a <i>bet rova‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Stated in Rebbi’s own <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.7.1">Mishnah 2:9</a>; it is extremely rare to find Rebbi accepting into the Mishnah anonymous opinions conflicting with his own basic positions.</i>? That is for a square field<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Of a minimum size of 2500 square cubits.</i>, here for a garden bed. Do the rabbis not recognize garden beds? Rebbi Yudan said, the minimal distance for Rebbi is the maximal distance for the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">For Rebbi, the width of three furrows is the lower limit of what is acceptable; for the rabbis, the width of three furrows is the upper limit of what is required. So everybody agrees that the separation by three furrows is acceptable under all circumstances.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar asked: What are the rules for a small field<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since for a standard field (50 by 50 cubits), the total area of the strips between the garden beds must be four square cubits, what is the required minimum for a field that does not contain the required 2500 square cubits?</i>? Let us hear from the following: “<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Parallel statements appear in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 2:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.4">2:4</a>, about the border strips, or furrows, that are required between two fields, either in the situation of the preceding or the present Mishnah. For fields longer than 50 cubits, they do not require that the separation of the fields, by empty strip or by planted flax, be longer than 50 cubits. (Probably, this means that in designating the border by one row of flax, one may not use a furrow longer than 50 cubits, in order to avoid commercially usable flax that would be <i>kilaim</i> and make the field forbidden.) The statement about a small field is not in the Tosephta concerned only with fields having at least the standard size.<br>Abba Yose bar Joḥanan (Hanan) from Yanoaḥ in Upper Galilee, a Tanna of the fifth generation, was a student of R. Meïr and Abba Cohen Bar Dalaiah.</i> Rebbi Eleazar ben Simeon: Abba Yose ben Joḥanan from Yanoaḥ said for a large field 50 cubits, for a small field along most of it.” Since those who specified a measure for a large field did not do so for a small one, the Sages who did not specify a measure for a large field certainly would not specify a measure for a small field.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A vertex of wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">See <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.5">Chapter 1, Note 202</a>. “vertex” is the interpretation of Maimonides, who translates תורי זהב (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 1:11" href="/Song_of_Songs.1.11"><i>Cant</i>. 1:11</a>) not by “golden chains” but “golden triangular earrings,” and ראש תור by vertex of a triangle.” R. Simson and R. Isaac Simponti explain that ראש תור really is a Hebrew/Aramaic hybrid and means “ox head”, “trapezoid” as schematical approximation to the shape of an ox head. In the technical sense, ראש תור is applied to any trapezoidal or pentagonal field in which the furrows are not parallel, in particular if the last furrow in a field is not parallel to the other furrows and certainly not to the furrows in the adjacent field. The two explanations are probably identical; usually ראש תור is created if the outermost furrow of a field is not straight but approximately forms the legs of an obtuse triangle, with its vertex touching the other field. (In <i>Targum Yerushalmi</i> to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 39:10" href="/Exodus.39.10"><i>Ex</i>. 39:10</a>, a direction is called טריגון τρίγωνον “triangle,” meaning an arrowhead pointing in a direction. The identification of directions with triangular features is traditional.)</i> coming close<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">On the basis of both the usage of the Babli and Modern Hebrew, it would seem that one has to translate נכנס by: “enters into.” This cannot be the meaning of the expression here because, at the end of the first paragraph of the Halakhah, a furrow entering into another field is characterized by מובלעת “absorbed into.” Hence, נכנס must mean: “coming closer than permitted by the separation rules but not actually entering the row of growing barley.”</i> to a field of barley is permitted since it visibly is the end of its field. If someone’s field was sown with wheat and that of his neighbor with another kind, he may have of that kind adjacent to it<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">He may sow in his own field of the kind his neighbor grows and not separate it from his neighbor’s crop by the distance required by the Mishnaiot of this Chapter but only by a minimal strip that would designate the border between two fields of separate owners.</i>. If both his and his neighbor’s fields were growing wheat, it is permitted that he have one row of flax adjacent to his, but no other kind<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of having an empty strip as boundary marker, he may plant one row of flax to indicate the border. Since one row is much too little to serve as commercial crop, nobody will think that he is raising <i>kilaim</i>; everybody will know that this is a boundary marker.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, either flax or any other kind<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Probably, this applies only to kinds routinely sown on fields, not garden beds, since for the latter one row may yield worthwhile produce.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, even in the middle of his field he may test a row for flax<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">While the grain is growing, he may test in a short row whether flax will grow there. Since everybody knows that such a small quantity can only be a test, not production for use, it never will look like <i>kilaim</i>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Ḥizqiah: A vertex from a dry spot is permitted! Rebbi Joḥanan said, they stated only: “A vertex of wheat<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning the case of a well defined field, not an irregularly shaped plot resulting from sowing near a dry spot. (This paragraph may be used to support the interpretation by R. Simson and R. Isaac Simponti of ראש תור).</i>;” hence, a vertex that comes from a dry spot is forbidden.
Ḥizqiah said, if somebody has slightly more than a <i>bet rova‘</i>, then in that excess he may sow anything he likes<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Assuming one has a small strip along which the kinds can be sown in linear order, each kind being separated from the other. The discussion here follows the previous disagreement between Ḥizqiah and R. Joḥanan.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he may sow in it only one kind. Rebbi Zeïra said, Rebbi Joḥanan agrees that if he had a <i>bet rova‘</i> and a half, he may sow half a <i>bet rova‘</i> from either side and the last half <i>bet rova‘</i> is valid for both sides<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The middle half is enough of a separation; one does need a full <i>bet rova‘</i> of separation only for a standard field.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Joḥanan: “<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">. A similar text is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 2:1</a>.</i> If he had fields, one sown with wheat and the other sown with barley, he may bring straw<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">All commentators replace תבן “straw” by תלם “a furrow.” But that substitution is not supported by any manuscript. It is induced by misreading of the Tosephta, where one reads: “He ploughs and brings wheat into the barley field, or barley into the wheat field; this is permitted since the wheat looks like part of the wheat field, the barley like part of the barley field.” Similarly, the statements of R. Eliezer ben Jacob are close to the quote here but not identical. “Ploughing” here certainly means ploughing at least two furrows; so the substitution is not supported.</i> in between and sow one side<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Of the straw-covered middle strip. One certainly cannot permit sowing the two slopes of one and the same furrow with two different kinds; that would constitute <i>kilaim</i> according to all definitions.</i> with wheat and the other with barley because the wheat looks like the end of the wheat field, and the barley like the end of the barley field.” What does Rebbi Joḥanan do with that? He explains it<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> and the corresponding Tosephta follow R. Eliezer ben Jacob; he follows the Sages of the Mishnah.</i> following Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob, for Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob said<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:2-3" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.2-3">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 2:2–3</a>, language close to the quote here.</i>: “Even if wheat after wheat comes close to barley<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">If the second field is not triangular in shape but trapezoidal (or, maybe, even rectangular) there still is no <i>kilaim</i> as long as the visual impression of the two fields is one of separation.</i>, barley after barley comes close to wheat, it is permitted.” For wheat after wheat only comes close to barley (wheat), barley after barley comes close to (barley) [wheat]<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">This now is the explanation of the interpretation of R. Joḥanan. The words in parentheses are in the text and do not make sense, even though they appear in both manuscripts. The word in brackets is inserted as conjecture. The main point is the difference in meaning between “coming close to” and “being absorbed in.” The way Ḥizqiah lets one sow a narrow strip would be classified as one kind being absorbed in the other, at least if the excess area forms a thin strip all around the <i>bet rova‘</i> square.</i>, but if only one wheat stalk is absorbed into the barley, or one stalk of barley is absorbed into the wheat, then that is forbidden.
What are we dealing with<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence in the Mishnah, the statement of Rebbi Yose. If somebody plants with the intention of not harvesting, but only to test the soil, why should the rabbis disagree (on condition that the test plot be clearly delineated)? The answer is that the rabbis are afraid that the neighbors either will suspect the farmer of sowing <i>kilaim</i>, or they might conclude from his action that one may sow flax in a grain field even as a commercial crop.</i>? If he wants only to test, even in the middle of his field it should be permitted. If he does not intend only to test, why just linseed, and not any other kinds? We deal with the case he wants to test. Rebbi Yose does not worry about bad appearances<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, he will never allow testing for another kind of food crop in a field growing food, only an industrial crop.</i>; the rabbis worry about bad appearances.
Rebbi Samuel said in the name of Rebbi Zeïra, it is Rebbi Simeon’s, for Rebbi Simeon said, nobody can sanctify property that is not his own<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon’s (and R. Yose’s) statement is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.3.1">Mishnah 7:4</a>: “Somebody who draws his vine over another person’s grain, sanctifies the latter and has to pay for it. R. Simeon and R. Yose say, nobody can sanctify property that is not his own.” Similarly, any action of a farmer in his field cannot implicate his neighbor in a sin. Hence, the Mishnah itself must be R. Simeon’s: if no crop of the active farmer A has any influence on that of the passive farmer B, then farmer A’s crop itself cannot be forbidden. R. Simeon’s statement is an explanation of the Mishnah, not a disagreement. But the anonymous rabbis of 7:4 will not agree.</i>. Rav said, nobody can jail property that is not his own<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">I. e., make it forbidden to be used. Here and in the following, “to jail” means to have it enclosed from all sides by other produce.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, Rav comes out like Rebbi Simeon; just as Rebbi Simeon said, nobody can sanctify property that is not his own; so Rav said, nobody can jail property that is not his own. But it cannot be explained following the Sages.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not sow mustard or saffron close<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Without any bare strip between one crop and the other. As R. Simson notes, the reason for this Mishnah is not explained. The explanation of Maimonides in his Mishnah Commentary is contradicted by the Halakhah. Probably the correct explanation is given by Maimonides in his Code, <i>Kilaim</i> 2:18, that a crop of mustard or saffron surrounding a field of fifty by fifty (i. e., from a single furrow 200 cubits in length) is enough for a domestic crop (a commercial crop of mustard seed is defined in the next Mishnah as needing three <i>bate rova</i>‘).<br>The translation of חריע as saffron (<i>crocus sativus</i>) follows Targum <i>Cant</i>.,<i>Arukh</i>(<i>s</i>.<i>v</i>. חריע) and Rashi. The Gaonic Commentary to <i>Ṭahorot</i> and Maimonides define it as safflower (<i>carthamus tinctorius</i>), a red dye. In the Halakhah, חריע is defined as “yellow dye.”</i> to a grain field, but one may sow mustard or saffron close to a vegetable field. One does sow close to a fallow field, a ploughed<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">A field on which the stubs of the previous harvest have been ploughed under but which has not been sown with a new crop.</i> field, a closure, a foot path, a fence ten hand-breadths high, a ditch ten<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">All measurements given in the masculine are hand-breadths.</i> deep and four wide, a tree giving shade on the ground<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The place under the tree free of superficial roots is not the tree’s domain; hence, it may be used. This excludes vines which go under specially stringent rules because they cause <i>kilaim</i> to become sanctified and forbidden. For any other crop, it is only necessary that fields or garden beds be clearly distinguished.</i>, and a rock ten high and four wide.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What is חריע? Yellow dye.
So is the Mishnah: “One does not surround,” hence one may sow close up<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">For the meaning of סומך “to sow close to another crop”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.3.2">Note 57</a>. The language of the Mishnah is corrected by a text close to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 2:5</a>, quoted in the following. On the basis of that correction, J. N. Epstein, and S. Lieberman who follows him, want to explain מקיף by “coming really close, closer than leaning to.” However, since they have to admit that in the statement of Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel מקיף means “surround,” it is irrational to accept any other meaning for the other parts of the Tosephta.</i>. Our Mishnah is Rebbi Jehudah’s, as it was stated: “One may surround by mustard or saffron only bulbous vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">So defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Terumot (Lieberman) 9:5" href="/Tosefta_Terumot_(Lieberman).9.5">Tosephta <i>Terumot</i>9:5</a>. Etymologically, חסית or חוסית is related to Syriac חסוסא “bulbous.”</i>, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah said, any field one may surround by mustard or saffron except a grain field. Rebbi Simeon said, any field one may surround by mustard or saffron<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is not in the Tosephta.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, one may surround small vegetable beds by mustard or saffron.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text, but without the condition, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:4" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.4">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 2:4</a> in the name of R. Yose.</i>: “One may sow a row of mustard or saffron in the middle of one’s field on condition to make the row long, a length of 10.5 cubits the entire width.” Rebbi Eleazar said, that applies only to a small field, not to a large one. What is large and what is small? If there is between the two ends a <i>bet rova‘</i>, that is large, otherwise it is small. Does this not disagree with Rebbi Joḥanan, for Rebbi Joḥanan said that a vertex that comes from a dry spot is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, in the middle of a field it certainly will be forbidden.</i>? There it is for grain, here for vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Whose plants are much larger than the grasses grown for grain.</i>. Even though Rebbi Joḥanan disagrees for grain, he will agree here with respect to vegetables.
On dry soil<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Hard soil which cannot be ploughed to the depth required for grain and vegetables. Only a few adapted species grow there. Since the ground is different from the surrounding agricultural soil, a different crop is not <i>kilaim</i> but visibly forms another field.</i> one may sow any kind he desires. Rebbi Joḥanan said, only if he uses three of six [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">To make the other crop stand out, a minimal width of the cultivated area is required.</i>. Three that are close to a fence are like dry land<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The three hand-breadths close to a fence are not usable in a regular way. If they can be added to another three hand-breadths of irregular soil, the conditions for their use for other crops are given.</i>. If there were three inside six and one outside six<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Adjacent to the dry spot but outside it. Then the special crop introduced on the dry soil does not have to stop exactly at the border of the dry spot but can exceed it, up to a hand-breadth, presumably to give a more regular shape to the plot sown or planted with the dry crop.</i>, since the former ones were saved, that one also is saved. If only two were inside six and one outside six<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">If the dry soil itself is not used for at least 50%, it cannot be used at all for crops differing from the surrounding one. Hence, even good adjacent soil cannot be used to make up for the deficiency on the dry soil.</i>, since the first ones were not saved, neither is that one. If there was one row dry, one row fence, and another row, one jails the row<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">If there was a narrow strip of good soil between the less usable strip along the fence and the less usable strip of dry soil, the good soil is caught in between (“jailed”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:5:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.5.5">Note 120</a>) and cannot be sown with the regular crop but must be used for the same crop planted on the dry strip and along the fence. In addition, the three hand-breadths each along the fence and of dry soil add up to the required minimum of six hand-breadths for separate use, even though they are separated by three hand-breadths of good soil. The good soil is added to the strip along the fence.</i> by the fence on fields<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The language is very difficult. Ms. Rome is completely different: גגו של גדר כשרות “the roof of a fence is permitted,” meaning that on the soil forming the top of a stone fence one may plant anything, irrespective of what is growing below. In the Leyden version of the Yerushalmi, the rule is restricted to fields, i. e., to plots that measure at least 50 by 50 cubits.</i>. Two fields<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">The fields are terraced, the upper one bounded at the upside by a ditch, either an irrigation ditch or one to catch rain water, the lower field is bounded below by a stone fence. In that case, both the upper and the lower borders have the status of fences.</i>, one above the other, the upper one is close to a ditch, the lower one close to a fence.
The House of Yannai said, on the fence one may sow all kinds of mixtures<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">Root רבך “to mix” (also in Accadic and Arabic.) The statement seems to mean that on top of a stone fence one may sow anything he wants, irrespective of the crops on either side of the fence.</i>. A fence implies leniencies and stringencies. How is this? Less than a <i>bet rova‘</i> shaven<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Not empty, but shaven of the surrounding crop. The next Mishnaiot will elaborate this notion. Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.7.1">Note 150</a>.</i> in a grain field may not be sown<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Sown with a kind different from the one growing around it.</i>; if surrounded with a fence it may be sown. A <i>bet rova‘</i> in open country may be sown with two kinds<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">If the clear separation rules established earlier are followed. If the <i>bet rova‘</i> is enclosed, it does not qualify as a field and must follow the rules of משר.</i>; if surrounded with a fence this is forbidden.
Does a fence save grains<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The question as it stands makes no sense since the Mishnah states clearly that a fence absolutely separates crops. One has to say that one does not speak of a (wooden) fence but of a stone wall and the question is whether the stone wall loses its status if it is used as a field in itself. (R. Moses Margalit wants to emend the following גריד to גדר and take the next sentence as explaining the meaning of the present one. This is impossible since the text is also supported by the Rome ms. and, as R. Eliahu Fulda notes, dry land is only mentioned to separate vegetables, whose rules are not as strict as those for grains.) One has to take the text here parallel to the following one and interpret “fence” as “stone wall topped by soil on which produce is growing.”</i>? Does sown dry land save vegetables? Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Zeïra understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 2:5</a>. There it is added that the roots have to be 10 hand-breadths high, corresponding to the minimal size of a separating stone wall. Hence, living roots are not different from a stone wall on which some crop was planted.</i>: “One sows close to dried-up tree roots.” This mentions only dry ones; hence, if they are moist, it is forbidden. Rebbi Yose understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the current Mishnah. Here, “fence” (and also, “closure”) is used without qualification and includes stone walls on which produce is grown.</i>: “One does sow close to a fallow field, a ploughed field, a closure, a foot path, a fence ten hand-breadths high.” That implies that a sown fence saves grains! Even if you accept “fence ten hand-breadths high,” a sown fence does not have to save grains. There is a difference here because the fence is sown and no sown fence saves grains<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Since the stone wall carries produce, all separation rules for fields apply; there is no longer any wall in the legal sense once it became a field.</i>.
Rebbi Judan asked: Do dry land and length add together? How is that? If there was space ten and a half cubits long and six hand-breadths wide<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">On separate ground in a grain field one may sow a row of vegetables since, as was just stated, dry land only helps for vegetables.</i>, one does not have to have dry land<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">In order to permit the planting. But if dry land is added, one can sow grain close to it. Since the row of vegetables also takes up space, without the dry strip there would not be the required separation of at least three hand-breadths from the grain.</i>. If dry land was added on both sides, one may sow close on both sides.
It was stated: Rebbi Jonathan ben Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the fourth generation, student of R. Aqiba, and perhaps also of R. Ismael. He is mentioned only in Tosephta and <i>baraitot</i>. His statement here refers to the Mishnah, that one may plant different crops at the sides of a rock outcropping that is at least ten hand-breadths high (about a yard). He states that any side of the outcropping at least one yard long forms an absolute barrier for <i>kilaim</i>.</i> said, one may sow close to a rock ten hand-breadths long and four hand-breadths wide on both of its sides. If it was ten by ten hand-breadths, one may sow close to it on all four sides.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who wants to turn his field into patches<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic קַרָאח “terrain appropriate for being sown,” used here for equal patches all sown or planted with different kinds. Clearly, R. Meïr cannot require the patches all to be square and equal, since 24 is not a square number. Since a <i>seah</i> contains 24 <i>rova‘</i>, each plot will have just the minimal size that allows different seeds to be sown. Since the patches have to be rectangular and equal in shape, on a field of 50 by 50 cubits the sides of the rectangles are 50/4=12.5 by 50/6=8. 3̄ cubits (or 50/3=16.6̄ by 50/8=6.25 cubits.)</i> for all different kinds makes 24 patches per <i>bet seah</i>, each patch one <i>bet rova‘</i>, and sows in each one any kind that he desires. If there were one or two patches<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">A new statement. If an otherwise continuous field has two square excisions of a <i>bet rova‘</i> each, separated from one another, he may sow both of them with mustard because that is still less than a commercial crop. Three he may not sow because the yield would be enough for a commercial crop and would make the remainder <i>kilaim</i>.</i>, he may sow them with mustard; three he should not sow because that would look like a mustard field, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Mishnah up to here is R. Meïr’s.</i>. But the Sages say, nine patches are permitted, ten are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in the Halakhah. Maimonides, who insists that a קרחת must be square, takes as model of the 50 by 50 cubits field a five-by-five square checkerboard. All white fields in the first, third, and fifth rows are squares of a <i>bet rova</i>‘ each (squares of √2500/24 = 10.207 cubits edge length) while the black fields in these rows are rectangles of edge lengths 10.207 and 9.69 cubits. In rows 2 and 4, the black fields are squares of a <i>bet rova</i>‘, the white fields are rectangles of edge lengths 9.69 and 10.207 cubits. Together, they fill the large square, but only the nine fields selected first may be sown and all others must lie fallow.<br>According to Maimonides, the number 9 as maximum of patches is a mathematical fact. In the interpretation of R. Simson who admits rectangular patches, 10 patches would be possible but are forbidden. In a 4-by-6 checkerbord, a valid centrally symmetric configuration of 10 patches might contain (1,1) (1,4) (2,2) (3,1) (3,3) (4,2) (4,4) (5,3) (6,1) (6,4). The expression “ten are forbidden” supports R. Simson.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, even if its field is a <i>bet kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">1 <i>kor</i> = 30 <i>seah</i>. 1 <i>bet kor =</i>75’000 square cubits, larger than the <i>bet kor</i> appearing in cuneiform documents.</i>, he should not make in it more than one patch.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Yasa, in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: For the first part, Rebbi Meïr says, even jailed, even adjacent, but the rabbis say, on condition that they were neither jailed nor adjacent<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Two rectangular fields may meet one another at a vertex, since each one is ראש תור for the other. “Jailed” means here that a rectangular field forms ראש תור at all four of its vertices. Rebbi Meïr permits to sow rectangular fields, each of the minimum size of <i>bet rova‘</i>, even if they are adjacent, i. e., they are joined at an edge, and even if they are jailed at all corners, which means that he permits to use the entire field. The Sages do not disagree that a קרחת must have a minimum size of <i>bet rova‘</i>. If the field is divided into equal rectangles, one may imagine that the different rectangles are alternately black and white in checkerboard fashion. Since the rabbis do not permit adjacent rectangles to be sown, the first condition is that only rectangles of the same color are chosen. The second condition is that no chosen rectangle can be surrounded by 4 chosen rectangles. The opinion of Maimonides about the position of the rabbis was explained in the Mishnah. His opinion is difficult to accept, since he does not permit any two fields to touch at all, so the mention of “jailing” is totally redundant. It is therefore better to look for a partition of the 50 by 50 field into 24 equal parts. The rabbis cannot speak about division of the field into squares since their number would have to be either 16 (with 7 chosen squares) or 25 (where 12 chosen squares are possible.) Also, the squares in the second case would be smaller than a <i>bet rova‘</i>. If the Sages would follow the division of R. Meïr and cover the field by a 4-by-6 checkerboard, they could accomodate 10 chosen rectangles (fields 1,3,5 in the first row, 2,4,6 in the second, 1,5 in the third, 2,4 in the fourth.) Hence, it seems that the rabbis insist that the area for קרחת should be strictly larger than a <i>bet rova‘</i>, and on a 5-by-4 board one may select at most nine (e. g., fields 1,3,5 in row 1; 2,4 in row 2; 1,5 in row 3; 2,4 in row 4.) The area of each plot is then 125 sq. cubits. (Explanation of R. Eliahu Fulda.)</i>. How is that done? 3,2,1,2,l<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">This statement fails in two respects. First, the underlying checkerboard is 5-by-5, allowing only 100 sq. cubits per plot, and second, on such a checkerboard that has 13 white and 12 black fields, one may choose all white fields with the exception of the central one (#3 in row 3), for a total of 12 admissible fields. Hence, this sentence (choosing 3 plots in the first row, 2 in the second, 1 in the third, 2 in the fourth, 1 in the fifth) must be a later addition. Even if one chooses the central element in row three, one still could accomodate 10 plots (1,3,5 in the first row, 2,4 in the second, 3 in the third, 1,5,in the fourth, 2,4 in the fifth).<br>The argument is not part of R. Joḥanan’s statement since it is in Aramaic. R. I. J. Kanievski proposes to read תְּלַת תַּרְתֵּי וְחָדָא תַּרְתֵּיי וְחָדָא, eliminating two conjunctions and translating “three [times] two and one each.” The syntax is very unusual.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Anything that is inside the <i>bet rova‘</i> counts for the measurement of <i>bet rova‘</i><sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>bet rova‘</i> which must be left uncultivated between two fields. The separating space need not be fallow agricultural land; land which cannot be used for agriculture may be counted since all that is needed is a separation.</i>. The surroundings of a vine<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">The space around a vine that must be left free for tending it.
