Maimonides writes that they adopted this raw approximation since in the opinion of Aristotle, the straight and the curved are original opposites and it is impossible to express curved quantities precisely in terms of linear measures. (In the framework of Archimedean mathematics, the falsity of this assertion was shown only in the Seventeenth Century by John Wallis.) Since we tend to disregard fractions in Talmudic computations, it seems reasonable to take π = 3. We do not have access to the table of chords used by the Talmudists to compute the contribution of A4 unless we want to take Ptolemy’s table (Ptolemäus, Handbuch der Astronomie, transl. K. Manitius, ed. O. Neugebauer. Leipzig 1963, pp. 37–40). This means all our computations here are only an approximate reconstruction. If we take modern values for the trigonometric functions in step 4 the total area is 800 for h = 2.94. (For h = 3, the deviation of the modern value from Ptolemy’s is negligible, about 0.001, much less than the precision of our computation.) It is reasonable to assume that in talmudic approximation, this means h = 3. [Better values of π known from Antiquity, 3 ⅛ or 3 ⅐, yield smaller values of h which have no reasonable explanation.]According to R. Joḥanan, we have to add to the standing vines the ones that complete the minimal vines, i. e., the vines at positions (1,3), (1,7), (3,1), (3,9), (7,3), (7,7). This means that from the original 64×48, at each corner we excise a square of edge length 16. The remaining area is 3072 - 4×16×16 = 3072 - 1024 = 2048 = 2500 - 452 cubits.</i>. You might say, they are separated as single ones<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since the scheme of R. Zeïra does not work out to produce 10 vines on exactly 2500 square cubits, we have to conclude that the 10 vines are isolated in the field. Then, each vine has 250 square cubits around it, which means that it is situated in the center of a square of edge √250 = 15.81 cubits. This length is also the distance of two vines (or better, the distance of the centers of any two vines) from one another, and this is just below the 16 cubits that would diqualify the field from being a “vineyard”.</i>. You might say, they are closer together<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Than 15.811 cubits.</i>: we have stated only “10 per <i>bet seah</i>;” you could not state that.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 3:4" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).3.4">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 3:4</a>; the text is almost identical with the one given here, including the queer use of the<br>singular in the first two statements that make sense only if applied to at least three vines. The later statement, commented on by R. Yose, is from a source other than the Tosephta.</i> The trunk is aligned but the top is not aligned, that is a vineyard. The top is aligned but the trunk is not aligned, that is not a vineyard. If they were thin, but when they grow thick they will be aligned, that is a vineyard. How does one know that they are aligned? One brings a rope and stretches it.” Some Tannaïm state: “From within<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This Tanna requires that the centers of the trunks be aligned. That obviously is not directly measurable; hence, one has to measure from the outside and determine that the centers are within one hand-breadth of the streched rope. The second version, that one puts the rope along the outsides of the vines, is that of the Tosephta.</i>.” But some Tannaïm state: “From without.” Rebbi Jonah said, he who says “from within,” within a hand-breadth, but he who says “from without” requires that it touches. “If the outer row is not aligned in its trunks, one reserves for it the space to tend it<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vines in this case do not form a vineyard and do not belong to the vineyard, they are treated as single vines and have only six hand-breadths reserved for tending. The language of the Tosephta is: “If one [vine] deviates to the inside and one to the outside (i. e., the straight segments connecting the vines form a zig-zag line), that is not a vineyard.”</i> and one may sow the rest.” Rebbi Yose says, if one sows within six hand-breadths all is forbidden; outside of six hand-breadths single vines are permitted but a vineyard is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Since a vineyard needs four cubits for tending.</i>.
What means “a poor vineyard?<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The one mentioned in the Mishnah. It needs a large tending area since the imaginary vines also belong to the tending area.</i>” Rebbi Jonah said, it is poor in vines but rich in tending areas.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A vineyard that is planted less that four cubits apart<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This violates standard agricultural practice.</i>, Rebbi Simeon says that it is no vineyard. But the Sages say that it is a vineyard and one disregards the ones in the middle as if nonexistent<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">If every second vine is disregarded the remaining vines still are less than eight cubits apart and, hence, still form a vineyard. The argument of the Sages (made explicit in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 102b" href="/Bava_Batra.102b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 102b</a>) is that vines planted too close to one another will not bear the best or the most fruit; hence, the vintner might plant too many vines at the start and then, when it becomes clear which vines grow best, he will take out the rest and use as wood and replant the best ones as a regular vineyard. Rebbi Simeon is of the opinion that a vineyard is planted for its yield, so he waits until the vineyard is thinned before he imposes the restrictions of a vineyard. (In the Babli, R. Simeon is interpreted to mean that nobody plants a vineyard with the intention of thinning.) The Sages think that planting for a vineyard makes a vineyard from the start.<br>It is not quite clear what the minimum distance is; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.1">Chapter 4, Note 2</a>. The cubit of 52.6 cm would give 210.4 cm as minimal distance of two vines. R. A. H. Nach defines the hand-breadth as 8.5 cm, which implies that 4 cubits of five hand-breadths each are 170 cm. Modern viticulture works with distances of 135 to 150 cm; one is best off by adopting the minimal interpretation of cubits.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Simeon said to them: Those about which you say that one disregards the ones in the middle as if nonexistent, are the essence of the vineyard! Rebbi Ḥananiah<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥananiah and R. Mana disagree about the meaning of the last statement of R. Simeon. According to R. Ḥananiah, in the case of two rows of five vines each, if one disregards any vines numbered 2 and 4 in their rows, one retains two rows of three vines each forming a legal vineyard. But if one has two rows of 6 each, one eliminates either the even or the odd numbered vines; the ones eliminated would also form a vineyard and that is unacceptable in the opinion of R. Simeon. R. Mana has essentially the same argument, only he says that the disagreement concerns the first case, when one would have a vineyard in case one eliminates vines 2 and 4, but not if one eliminates 1, 3, 5. However, in the case of 6 or more vines, one always retains a valid vineyard (and the vines considered nonexistent do not give the possibility of sowing there since the distance from vine to remaining vines is less than 8 cubits.)</i> said, that applies in the case where there are six parallel six. But if there are five parallel five, everybody agrees that one disregards the ones in the middle as if nonexistent. Rebbi Mana said, that means if there are five parallel five. But if there are six parallel six, if you disregard the ones in the middle as if nonexistent, these are like those! What does that mean which we have stated: One disregards the ones in the middle as if nonexistent? Rebbi Huna said, that one is permitted to draw them<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">If the domain at the border of the vineyard is sown with other produce, the shoots of the vines destined to be taken out can be drawn on a trellis that goes sideways over the produce, since the vines will be eliminated before they bear fruit.</i> over his seeds. Rebbi Mana said, that supports what Rebbi Yose had said, that if one sows within six hand-breadths all is forbidden, outside of six hand-breadths single vines are permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In this interpretation, “single vines” are those planted too close and destined to be removed, even if initially planted in a vineyard pattern.</i> and a vineyard is forbidden
Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This discussion is referred to in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 102b" href="/Bava_Batra.102b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 102b</a>. A parallel to the text here is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:3:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.3.2-8">Yerushalmi <i>Nazir</i> 9:3,</a> fol. 57b. The text there is slightly different from the text here. Since the Rome ms. here supports the text from <i>Nazir</i>, and the last sentences here show that both times the paragraphs are a confluence from texts from both sources, it is appropriate to give the full text of the paragraph there:<br>תמן תנינן כרם שהוא נטוע על פחות מד̇ אמות ר׳ שמעון אומר אינו כרם. וחכמים אומרים כרם. ורואין את האמצעיות כאילו אינן. שמעון בר בא בשם ר׳ יוחנן כשם שהן חלוקין כאן כך הן חולקין בשכונת קברות. א״ר יונה ולא דמייא תמן יש עליהן שכונת קברות. ברם הכא אין עליה שכונת קברות. א״ר יוסי ולא דמייא תמן מרװחין ורצפן במחלוקת. רצופין וריװחן דברי הכל. ברם הכא מהו פליגין. בשבא ומצאן רצופין ר״ש אומר גל נפל עליהן ורצפן. ורבנן אמרי מרװחין היו ורצפן. “There, we have stated: ‘A vineyard which is planted less that four cubits apart, Rebbi Simeon says it is no vineyard. But the Sages say it is a vineyard and one disregards the ones in the middle as if nonexistent.’ Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Just as they disagree here, they disagree about a graveyard. Rebbi Jonah said, one cannot compare these. <i>There, they form a graveyard, here, they do not form a graveyard. Rebbi Yose said, one cannot compare these. There</i>, if they were spaced apart and one moved them closer together, that is the disagreement. If they were close and one moved them apart, everybody would agree. But here, what is their disagreement? When one found them close together. Rebbi Simeon said, a landslide fell on them and compressed them. But the Sages say, were they spaced apart and somebody brought them close together?”</i>: Just as they disagree here, they disagree about a graveyard<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.3.1">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i>9:3</a>: “If somebody stumbles upon a grave (on his property; he did not know that the grave was there), he removes it and its surroundings (the earth that absorbed the decomposed flesh of the deceased, for reburial.) If he found two, he takes them and their surroundings. If he found three and between any two graves is a distance from 4 to 8 cubits, space for the bier and its carriers, that is a graveyard (and cannot be disturbed) and one has to check another adjacent 20 cubits.” In a <i>baraita</i>, quoted only in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 102a" href="/Bava_Batra.102a">Babli <i>Bava Batra</i> 102a</a>, but presupposed in the Yerushalmi, there is a discussion about a group of several graves that are found closer than 4 cubits together. The Sages think that these indicate haphazard burials which do not constitute a graveyard, but R. Simeon holds that one disregards alternate graves and the rest form a graveyard that may not be disturbed. The Babli explains the obvious switch of opinions between R. Simeon and the Sages in that R. Simeon holds that nobody plants vines for thinning, but that in graveyard there sometimes are emergency burials when one cannot observe all necessary distances, whereas the Sages hold that one plants a vineyard with the idea of selecting the best vines, but in a graveyard one never violates common decency by piling grave upon grave.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, one cannot compare these. There, if graves were spaced apart and one moved them together, they would form a graveyard<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">While it is forbidden to do so, if it is done, then according to everybody the sanctity of a graveyard will not be impaired.</i>. When they were close together and one spaces them apart, that does not make a graveyard<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence, missing in <i>Nazir</i> and the Rome ms., is difficult to accept, since one always may make a new graveyard. What is meant is that the reburial does not retroactively make the burial place a graveyard. In a graveyard, the soil cannot be used for anything but burial. In a row of single graves, any soil that does not belong to the immediate surroundings of the graves may be used for any private purpose.</i>. But here, if they were spaced apart and one moved them closer, that is the disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">While this is not mentioned in the Mishnah, it is assumed that the Sages expect the new plantings to be thinned and R. Simeon does not.</i>. If they were close and one moved them apart, everybody would agree<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This will create a vineyard.</i>. But here<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">“Here” is the Mishnah in <i>Nazir</i>. This shows that the text in <i>Nazir</i> is the original one.</i>, what is their disagreement? When one found them close together. Rebbi Simeon said, a landslide fell on them and compressed them<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, they formed a graveyard whose sanctity cannot be lost.</i>. But the Sages say, were they spaced apart and somebody brought them close together<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">This is a most unlikely scenario and can be disregarded. The setting of the Mishnah is the Land of Israel where every grave is expected to be Jewish.</i>?

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A ditch that crosses a vineyard, ten hand-breadths deep and four wide. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob said, if it is open from one side of the vineyard to the opposite side it appears to be between two vineyards; one sows in it. Otherwise, it is like a vat<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">In Biblical Hebrew, גת denotes the winepress. In Mishnaic Hebrew, it may denote a winepress, the time of wine making, and the big vat into which the grape juice flows from the press, as well as any vat of similar size. Here, גת denotes the depression in which grapes are temporarily stored before being transported to the winepress.</i>. If a vat in the vineyard is ten deep and four wide, one sows in it, the words of Rebbi Eliezer; but the Sages forbid it<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As a matter of principle, a vertical distance of 10 hand-breadths creates a separate domain. If the domain is at least 4 hand-breadths wide, it can stand on its own (Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 7:2–5.) The Sages forbid planting if the separate low domain is “jailed” by the surrounding vines. In the case of an elevated mound, they consider it “jailed” only if branches of vines reach the top of the mound (R. David ben Zimra on Maimonides, <i>Kilaim</i> 7:20).</i>. On a watchman’s mound in a vineyard that is ten hand-breadths high and four wide one may sow; but if hair was pressing down<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If vine leaves touch the ground on the hill; cf. Mishnah and Halakhah <i>Peah</i> 2:3.</i>, it is forbidden.<br>For a vine planted in a depression or in a cleft one allows the area needed for tending it and one sows the rest. Rebbi Yose said, if there are less than four cubits one should not bring seeds there<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Any area that does not contain a square of four by four cubits is not considered a separate domain; it would remain part of the vineyard.</i>. A house in a vineyard may be sown<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A place surrounded by four walls and having a dirt floor. The walls separate between anything growing inside and the vines outside; no minimal distance has to be kept between vines and walls.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Unless it separates<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Reading מבריק as equivalent of Biblical and Babylonian מפרק “disassembles.”</i> it and the area needed to tend it<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages require either six hand-breadths or four cubits free of growth on either side of the ditch, to separate the vines and their working spaces from other growth.</i>, since we have stated: “The Sages agree with Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob in the case of a ditch that separates it and the area needed to tend it.” Hence, Rebbi Eliezer thinks that even if it does not separate it from the area needed to tend it. When do they disagree? If there are two rows on each side<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Then on each side, there is a vineyard. But if the ditch separates two single rows, none of which is a vineyard on its own, even the Sages will agree that the ditch deep 10 is enough to separate them.</i>. But if there was only one row on either side, once it reaches three vines there no longer is a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If the two (parallel) rows did form a vineyard, if afterwards the ditch is dug and separates six vines, three in each row, they no longer form a vineyard.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In a <i>baraita</i> stating the position of the Sages as opposed to R. Eliezer ben Jacob.</i>: “Less than that is forbidden.” Without it, would we not have said that ten is forbidden, so much more nine? But what does it mean that “less is forbidden?” Would we not say that it becomes as if closed and would be permitted? It should become as if closed and be permitted; was it not stated<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 4:11" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).4.11">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 4:11</a>. The passage already was quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.8.2">Halakhah 1:8</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.8.2">Chapter 1, Note 159</a>.</i>: “Roots crossing a symbolic fence and entering within four cubits of a vineyard are permitted if they are deeper than three hand-breadths?” There is a difference, because the air<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀήρ.</i> of the vineyard surrounds<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta deals with plants outside the vineyard whose roots penetrate the area reserved for tending the vineyard. Here we are dealing with a ditch inside the vineyard where the rules are stricter.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob said to them: Do you not agree that depth is like height<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Why do the Sages agree that the mound may be sown but the ditch may not? R. Eliezer ben Jacob will forbid sowing on the mound unless it extends from one side of the vineyard to the other. He also disagrees with R. Eliezer (ben Hyrkanos) because the vat does not divide the entire vineyard.</i>? Only that the air of the vineyard surrounds. It is reasonable to think that Rebbi Eliezer agrees with Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob but Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob does not agree with Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Eliezer agrees with Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob that depth is like height. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob does not agree with Rebbi Eliezer because the air of the vineyard surrounds.
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is necessary that there be an empty space of four hand-breadths. Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac said, that is, if it is round<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">As Maimonides explains, given the direction of the rows of vines, a round mound has the required size only at its diameter, but the lengths of the parallel chords go to zero and there is no separation at the circumference. For the same reason, a circular mound needs a dirt wall around the seeds.</i>. But if it is square, it does not need an empty space of four hand-breadths. Rebbi Ḥalaphta ben Shaul said<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The Rome ms. reads: “R. Ḥalaphta ben Shaul stated…” Since the author belongs to the generation between Tannaïm and Amoraïm, both readings are acceptable.</i>, it needs three hand-breadths of dirt on top. Rebbi Yose said, that is, if it is round. But if it is square, it does not need three hand-breadths of dirt on top.
The Mishnah deals with the case from three to four [hand-breadths]. With less than three it is closed; from three to four one has to complete, four one may sow immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The depression or cleft has to be at least three hand-breadths wide, otherwise one may easily step over it and it can be disregarded. If the width <i>w</i> is between three and four hand-breadths, one has to leave on either side an empty strip (4-<i>w</i>)/2 hand-breadths wide. If the cleft is four wide, one may sow in it up to the wall; the six hand-breadths used to tend the seeds are only needed as lengthwise separation between the vine and the seeds (R. Simson).</i>.
Rebbi Abin in the name of Samuel (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 42:22" href="/Isaiah.42.22"><i>Is</i>. 42:22</a>): “They were hidden in jail houses,” where one hides from <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence of the Mishnah, that a house protects from <i>kilaim</i>. The Talmud identifies the roots כלא “to restrain, keep back” (Arabic كلأ) and כלא (Arabic كلا) “double”. (R. Simson reads: “R. Samuel in the name of R. Abin”; but then it would have to read: R. Samuel R. Abbahu.)</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who plants vegetables in a vineyard or who lets them grow<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">If the edible vegetable grew from wind-borne seeds.</i> sanctifies 45 vines. When is this? In case they are planted four four or five five apart. If they were planted six six or seven seven apart, he sanctifies sixteen cubits in every direction, circular but not square<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The language of this Mishnah is so involved because of the special rule for a vineyard planted in spaces of five cubits. In general, vegetables can be planted in a vineyard only in a bald spot; according to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:1</a> that means at a distance of 16 cubits from the next vine. The rule of the Mishnah for vines planted more than 5 cubits apart is just that: In the interior of the circle of radius 16 and centered at the vegetable there may not be any vine.<br>The application of that principle to vines planted 4 apart leads to 45 sanctified vines. One assumes that the vegetable is sown closely around a vine. One takes the stern of that vine as center of a Cartesian coordinate system and takes the <i>x</i>-axis in the direction of the rows of vines, the <i>y-</i> axis perpendicular. The unit of measurement is the cubit. Points with coordinates (4<i>m</i>,4<i>n</i>) with integer <i>m</i>,<i>n</i> represent vines. According to Pythagoras, the distance from the origin is &lt;16 if and only if <i>m</i><sup>2</sup><i>+n</i><sup>2</sup><i>&lt;</i> 16. This means that for <i>m</i> = 0, ±1, ±2, <i>n</i> = 0, ±1, ±2, ±3, and for <i>m =</i> ±3, <i>n</i> = 0, ±1, ±2. In all, there are 5·7 + 2·5 = 45 admissible couples (<i>m</i>,<i>n</i>). This is explained in the Halakhah. There the language is that all acceptable couples are <i>m</i>,<i>n</i> = 0, ±1, ±2, ±3 except for couples (±3,±3). The case of vines planted 5 apart is not treated; one has to assume that one excludes the vines at points (5<i>m</i>,5<i>n</i>) for <i>m</i><sup>2</sup><i>+n</i><sup>2</sup><i>&lt;</i> 25 because vineyards planted 5 apart look similar to those planted 4 apart. (For an opposing opinion in the Tosephta cf. <i>Tosefta ki-fšuṭah</i> p. 629.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, only if he sowed near the middle vine. Rebbi Abin in the name of Samuel: Only if the middle vine is surrounded by vegetables. How is it computed? Seven rows by seven rows. Subtract from them four vines at the four corners; there are 45 vines remaining. That is what we stated: “He sanctifies 45 vines”<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">All this was explained in Note</i>.
“When is this? In case they are planted four four or five five apart.” This supports Rebbi Zeïra, since Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This has to be “R. Zeïra”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.6.1">Chapter 4, Note 122</a>. If the trunks were counted in the distances, the four vines at distance 16 from the center on the axes would also be forbidden, for a total of 49, not 45.</i> said: Eight except for the place of the trunks. “If they were planted six six or seven seven apart”, that supports Rebbi Eleazar, since Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 4, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8">Halakhah 8 (Note 125</a>), this also is a statement of R. Zeïra. The argument here is that “eight eight” is not mentioned, which implies that vegetables in a vineyard planted eight cubits apart do not “sanctify”.</i> said: From four cubits to eight, it is forbidden and “sanctifies”, from eight to sixteen it is forbidden but does not “sanctify”.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody sees a vegetable in his vineyard and says, when I get to it I shall remove it, that is permitted; when I return I shall remove it, if in the meantime it increased by one twohundredth it is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, they taught about the worker<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">“Somebody” mentioned in the Mishnah is not the owner, whose negligence would sanctify vines and vegetable and not just make the vegetable forbidden. It cannot be a stranger whose actions cannot influence the status of the farmer’s goods. Hence, it must refer to the farm hand who works in the vineyard for the proprietor.</i>.
They gave the owner occupied with his work the status of a farmhand<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">While the rules for the vintner are more strict that those for his employees, that only applies if he is not occupied tending his vineyard.</i>. If the owner kept field vegetables [growing] in his vineyard, they are forbidden to him and to others. If a farmhand kept field vegetables [growing] in the vineyard, they are forbidden to him but are permitted to everybody else. This is difficult. If they are forbidden to him, they should be forbidden to everybody. If they are permitted to everybody else, they should be permitted to him. It must follow Rebbi Simeon who says that nobody may sanctify anything that is not his own property. Even though Rebbi Simeon says that nobody may sanctify anything that is not his own property, he agrees here that it is forbidden to him if another person harvested it<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This is a fine for his laziness. If he did not remove the vegetable when he should have but returned later, the strict rule of the Mishnah applies.</i>; but if he himself harvested, it is forbidden to him and to everybody else.
“If it increased by one twohundredth it is forbidden.” Those of the house of Rebbi Yannai estimate by purslain. How does one check? Rav Vivian in the name of Rebbi Ḥaninah: He takes one out and leaves one in [the ground]; what the first one is less, the other did increase.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody was passing through a vineyard and accidentally dropped some seeds, or that seeds were brought there by manure or water, or someone sowed and the wind carried the seeds behind him, all this is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Unintentional contamination has no effect on the status of the vines.</i>. If the wind carried the seeds before him, Rebbi Aqiba says, if they produce greenery it should be plowed under, if they are green stalks, they should be broken, if they produced grain it must be burned<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the seeds fall on ground to be worked on in the future, he has to clear the ground. If he is derelict in his duties, the growth will be sanctified.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, our Mishnah speaks of one standing in a grain field while the wind carried the seeds into a vineyard. Rebbi Zeïra inquired<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">R. Zeïra shows that the statement of R. Eleazar about the part of the Mishnah dealing with windswept seeds is a logical necessity.</i>: Was [the Mishnah] speaking about one standing in [a grain field] or even one standing [in a vineyard]? If you say in a grain field, then not in a vineyard. If you say even one standing [in a vineyard] there is no difference. Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 3:12" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).3.12">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 3:12</a>. the arguments of R. Zeïra and R. Eleazar are accepted by Maimonides, <i>Kilaim</i>5:17.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Simeon: If one was sowing and the wind carried the seeds behind him, this is permitted, since it happened by accident.” What are we dealing with? If he stands in the vineyard he is tending it, and you say it is permitted? But we must deal with the case of one standing in a grain field and the wind carried the seeds into a vineyard.
Rebbi Zeïra, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: “If they produce greenery it should be plowed under:” all is permitted. “If they are green stalks, they should be broken:” the straw is permitted but the grains are forbidden. “If they produced grain it must be burned:” all is forbidden. Rebbi Joḥanan said, all is forbidden. What does our statement mean, “if they produce greenery it should be plowed under, if they are green stalks, they should be broken, if they produced grain it must be burned”? It parallels what we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 7:5" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.7.5">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 7:5</a>. There, Rashi in the Babli explains that “what usually is buried” is fluids, “what usually is burned” is produce. This cannot be the position of R. Joḥanan here. Maimonides in his Commentary explains the Mishnah following R. Hoshaia, whose position is easily squared with the Mishnah in <i>Temurah</i>, but in his Code (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>.) he follows R. Joḥanan since it is the position of the Babli that in a disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish, practice almost always follows R. Joḥanan. (For <i>orlah</i> cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.7.5">Note 94</a>).</i>: “<i>Orlah</i> and <i>kilaim</i> of a vineyard. Anything that usually is buried should be buried. Anything that usually is burned should be burned.”
Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Sometimes, the straw is permitted but the grain is forbidden, and sometimes the straw is forbidden and the grain is permitted. How is that? If his sowing was permitted but later he trained the leaves of a vine over it, the straw is permitted but the grain is forbidden If he sowed where it was forbidden and then removed the overhanging growth, the straw is forbidden but the grain is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">It is assumed that the grain developed only after the <i>kilaim</i> were removed. Then the grain is permitted. Similarly, in the first case it is assumed that the vines were drawn over the grain only after the stalks reached their full height, then the stalks grew while permitted and are permitted.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra inquired: It is understandable that the straw would be permitted but the grain forbidden. If the straw should be forbidden and the grain permitted, it grew while forbidden and you say is is permitted? Rebbi Zeïra follows his own opinion, as Rebbi Zeïra said in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: An onion from <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard that he removed from the soil and planted anew is forbidden even if it increases manifold, since growth of what is forbidden can never justify forbidden produce<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Since the onion is a root bulb, the root determines the growth of the plant. Since the root is forbidden, its growth will always be forbidden, even if the greens grow only after the <i>kilaim</i> were removed. “Justify” here means that there is enough of permitted produce (60, 100, or 200 times the amount of other produce, as the case may be) so that the prohibited produce should be considered negligible. It is asserted here that growth from a forbidden root is never permitted.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody keeps thistles growing in a vineyard, Rebbi Eliezer says he sanctified, but the Sages say only produce apt to be stored sanctifies. Iris<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἶρις, -ιδος, ἡ, “iris, orris root”; the root was used to produce an ointment.</i>, ivy<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κισσός. One would not expect a plant holy to Osiris and Bacchus in a Jewish vineyard, but its leaves were sometimes used as spice (and possibly in medicine) and the name might also be applied to related kinds of angelica of which both roots and berries were used. |Cf. κίσσινον, τό, name of a plaster (E. G.)]</i>, king’s lily, and all kinds of seed-plants<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Producing inedible seeds. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 15b" href="/Menachot.15b">Babli (<i>Menaḥot</i> 15b</a>) declares them to be <i>kilaim</i> by rabbinic decree.</i> are not <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard. Hemp<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κάνναβις, Latin <i>cannabis</i>.</i>, Rebbi Tarphon says it is not <i>kilaim</i> but the Sages say it is <i>kilaim</i>. Artichokes<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κινάρα, Modern Greek plural κινάρας (H. L. Fleischer). Since artichoke is an edible thistle, it has to be mentioned as <i>kilaim</i>.</i> are <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rabbi Abbahu said, the reason of Rebbi Eliezer is that at some places in Arabia one lets them grow for camels<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">See <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.1.3">Chapter 1, Note 15</a>.</i>.
ἰρός is אירוסיה. κισσός is קסוסא. King’s lily is κρίνον.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 3:15" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).3.15">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 3:15</a>. The next sentence is 3:14 there. The names of the plants appear in great variations, cf. תוספתא כפשוטה pp. 631–635.</i> Reeds, spiny bushes<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosepha mss. החגין, Arabic حاج، حيج “spiny bush”.</i>, roses, and way-thorn are trees and not <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard. Reed-mace<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In the Tosephta and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Makhshirin 5:5" href="/Mishnah_Makhshirin.5.5">Mishnah <i>Makhširin</i> 5:5</a> חשיפה, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 9:8" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.9.8">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>9:8</a> שיפה. A kind of reed, in the opinion of I. Löw <i>Typha angustata</i>. Rashi in Babli <i>Sukkah</i> 20a translates <i>paille</i>“straw”.</i>, rush<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The Gaonic commentary to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 9:8" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.9.8">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> (9:8</a>) defines this as equivalent of Arabic اسل “reeds used to make lances”; the corresponding Arabic verb means “to pierce”.</i>, and bulrush, and all other plants growing in ashes are kinds of grasses and not <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of Maimonides (<i>Kilaim</i> 5:19), these are not <i>kilaim</i> because they are grasses. In the opinion of R. Abraham ben David they are not <i>kilaim</i> because they are not collected as staples, but there are kinds of grasses subject to the laws of <i>kilaim</i>. The difference seems to be that Maimonides reads here and in the Mishnah ואינן but R. Abraham ben David reads אינן. There is no doubt that trees cannot be <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard since they are not sown.</i>.” But did Rebbi Hoshaiah not state<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.2-26"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1</a>; see there Notes 111–114 for the determination of the plants mentioned.</i>: “The following are the kinds of grasses: artichoke, <i>ḥalimah</i>,<i>demua</i>, and lycium.” There for benedictions, here for <i>kilaim</i>. Rebbi Yose said<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">His statement belongs to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 6:1:2-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.6.1.2-26"><i>Berakhot</i> 6:1</a>; one has to wonder why it was not placed there after the statement of R. Hoshaiah. R. Yose refers to the statement in <i>Berakhot</i> of R. Joshua that the appropriate benediction for edible young palm shoots is “Creator of kinds of grasses” (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. Note 110). On this R. Yose notes that the same argument might apply to the soft sprouting parts of citrus that have just formed after the flower has fallen off.</i>, this means that while one says the benediction “Creator of the fruit of the tree” for an <i>etrog</i>, one says the benediction “Creator of kinds of grasses” for its <i>tmrz</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The word is a <i>hapax</i> unless the ז is deleted. There are two possible translations of תמור: 1. “A new shoot growing straight up like a date palm”; 2. “A bud looking like a date”, as in Mishnah <i>Ma‘serot</i> 4:6. The second definition is preferred by R. Eliahu Fulda and all later commentators; both definitions are consistent with the parallel passage in <i>Berakhot</i>. The second meaning might be expressed in the Arabic root ثمر “to bear fruit”.</i>.
The caper bush<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">A shortened version, not mentioning grains, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 3:17" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).3.17">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 3:17</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 36a" href="/Berakhot.36a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i>36a</a>. The main place of the paragraph is <i>Ma‘serot</i> 4:6, in a discussion of the caper bush.</i>. The House of Shammai say, it is <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Shammai are unsure whether capers are trees or vegetables and therefore apply the stringency of vegetables for <i>kilaim</i> and those of fruit trees for <i>orlah</i>, ao that its fruit is forbidden the first three years after planting (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:23" href="/Leviticus.19.23"><i>Lev</i>. 19:23</a>).</i> but not with grains. The House of Hillel say, it is never <i>kilaim</i>, neither in a vineyard nor with grains. Both agree that it is subject to <i>orlah</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena bar Papa<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">He is R. Ḥanina bar Pappai. His statement appears in another version (another redaction) in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 5:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.5.2.2"><i>Baba Batra</i> 5:5</a>: “R. Ḥama bar ‘Uqba in the name of R. Yose. Anything that grows from both root and stem is a root (a vegetable); from its stem but not from its root it is a tree.” In that formulation, cabbage presents no problem since there is no piece of stem free of leaves.</i> stated: Any plant that branches out from its stem is a tree; from its roots it is a vegetable. They objected: But cabbage forms its leaves from its stem! Here<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The caper bush. Since for cabbage, the lower end of the stem is the lower end of the leaves, it is questionable whether the lowest leaves come from stem or root.</i> when it is sure, there when it is doubtful.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is a trellis<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This is the interpretation of most commentaries, from Hebrew ערש “crib”, Arabic عَرِبش “trellis, crib”. R. Abraham ben David in his commentary to <i>Idiut</i> 2:4 explains that five vines in a row form a vineyard for the House of Hillel only if the vines are growing on a trellis near a fence, not if the fence is used as a trellis. This is the only possible explanation in <i>Idiut</i>, but in his Commentary on this Mishnah, Maimonides explains that vines lean on or upon the (stone) fence. He gives the same interpretation in his Code (<i>Kilaim</i> 8:2) but then has to come to the conclusion (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. 8:3) that the four cubits to be measured from the fence are on the side pointing away from the vines. R. Abraham ben David protests against this interpretation which “never entered the mind of any commentator.” Maimonides’s interpretation is difficult to accept, the tortured justifications of R. David ben Zimra and R. Joseph Caro in their commentaries on the Code notwithstanding, since the vines according to the Mishnah can be up to three cubits distant from the fence.</i>? If somebody plants a row of five vines along a fence ten hand-breadths high or a ditch ten hand-breadths deep and four wide, one gives it four cubits working space. The House of Shammai say one measures four cubits between the stems of the vines and the field. The House of Hillel say, between fence and field<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For them, the trellis (or plantation) is not quite a vineyard; it is irrelevant where in the strip near the fence the vines are planted.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said, anybody who affirms this is in error; if there are four cubits between the stems of the vines and the fence one gives it its working space and he may sow the rest. What is the working space of a vine<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A single vine. For R. Ismael, five vines in a row are never a vineyard; they are a collection of single vines; the restrictions of the Mishnah apply only because the trellis is too close to the fence. But as soon as the vines are a full four cubits distant from the fence, the vines are treated as isolated. For R. Aqiba, the working space must have a diameter of six hand-breadths.</i>? Six hand-breadths in every direction; Rabbi Aqiba says three.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is difficult. Is it not forbidden anyhow for the House of Shammai as a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Five vines in a row constitute a vineyard for the House of Shammai, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.3.1">Mishnah 4:5</a>.</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this was taught about a curved trellis<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Simson, the trellis is in a straight line but the vines are planted alternatingly on both sides of the trellis; they are not in a straight line. According to R. Isaac Simponti, the trellis is built as arc of circle, to provide wider spacing for the branches of the vines exposed to the sun.</i>. In that case, it would not be forbidden as a vineyard without a fence. Rebbi Jonah inquired<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The interpretation of this paragraph and the next follows R. Iehiel Michel Halevi Epstein in his notes published as <i>Mēkhal Hammayîm</i>, Wilna 1928. The question is addressed to the House of Hillel in the interpretation of R. Simeon ben Laqish. For them, two parts of the definition of a vineyard are missing; there is only one row and the vines are not planted in a straight line. The Mishnah declares a trellis in front of a fence to have a status similar to a vineyard but it is not clear whether the rules of a vineyard are valid without change. Since in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.4.1">Mishnah 7</a>, the space required to separate two trellises is only half the space required to separate two vineyards, it is clear that the rules of vineyards cannot be applied without change to trellises.<br>We have to assume that also along the trellis the vines are planted at least four cubits apart. Since the vines are planted in a zig-zag pattern, between two vines in the same row the distance is almost eight cubits. Rebbi Jonah now asks, according to R. Simeon ben Laqish does the forbidden area cover the union of circular disks of radius 4 centered at the vines or is the entire convex hull of that domain (the smallest convex set containing the disks) forbidden? In the first case, between the vines the minimal distance from the trellis is less than 4 cubits; this is “in between”. The answer is that only in the case of vines planted in a straight line can one say that the interior of the parallel figure to the trellis in distance 4 is forbidden; here it seems that not even the entire parallel figure to the zig-zag line connecting the vines is forbidden but only the union of the five circular disks.<br>The second question then asks whether maybe not even the entire disks are forbidden but only the union of five polygons, each centered at a vine. Let <i>v</i> denote the center of a vine, <i>a</i> the point at distance 4 from <i>v</i> on the perpendicular from <i>v</i> to the fence, <i>a’</i> the reflection of <i>a</i> in <i>v</i>. On both sides of the line <i>a’va</i> draw parallels in distance 1. They intersect the parallels to the zig-zag line through <i>a</i> and <i>a’</i> at <i>b</i>,<i>b’; c</i>,<i>c</i>’. The polygon attached to <i>v</i> is then <i>abb’a’c’ca</i>. The forbidden area around <i>a</i> now is 8, not 16π &gt; 50, and even on the zig-zag line there is an interval of almost 2 cubits that might be sown. The answer is negative, with reference to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 3:6:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.3.6.2-3">Halakhah 3:7</a>.<br>FIGURE 6-1 HERE</i>: May one sow in between? Is it in a straight line, that you say it is forbidden to sow in between? May one sow between the vines? As you say in the case of a row of gourds that it is forbidden to sow in between the pits, so here also it is forbidden to sow in between the vines.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Mishnah was taught about a straight trellis. In that case, would it not be forbidden as a vineyard without a fence<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For the House of Shammai.</i>? Let us say, it was stated because of the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of R. Joḥanan, the presence of a trellis has no legal consequences for the House of Shammai; else he would have stated that for the House of Hillel a straight row on a trellis, for the House of Shammai even a curved trellis, would form a vineyard.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, it is reasonable to assume that Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish agrees with Rebbi Joḥanan that it was taught about a straight trellis because of the House of Hillel, and Rebbi Joḥanan agrees with Rebbi Simeon [ben Laqish] that it was taught about a curved trellis because of Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri, since Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri said that anybody who affirms this is in error. If you say it was taught about a straight trellis, what can be in error<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">This rhetorical question is difficult to accept, since for R. Joḥanan ben Nuri only one cubit around a vine has to be left empty, but for the House of Shammai four cubits along the trellis are required. The question seems to be that in a normal case, if the trellis is parallel to the fence at a distance of three cubits there is no difference since one may not sow between the trellis and the fence (R. Yose in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:4</a>) and the next cubit away from the fence is forbidden according to both Houses and R. Joḥanan ben Nuri. If the fence is a stone fence belonging in part to a neighbor, three cubits distance is the legal minimum (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Batra</i> 2:1</a>).</i>? What is the difference between him who says one measures from the roots of the vines and him who says one measures from the bottom of the fence? Rebbi Yose said, explain it if the stems were partially inside four cubits and partially outside of four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The trellis is straight but not parallel to the fence; at one end one cubit from the trellis is more than three cubits from the fence. If one measures from the vines, everything on this side of the fence is forbidden up to a distance of four cubits from the trellis; there is no difference between the House of Shammai and R. Joḥanan ben Nuri. But if one measures from the fence and the last vines are 4-ε cubits from the fence, then the House of Hillel forbid only a strip four cubits wide from the fence and R. Joḥanan ben Nuri will forbid up to 5- ε cubits from the fence.</i>. For him who says one measures from the roots of the vine, all is in order. For him who says one measures from the bottom of the fence, where the string drawn from the fence ends it is forbidden, the rest is permitted. If there were two cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is a different situation and refers to the disagreement between the Houses of Shammai and Hillel, but not to R. Joḥanan ben Nuri. Here the trellis is only two cubits from the fence; the fence must be the sole property of the vintner. According to Maimonides (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>.), the House of Shammai will forbid only strips of two cubits each on both sides of the trellis but the House of Hillel will forbid an additional two cubits on the other side of the fence. According to R. Abraham ben David, the explanation of Maimonides defies all logic since a fence separates in all respects. R. Abraham explains that for the House of Shammai, a strip of width 2 is forbidden on each side of the vines. If the vines are <i>d</i> cubits wide, the total width of the forbidden strip is 4+<i>d</i>. But for the House of Hillel, the width is only 4, less than the width required by the House of Shammai.</i>, only two cubits are forbidden for him who says one measures from the roots of the vines, but for him who says one measures from the bottom of the fence, another two cubits are forbidden.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Yose, Rebbi Ismael and Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri, all three said the same thing. Rebbi Ismael, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 2:4" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.2.4">Mishnah Idiut 2:4</a>, of the statements about accepted practices collected when Rabban Gamliel was deposed and R. Eleazar ben Azariah was installed as patriarch. The garden is a small plot, on each side a vine is planted and their branches are trained over a trellis. For a situation comparable to the one here one has to assume that plot and trellis are enclosed by a fence ten hand-breadths high.</i>: “About a small garden surrounded by a trellis, if there is room for the vintner and his basket on each side one may sow there, if not, it may not be sown.” Rebbi Jonah said, the vintner requires one cubit, his basket one cubit on each side. They wanted to say, for him who says one measures from the roots of the vine, all is in order. For him who says one measures from the bottom of the fence, do the stems not diminish the measure of four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">If one measures two cubits from each side of the stem of thickness <i>t</i> the distance of the end of the forbidden domain from the fence will be 4+<i>t</i> &gt; 4 and R. Ismael cannot agree with R. Joḥanan ben Nuri that as soon as the distance is &gt; 4 there are only isolated vines.</i>? Explain it if they were clinging to the wall. But are there not only four and the rules of the trellis start with five? Rebbi Ḥananiah said, explain it that two vines are planted at a corner; if one stretches a rope one sees four intervals<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">There are two possible interpretations of this statement. The traditional one is that there is a single vine on each side and an additional one planted at one corner for a total of six. But in that case, there are five intervals; in addition, the comparison between the opinions of R. Jose and R. Ismael would not work! R. Ḥananiah must insist that the rules of trellis do not depend on the number of vines but on the number of spaces between them. In a linear order, for four spaces one needs five vines. But in a circular arrangement, four vines define four intervals. I prefer to read the statement as: “<i>Any</i> two vines form a corner, for a total of four intervals.”</i>. Rebbi Yose, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:4</a>.</i>: “If there are less than four cubits one should not bring seeds there.” For him who says one measures from the roots of the vine, all is in order. For him who says one measures from the bottom of the fence, does the stem not decrease the measure of four cubits? Explain it if it was clinging to the wall. And similarly Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri here. It is reasonable to assume that Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Ismael will agree with Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri, but Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri will not agree with Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Ismael<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan ben Nuri might not agree that vines not planted in a straight line, even if strung on a trellis, ever need four cubits. But R. Yose and R. Ismael must agree that in the case of the Mishnah, the first four cubits are forbidden. R. Yose has to agree with R. Ismael since he requires four cubits even if the vines do not form a regular pattern, but we have no statement of R. Ismael if the stems of the vines do not form a square or at least a parallelogram.</i>. Rebbi Yose will agree with Rebbi Ismael but Rebbi Ismael will not agree with Rebbi Yose.
Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This must be Rav Jehudah. The following statement of R. Jacob bar Idi, taken from the end of Chapter 4, restricts the opinion of Rav to vineyards outside the Holy Land. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 39a" href="/Kiddushin.39a">Babli (<i>Qiddušin</i> 39a</a>) disagrees and holds that <i>kilaim</i> is forbidden only in the Holy Land; elsewhere only sowing grain directly under a vine is forbidden by rabbinic decree since in the Holy Land this would sanctify.</i> in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Practice outside the Land follows him who is lenient. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, there they stated: Practice outside the Land follows him who is lenient.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A trellis coming from a terrace. Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, if someone standing on the ground can harvest all [vines] then it forbids four cubits in the field; otherwise it only forbids around it<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of the trellis apply only if the harvest can be conducted in one plane; otherwise, the different parts planted on different levels are considered separate and its vines will have the status of isolated ones unless on each level there is a full trellis.</i>. Rebbi Eliezer says<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of R. David ben Zimra, the name should be deleted and the statement taken as anonymous Mishnah, since, in the discussion in the second paragraph of the Halakhah, the second statement is accepted as practice while the first one is rejected. A vineyard needs at least two rows; if the rows are ten hand-breadths apart in elevation no vineyard is formed.</i>, also if one plants one row on the ground and one on a terrace, if the latter is ten hand-breadth. higher they do not combine; otherwise, they do combine.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman, Rebbi Jonathan, in the name of Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Eliezer ben Jacob, as in the next statement, the parallel in <i>Ševi‘it</i>, and the corresponding text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 43b" href="/Sotah.43b">Babli <i>Soṭah</i> 43b</a>. The traditions of the Babli are in the name of R. Joḥanan, not R. Jonathan, and are not identical with the statements here.</i>: He who prunes his vineyard down to less than a hand-breadth subjects it to <i>orlah</i> because of the bad appearance<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The person who sees vine stumps less than a hand-breadth high might think of them as new growth whose yield is forbidden as <i>orlah</i> the first three years after planting (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:7:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.7.4">Chapter 5, Note 93</a>). The Sages apply <i>orlah</i> only if all growth above ground is cut off, and not as a matter of bad impressions. The Babli deals with a minimal vineyard of dwarf vines that never grow a hand-breadth tall and declares them to be permanent <i>orlah</i>.</i>. The Sages say, only if he prunes down to the ground. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, in the name of Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob: He who forms a heap from gleanings, forgotten sheaves, and <i>peah</i>, is subject to the Great Heave<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This is the regular heave, called “great” in contrast to the heave of the tithe (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 1:1:30" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.1.1.30"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 1, Note 127</a>). The Babli requires full tithing once a heap is formed on the field from exempt gifts to the poor (but the townspeople know the poor who have no land).</i> because of the bad appearance. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan, Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob in the name of the House of Shammai: A corpse in the public domain induces impurity four cubits around it, to sustain its dignity<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">A person standing over a corpse is forming a “tent” by his body and becomes impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19" href="/Numbers.19"><i>Num</i>. 19</a>). The House of Shammai extend this impurity by rabbinic decree to anybody within four cubits of the corpse, so that only those occupied with the burial will come near the corpse.</i>. Rebbi Mana said, this last statement is in the name of the House of Shammai; Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, all are in the name of the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">None of the statements in this paragraph are practice to be followed.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, also that which we stated here<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The statement of R. Eliezer ben Jacob in the Mishnah. If vines growing on two different levels did form a vineyard or a trellis in any Biblical sense, the rabbis could not make the definition dependent on whether all grapes can be harvested from the lower level. The other statements of R. Eliezer ben Jacob in this paragraph were introduced as background for R. Jonah’s statement.</i> is because of the bad appearance. If it were not so, what would be the difference whether one stands on the ground or on the terrace?
“Higher<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the second sentence of the Mishnah, of a row which is “higher than 10 hand-breadths” than the other row. In the entire discussion, “higher” means “higher than 10 hand-breadths”. The question is whether the terrace wall has to be 10 hand-breadths high or whether it might be less if only the vines appear on the surface at an elevation “higher than 10 hand-breadths”.</i>.” Is the row higher or is the terrace higher? If you say that the row is higher, the declivity of the terrace is counted as “below”. If you say the terrace is higher, the declivity of the row is counted as “above”. But did we not state: “Two rows on a terrace?<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">This must be from an otherwise unknown <i>baraita</i>, stating that one row below and two above do not form a “large vineyard” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:4:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.4.2-4">Halakhah 4:6</a>) but a small vineyard on the top and an isolated row at the bottom. Since the two upper rows may be at slightly different heights but are counted together, their position cannot be the deciding factor.</i>” Can you say that the row is higher? No, the terrace has to be higher. Rebbi Mana said, they declared the entire ten hand-breadths of the terrace a domain by itself<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">R. Mana rejects the entire discussion and declares the (vertical) declivity to be a separate domain, not counted either way, so anything sown there is not impacted by anything planted below or above.</i>.
We have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 11:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.11.3.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 11:4</a>. It is forbidden to transport anything in the public domain on the Sabbath a distance of more than four cubits. “Transporting” in the legal sense includes taking up, moving, and depositing. If one of these elements is missing, no offense occured. “Public domain” in this context extends only to ten hand-breadths above ground. Anything higher is free space to which the prohibition of transporting does not extend. Hence, if something was taken up from the public domain, thrown, and came to rest in free space, it is as if it was thrown and never came to rest; no offense occured. But if it came to rest within ten hand-breadths of the ground, the Sabbath was desecrated.</i>: “If somebody throws from four cubits [distance] to a wall, if [the projectile hits] higher than ten hand-breadths, he is like one throwing into the air, if lower than ten hand-breadths, he is like one throwing onto the ground.” Rav Ḥisda said, if it extends slanting<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Taking מדד not as Biblical Hebrew “to measure”, but from Arabic مدّ، مدّد “to extend, to rise”. R. Ḥisda’s problem is that nothing can stop on a vertical wall. The same answer is given by R. Ḥisda’s student and son-in-law Rava in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 5b" href="/Shabbat.5b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 5b</a>, the material there being sheets of paper or parchment. In the Yerushalmi, the discussion here is reproduced in <i>Šabbat</i>11:3 (fol. 14a).</i>. But will it not finally descend? Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, explain it that it was a soft fig cake and it stuck<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The same answer is given by R. Joḥanan himself in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 7b" href="/Shabbat.7b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 7b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 100" href="/Shabbat.100">100</a> a.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai asked before Rebbi Yose, does this not imply that the declivity of a terrace belongs to the level below<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥaggai assumes that the reason one insists on a separation of 10 hand-breadths is the same for the rules of the Sabbath and of <i>kilaim</i>. In that case, the Mishnah seems to extend the reach of any “bottom” upwards to 10 hand-breadths. He is answered that plants may grow on a slanted wall but anything sticking to the lower part of any wall bordering the public domain will be rubbed off; the fig cake cannot be considered to be at rest at such a place. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 100a" href="/Shabbat.100a">Babli (<i>Šabbat</i> 100a</a>) disagrees with R. Yose and points out that even if a Tannaitic statement such as suggested by him did exist, it would not prove anything since R. Meïr [Yerushalmi <i>Šabbat</i> 11:3 (fol. 13a), <i>Eruvin</i> 10:8 (fol. 26b); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 7b" href="/Shabbat.7b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 7b</a>, 100 a, <i>Eruvin</i> 11b, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 33b" href="/Shabbat.33b">33b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 101b" href="/Shabbat.101b">101b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 11b" href="/Yoma.11b"><i>Yoma</i> 11b</a>] considers any depression a separate domain excluded from “free space”.</i>? He said to him, there the vegetables profit from the terrace but here people rub it and it falls down. If it would say, if there is a hole [in the wall] and it profits from the hole just as vegetables profit from the terrace, you would be justified.
“Two gardens, one above the other, the lower one is made into a vineyard, the upper one is not made into a vineyard. One may sow the upper one until one reaches the airspace of ten<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 3:9" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).3.9">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 3:9</a>, in a slightly different text. Instead of “the airspace of ten”, the Tosephta has: “the stems of the lower one.” The text is missing in the Vienna ms.; the language is probably influenced by the second sentence. The version in the Yerushalmi permits sowing on the declivity above 10 hand-breadths from the bottom. The question of R. Abun bar Ḥiyya refers to these lower 10 hand-breadths.</i>.” Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Zeïra, does this not imply that the declivity of a terrace belongs to the below? He said to him, because plants will bend into the airspace of the vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This part of the statement does not prove anything; since we speak about sowing on an incline, the growing plants will bend over the vines.</i>. “If the upper one is made into a vineyard but the lower one is not made into a vineyard, one may sow the lower one and the declivity until one reaches the roots of the vines.” Rebbi Yose said, one does not have here “the roots of the vines<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The depth of the “roots of the vines” would be different for different strains of grapes. R. Yose insists that the three-hand-breadths rule proclaimed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.8.2">Halakhah 1:8 (Notes 158</a> ff.) and quoted by him is valid for all plants, even vines.</i>” but “below three [hand-breadths],” following that which was stated: “roots of madder that enter within four cubits of a vineyard are permitted below three hand-breadths.”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody trains a vine over part of a <i>papyrus</i><sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Definition of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:3" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.3">Gaonic commentary to <i>Kelim</i> 17.3</a>. Some sources here and in <i>Kelim</i> have אפפיריו, a medieval commentator writes הפיפיריות. The latter reading, seeing in the prothetic א the definite article ה, is supported by the Halakhah which uses פיפור, פיפר and the similar reading of most commentaries in <i>Kelim</i>. Most moderns see in the word a form of Greek πάπυρος (but this fits better with Tosephta <i>Kelim b</i>.<i>m</i>. 5:15, כלי פפיר “paper vessels”.) According to the Geonim, one takes rope from papyrus leaves (according to Maimonides, the reeds themselves) and puts them crosswise into a frame in order to form a platform that will be supported by posts. Sources confirming the use of papyrus rope in Antiquity are adduced by Loew in Krauss’s Dictionary, <i>s. v.</i> פיפור.<br>{In view of the formal parallelism between “part of a <i>papyrus</i>” and “part of a futile tree”, “part of a fig tree”, one might also take <i>papyrus</i> as a living plant, rather than a rope or mat of papyrus fibers.<br><i>Papyrus antiquorum</i>, mostly perennial grassy plants cultivated throughout the ancient world, had multiple commercial uses: e. g., <i>Cyperus esculentus</i> with edible, oil-rich bulbs (<i>Bulbuli Trasi</i>,<i>Dulcinia</i>), <i>Cyperus officinalis</i>, used medically, <i>Cyperus textilis</i>, used in basket-weaving and paper manufacture. A papyrus plant growing near a vine would therefore also make sense in a discussion of <i>kilaim</i>. (E. G.)}</i>, he should not sow under the rest, but if he did, it does not become sanctified<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear from the last sentence in the Mishnah that one speaks here of a single vine, not a vineyard or a trellis of five vines. Therefore, only a minimal distance has to be kept from the stem of the vine. Since the vine will expand, and the area below the part of the papyrus not covered by the branches and leaves of the vine is certainly more than a cubit away from the stem and can be used for other crops. However, if either the sown plants or the vine expand so that now parts of the plants are directly under the vine, the plants become forbidden. A prudent farmer, therefore, will not even start sowing close to the vine.</i>. If the new growth expands it becomes forbidden. The same applies to one who trains over parts of a futile tree<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">In modern Hebrew, the term means “non-fruit-bearing tree” and סרק (2) has the general connotation of “useless, abortive”. [סרק (1) means “to comb”.] The word may be derived from the root that appears as Arabic סרק, Assyrian שרק, “to rob” (as in Saracen), a tree which robs the farmer of his labor if he would tend it. It is clear from the next Mishnah that a tree bearing cheap fruit is also אילן סרק. Because it does not bear high quality fruit, it has the same status as a rope or a beam.</i>.<br>If somebody trains a vine over part of a fruit tree he may sow under the rest. If new grows expands, he must move it<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">He must move the vine to reestablish the required separation.</i>.<br>It happened that Rebbi Joshua visited Rebbi Ismael from Kefar Aziz<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This is the reading of the Leyden ms. and the texts dependent on it. All other Mishnah texts have: “Rebbi Joshua visited Rebbi Ismael at Kefar Aziz”, לכפר instead of מכפר. In that case, the host is R. Ismael (ben Elisha), the student of R. Joshua, who should have known his teacher’s opinion. It therefore seems likely that the Yerushalmi version is superior and the host is a minor figure mentioned only here. The paragraph establishes the previous Mishnah as R. Joshua’s.</i>. He showed him a vine trained over parts of a fig tree and asked, may I sow under the rest? He told him, it is permitted. He took him from there to the armory and showed him a vine trained over part of a beam and a sycamore stump on which were many beams. He said to him, under that one beam it is forbidden, the rest is permitted.<br>What is a futile tree? One which does not bear fruit. Rebbi Meïr says, every tree is futile except olive and fig trees. Rebbi Yose says, a futile tree is one of which one does not plant entire orchards.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Ḥizqiah said, the Mishnah speaks about distances from three to four hand-breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In all respects, a distance of up to three hand-breadths is neglected, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.2.1">Mishnah 4:4</a>, <i>Eruvin</i> 1:9, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.10.2"><i>Sukkah</i> 1:9</a>. Hence, sowing within three hand-breadths of a vine certainly sanctifies. If the distance is more than three but less than four, one tells the farmer to complete the four hand-breadths. More than four, while still forbidden, does not sanctify and even if the vine leaves grow they do not interfere as long as the distance is more than three hand-breadths.</i>. Less than three is close up, up to four one has to complete, four is easy for it. How far<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">How much does one have to separate from the place shaded by the vine leaves that it should not be forbidden to sow there? R. Jacob bar Idi applies the rules of a trellis to the vine trained over a lattice. It is difficult to find a reason for the second opinion given in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish and probably difficult to find a papyrus mat wide enough that away from the vine there should still be six unoccupied cubits. {It seems, therefore, that R. Simeon ben Laqish takes פיפיוד as name of the plant; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.3.1">Note 38</a>.}</i>? Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, up to four cubits. Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, up to six cubits. Rebbi Mana said: I went to Caesarea and heard Rebbi Hoshiah bar Shammai in the name of Rebbi Isaac ben Eleazar: If there were two papyri, it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">If two papyrus mats (or ropes, or plants) are laid out parallel over poles and a vine is trained over one of them, the other one is not affected. The opposing opinion states that if the vintner in the ordinary care of the vineyard usually steps from under one mat to under the other, they are considered as one and subject to the 4 cubits/6 cubits rule.</i>. Rebbi Ḥinena said, a statement disagrees: If he usually oversteps, it is forbidden. It was stated thus: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says, if he usually oversteps between lattices, it is treated as a single lattice.
What is the difference between a futile and a fruit tree? A person will disregard a futile tree relative to his vine but nobody will disregard a fruit tree relative to his vine<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The “futile” tree will not be considered as more than the support of the vine and will not receive attention for its own sake.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 26:4" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.26.4">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 26:4</a>. The only things that may be impure are living humans, dead humans and animals, vessels and garments, food and drink, and houses. Tractate <i>Kelim</i> deals with the impurity of vessels and textiles. In that tractate, “pure” means that the vessel in question cannot possibly become impure; “impure” means that it may become impure if exposed to impurity.<br>If a vessel is used as a container, it can become impure as long as it can be used as container. If an impure vessel springs a leak large enough to make it unusable for its primary purpose, it becomes pure. Animal hides used as containers for water or wine are obtained by skinning slaughtered lambs or sheep. The skin then is open at the neck, the tail, and the four feet. If the feet and tail end are sown tight, there is no doubt that a permanent container was formed which may become impure. If the feet are only tied with rope, they also can become untied; the vessel so created is temporary and cannot become impure. The anonymous Tanna states that Arabs never sow their skins; they have developed a technique to tie the skins with rope to create permanent vessels. R. Meïr extends the rule to all skins whose knots are not supposed to be opened. R. Yose notes that any knot can be untied without damaging the skin; hence, no knot is permanent.</i>, we have stated: “All tied-up water skins are pure except those of Arabs. Rebbi Meïr says, temporarily tied ones are pure, permanently tied ones are impure. Rebbi Yose says, all tied ones are pure.” Some Tannaïm state it inverted<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">They switch the statements attached to the names of R. Meïr and R. Yose. This invites comparison with the Mishnah here.</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Yasa: Like our Mishnah. Rebbi Yudan said, the indicator of <i>Kelim</i> is <i>Kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Kilaim</i>, a “futile” tree is turned into a prop for the vine and loses its character of tree by the vintner’s intention as stated in the preceding paragraph; so likewise the knots with which the skin is tied are temporary or permanent according to the intention of the owner. If we do not change the names in <i>Kelim</i>, the arguments of R. Meïr become consistent; the Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> should not be changed.</i>, for if it were not so, what would be the difference between permanent and temporary tie? In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said: A permanent tie must be cut, a temporary tie does not have to be cut<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The House of Yannai disagree with R. Yasa: The difference between temporary and permanent knots is not one of intent but of fact. A temporary knot is one that may be untied by hand, even if it is intended to be permanent.
