A permanent knot is one that cannot be untied with one’s fingers; it is so tight that it can be removed only by cutting the rope. In that case, a tool was needed to create the openings; this action is as if one used a drill to make a hole in a pot, which destroys the vessel and makes it pure. As a consequence, the permanently tied skin is a vessel and can become impure; there are parallel arguments in <i>Kelim</i> and <i>Kilaim</i>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> An interrupted trellis is more than eight cubits. No linear measurement stated by the Sages requires “more than” except an interrupted trellis. What is an interrupted trellis? A trellis [whose vines are] dried up in its middle, but there remained five vines on each side<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">One vine in the middle died. The original large trellis is now split into two small legal trellises.</i>. If between them are eight cubits, one should not sow there. If there are more than eight cubits, one gives each side its working space<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The usual six hand-breadths around the trellises in each direction.</i> and he may sow the rest.<br>A trellis which starts from a wall in a corner and ends there<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides, the trellis itself is built parallel to the walls, forming an angle. According to R. Simson (and apparently R. Abraham ben David), the five vines are in a straight line from one wall to the other, forming the base of a right triangle of which parts of the two walls form the legs. Then six hand-breadths from the base in the triangle are forbidden, the remainder may be sown even if the altitude of the triangle is less than four cubits.</i>, one gives it its working space and he may sow the rest. Rebbi Yose said, if there are less than four cubits one should not bring seeds there<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Mishnah 5:4, Note 56.</i>. Sticks that stand out from the trellis, if he worries whether to remove them<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The trellis has irregular cross beams. If he would like to cut them to one size but for some reason (e. g., not to touch a shaky trellis) does not do it, the excess width does not interfere with sowing. But if he intends to use the beams to support extending branches and leaves then they are part of the trellis and forbidden.</i>, then under them it is permitted, but if he made them so that the new growth can expand on them, it is forbidden.<br>One looks at a blossom that comes out from the trellis as if a plumb-line were hanging down from it; under it it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Any growth expanding over the sides of the trellis extends the area under which nothing can be sown (plus its six hand-breadths of working space).</i>. The same holds for a branch<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">A branch of a single vine. The forbidden space is the interior of the parallel figure in distance 6 of the orthogonal projection of the entire vine into the ground.</i>. Under a shoot of vine drawn from one tree to another, it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Naturally plus six hand-breadths on each side.</i>. If he binds them together with a rope or bast, under the binding it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The vine is not long enough to reach from one tree to the next; the vintner tied a rope around the end of the shoot and the tree. Then the rope is not a vine and not subject to the laws of <i>kilaim</i>. But if the rope was drawn to support future growth of the vine or other shoots that will appear in the growing season, it becomes subject to the laws of vines.</i>. But if he did that to accomodate future growth, it is forbidden.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Does this not disagree with Rebbi Zeïra since Rebbi Zeïra said<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.6.2-4">Halakhah 4:8</a>, speaking of the parallel rules for vineyards.</i>, eight except for the place of the trunks? Explain that as eight cubits and a little more<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Less than a hand-breadth. As explained here, the minimal length of which formal congnizance is taken is a hand-breadth, one sixth of a cubit; smaller distances are not enumerated in the Mishnah. If the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.1.3.1">Mishnah (<i>Eruvin</i>1:3</a>, <i>Ahilut</i> 12:6) states that the circumference of a circle of diameter 1 is 3 it means that 3 ≤ π ≤ <sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup>/<sub>6</sub> (3 ≤ π ≤ <sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup>/<sub>5</sub>).</i>. And why has this not been stated? Are you not concurring with Rebbi Joḥanan that in all linear measurements stated by the Sages, “more than” means [more by] a hand-breadth<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 4:9" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).4.9">Tosephta (<i>Kilaim</i> 4:9</a>) explains: כמה הוא ועוד אחד מששה טפחים באמה. “How much is ‘more than’? One of six hand-breadths per cubit.” The correct interpretation has been given by R. S. Lieberman: In Tosephta <i>Kelim</i>,<i>Baba Meẓia‘</i> 6:13, R. Meïr declares the cubits used in a vineyard to be cubits of five hand-breadths (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.2.1">Chapter 5, Note 27</a>). The Tosephta here redefines the hand-breadth to be added as one <i>new hand-breadth</i>, when 6 <i>new hand-breadths</i> are 5 common hand-breadths or one vine cubit. According to the Tosephta, “more than” means at least ⅚ of a common cubit. This Tosephta text is in the manuscript and the <i>editio princeps</i>. R. Isaac Simponti, R. Simson and R. Abraham ben David read “one sixth of a cubit”, leaving open the possibility that hand-breadth and cubit are the standard ones. Maimonides has another reading, “1/60 cubit”, supported by <i>Caphtor Waperaḥ</i> and R. Bezalel Ashkenazi. However, as R. Abraham ben David points out, such a text would contradict the Yerushalmi here; the length added by R. Zeïra is certainly more than a tenth of a hand-breadth.</i>? But what he added is little, therefore it was not stated.
Rebbi Jonah asked: Why do we say: “No linear measurements stated by the Sages require ‘more’ except an interrupted trellis?” Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Menachot 7:2" href="/Mishnah_Menachot.7.2">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 7:2</a>: “The flour sacrifice of a <i>Nazir</i> came in two parts, loaves and flatbreads, but no paste (from flour and oil). It turns out that these are 10 Jerusalem <i>qab</i> which equal six <i>esronim</i> and excess.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.1.1">Mishnah 7:1</a> had stated that the three kinds of bread for a thanksgiving sacrifice came from 10 <i>esronim</i> which were 15 Jerusalem <i>qab</i>. Hence, the breads of the <i>Nazir</i> are 6⅔ <i>esronim</i>. The “excess” (in the Hebrew, an otherwise nonexisting plural of “more”) is ⅔ <i>issaron</i>. We use the expression “more” in a domain other than vineyards.</i>: “Ten Jerusalem <i>qab</i> which equal six <i>esronim</i> and excess”? There about volumes, here about linear measures. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 4:7: “R. Eliezer says, heave can be lifted by one in 101. Rebbi Joshua says, by one in more than 100; that ‘more’ has no measure. Rebbi Yose ben Meshullam said, ‘more’ is a <i>qab</i> per one hundred seah, a sixth of the amount that causes <i>dema‘</i>”. <i>Dema‘</i>, the mixture of profane food and heave, was discussed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 4:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.4.1.9"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 4, Note 27</a>. If the amount of heave is small, it is possible to remove an amount equal to the heave and transfer the holiness to that food; the remainder of the food then returns to profane status. R. Eliezer states that in this respect “small” means at most 1 in 100 (1 part heave in 101 overall). R. Joshua requires that the amount be at most 1 in 99+ε; ε &gt;0 being arbitrarily small. R. Yose ben Meshullam requires that the amount of heave be at most 1 in 99.1666̄; if the amount of heave is one <i>seah</i>, the amount of profane grain has to be 99 <i>seah</i> plus one sixth oí the amount causing the trouble, i. e., 99 ⅙ <i>seah</i>. {In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 83a" href="/Eruvin.83a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 83a</a>, “and more” is defined as one twentyfourth of the volume of an egg.}</i>: “Rebbi Yose ben Meshullam said, ‘more’ is a <i>qab</i> per one hundred seah, a sixth of the amount that causes <i>dema‘</i>”? There about volumes, here about linear measures. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.2.5.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 2:5</a>: “Additionally, R. Jehudah ben Baba said, one may carry (on the Sabbath) in a garden or a corral which measure seventy and a remainder by seventy and a remainder and are enclosed by a fence ten hand-breadths high on condition that they contain a watchman’s place.” On the Sabbath, one may carry his utensils in his house and in any enclosed space containing a human dwelling. Enclosed spaces no part of which is used as a human dwelling, such as vegetable plots and corrals, are accepted as private domain only if their surface area is not more that 5000 square cubits, the surface area of the enclosed space of the tabernacle (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.9"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 2, Note 31</a>). Since √5000 = 70.71068, R. Jehudah ben Baba’s remainder is 0.71068 cubits, a linear measure.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah ben Baba said, the garden and the corral which are seventy and a remainder by seventy and a remainder”? Samuel said, they taught two thirds of a cubit<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Samuel notes that for non-mathematicians the domain is limited to 70 cubits 4 hand-breadths square, 70.666̄ cubits square. The excess over 70 is a genuine remainder, less than one cubit, but it is not a “more” which by definition can be at most one hand-breadth, the smallest unit of length accepted in these tractates of the Mishnah.</i>.
There is no difference whether one built a fence and then planted eleven vines or he planted eleven vines and then built a fence<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.1.1">Note 1</a> for a discussion of the role of the fence.</i>. If the middle vine is destroyed we have trellis and interrupted trellis<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">There now are two legal trellises that form one interrupted trellis.</i>. If the fence is destroyed, there is no trellis and no interrupted trellis. Rebbi Eudaimon, brother of Rebbi Yose, inquired: If he then rebuilt the fence, since the trellis was reconstituted, do you say that the interrupted trellis also was reconstituted<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since growing other crops in a vineyard is a potential transgression of a biblical prohibition, in doubt one has to take the more severe position. The absence of an answer therefore implies a positive answer.</i>?
Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Once Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri burned in Nagnegar<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.2.3">Chapter 4, Note 58</a>.</i>. What did he burn? He burned an interrupted trellis<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">He considered the growth illegal and sanctified; therefore, he destroyed it by burning. Since this is recorded as a memorable event, it cannot be accepted as common practice; one does not make a statement every time an accepted law has been executed.</i>. Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Once Rebbi Joḥanan ben Nuri burned in Nagnegar. What did he burn? He burned between the trellis and the fence. Rebbi Yose said, I pointed out a difficulty before Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa: It is understandable, if he burned an interrupted trellis then the words of the Sages disagree with his<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The majority follows <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.1">Mishnah 7:3</a> that the prohibition of an interrupted trellis is rabbinical, not biblical. A crop growing there does not sanctify and should not be burned.</i>. But if he burned between the trellis and the fence, are there words of the Sages which disagree with his? He had not heard what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said: Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish disagree about the trellis itself: Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is forbidden and sanctifies. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is forbidden but does not sanctify<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Joḥanan, five vines on a trellis form a vineyard in the biblical sense; according to R. Simeon ben Laqish there is never a vineyard in the biblical sense unless it contains at least two parallel rows.</i>.
All this applies if in the corner there are four hand-breadths of empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah is valid only if between any vine and any fence there are at least four hand-breadths of empty space; otherwise, six hand-breadths are forbidden around every vine, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:4</a>.</i>.
We stated here the opinion of Rebbi Yose and stated it there<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:4</a>. True duplicate statements are not admitted in the Mishnah (except in collections of stated historical character, e. g., Tractate Idiut.) Therefore, if a duplicate statement does occur, one has to find reasons to explain the necessity of the duplication.</i>. If we had stated it here but not there, we would have said he says it is forbidden here because of the laws of the trellis, but there where one does not deal with the laws of the trellis he would say it is permitted. Hence, it is necessary to state it there. Or if we had stated it there but not here, we would have said there he says it is forbidden because it is enclosed on all four sides, but here where it is not enclosed on all four sides he would say it is permitted. Hence, it is necessary to state it both here and there.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia.</i> one says, one considers as if a metal spit was stuck there. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: As far as was intended<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The translation is tentative. Medieval authors do not comment on this sentence. R. Eliahu Fulda and almost everybody else identify נקמן with נקמז "shrunk". R. Eliahu Wilna explains as "bent". These explanations have no basis and make little sense. I am taking קמן in the sense of Arabic "to intend". Then this paragraph belongs to the preceding Halakhah: Under an irregular trellis, the width is forbidden as far as the vintner intended to let the vines grow. At the end of this space, it is as if a metal spit subtended the bars; the wood sticking out farther is not counted.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Galilean Amora of the third generation. He quotes R. Simeon ben Laqish and is reported as teacher of R. Yasa (Assi).</i> in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Under the grapes it is forbidden and sanctifies but not under the leaves. Rebbi Yose said, even under the leaves it is forbidden and sanctifies<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Vine leaves are also a commercial crop.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one sinks<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">He takes a branch of a vine, bends it down into a ditch, covers the ditch with earth, and lets it come out again. Then the branch will grow roots in the earth and one has a new vine.</i> a vine into the ground he should not bring seeds over it unless there are three hand-breadths of earth above it, even if he sank it in a gourd<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A dried-up gourd used as ditch.</i> or in a pipe<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">A wooden or clay pipe.</i>. If he sank it in a rock<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">It cannot really be a rock but must be ground hard as rock.</i> it is permitted to bring seeds there even if it is covered only by three fingers’<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">A finger is one fourth of a hand-breadth.</i> depth of earth. For a knee-shaped vine one measures only from the second stem<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If a vine is bent back while growing and enters the ground again in a kind of natural sinking, the six hand-breadths for its working space are measured only from the point where it reappears above ground. (R. Simson explains: “a grafted vine” but this does not fit either language or context.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He should not bring seeds over it,” therefore it is permitted on the side<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 19b" href="/Bava_Batra.19b">Babli, <i>Baba Batra</i> 19b</a>, this is stated as a <i>baraita</i>.</i>. What are we dealing with? If it is because of seeds in a tree, why are we dealing with a vine and not any tree? If it is because of working space, they should mention six<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah should state that six hand-breadths on each side of the buried shoot are forbidden as working space, or at least three handbreadths following Rebbi Aqiba (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.1.1">Mishnah 6:1</a>).</i>. It might follow Rebbi Aqiba since Rebbi Aqiba says three. If it were following Rebbi Aqiba, it should mention three on the side. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: It is because of seeds on top of a vine<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This confirms the original statement that the only forbidden space is on top of the buried shoot.</i>.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.1.1">Mishnah <i>Baba Batra</i> 2:1</a>. The adobe wall is either common property of the landlord and his neighbor or totally the neighbor’s; the landlord is required to take all necessary precautions to avoid damage to his fellow man’s property.</i>, we have stated: “One keeps seeds, the plough, and urine three hand-breadths away from a wall.” Rebbi Jeremiah said that Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba raised a question: There<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">“There” means <i>Kilaim</i> and “here” refers to <i>Baba Batra</i>. It is possible that this paragraph is copied here from a Tiberian version of <i>Baba Batra</i> that has not come down to us. In the transmitted Caesarean version of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.1.2-4"><i>Baba Batra</i> (2:1</a>, fol. 13b), the statement of R. Yose refers to the prohibition of storing salt or quick lime near somebody else’s wall. R. Simson reads “here” for “there” but it is not clear whether this is a genuine reading or an adaptation for the purposes of his commentary.<br>The argument is that crop plants are allowed to be grown near a buried vine; their roots are not supposed to expand over the buried shoot, but since they are not allowed to be grown near a wall, one expects their roots to expand sideways and damage the wall.</i>, you say that the roots do not expand sideways and here you say, the roots do expand sideways! Rebbi Yose said, in both cases the roots do not expand sideways but they create soft earth and undermine the base of the wall<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 19b" href="/Bava_Batra.19b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 19b</a>, the printed editions quote the statement of R. Yose as “R. Ḥaga in the name of R. Yose.” This version is impossible since R. Ḥaga (Ḥaggai) was older than R. Yose and a greater authority in his lifetime. One has to prefer the reading of all mss. (<i>Diqduqe Soferim Baba Bathra</i> p. מה) “R. Ḥana”. In that case, “R. Yose” would probably be R. Yasa (Assi).</i>. You can see that because we also stated “urine”. Does urine expand sideways? Rebbi Yose the son of Rebbi Abun explained, did we not also state “the plough”? Does the plough expand sideways?
“Even if he sank it in a gourd or in a pipe.” That means, in a clay pipe; but in a lead pipe it does not need three hand-breadths of earth on top.
“If he sank it in a rock.” That means, in hard [earth], but in soft [earth] it cleaves<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simson reads מתפתחת “it [the root] can develop”.</i> it.
That is<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the last part of the Mishnah, regarding the knee-shaped vine.</i> only if the first stem is not visible, but if the first stem is visible one allows six hand-breadths from either stem. You can see that because we also stated afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.1">Mishnah 7:2</a>.</i>: “If one sinks three vines and their stems are visible,” and we have stated on this: When has this been said? For a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Regarding the conditions under which three vines can be extended to form a legal vineyard. If they are too close to or too far from one another, each one of the six vines so created is given its own working space.</i>. But concerning the working space one allows six hand-breadths for either one.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one sinks three vines and their stems are visible, Rebbi Eleazar bar Rebbi Zadoq says if there are between them from four to eight cubits, they go together; otherwise, they do not go together<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">If one has three vines in a row and sinks a shoot from each one to get another row of three vines, if the distance between the rows and between the vines in one row is between four and eight cubits then one has a legal vineyard since five of the six vines would be enough (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:4:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.4.2-4">Halakhah 4:6</a>). If the distances are too small, one has six single vines.</i>. A dried-up vine is forbidden but does not sanctify<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">If one sows within six hand-breadths from a dead vine it does not sanctify.</i>. Rebbi Meïr says, cotton also is forbidden but does not sanctify. Rebbi Eleazar bar Rebbi Zadoq says in his name<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In R. Meïr’s name. This attribution is missing in the Genizah ms., probably correctly; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.2">Note 23</a>.</i>, even on top of the vine it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this sentence will be clarified in the Halakhah.</i> but does not sanctify.<br>The following are forbidden but do not sanctify: The excess of a dried-up vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">By <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:1</a>, if a vineyard dried up in the middle, a space of 16 cubits square cannot be sown. But only four cubits on each side are needed for working the vineyard. If the dried-up space contains a square of sides 16-ε, the inner (8-ε)-by-(8-ε) space cannot be worked on as a rabbinic, rather than a biblical prohibition. Working that inner space is forbidden but a transgression does not sanctify either growth or vineyard.</i>, the excess of the circumference of a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">By <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:2</a>, 12 cubits have to be left empty between a vineyard whose outer rows have died and the fence. Again, if there are 12-ε cubits, of these only four are biblical, the remaining 8-ε have rabbinic status only.</i>, the excess of an interrupted trellis<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since the constituent parts of an interrupted trellis do not form vineyards by themselves, of the eight cubits between the inner vines only one cubit on each side (six hand-breadths) will sanctify, the remaining six cubits represent rabbinic prohibition.</i>, and the excess of a papyrus<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">If vines that do not form a vineyard are drawn over papyrus, only the space under any part of any vine is biblically forbidden; the rest is a rabbinic prohibition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 6:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.6.3.2">Halakhah 6:3</a>).</i>. But under a vine, the working space of a vine, and the four cubits of a vineyard do sanctify.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah discusses only the second part of the Mishnah, listing the plants near which it is forbidden to sow but which do not sanctify. Since part of the Mishnah is attributed to R. Meïr, all of it belongs to R. Meïr. The statement makes more sense if the Mishnah follows the reading of the Genizah text, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.2.1">Note 20</a>.</i>.
One understands in winter<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement that it is forbidden to sow next to a dead vine. The only reason for such a rule is bad appearance, that passers-by will not notice that the vine is dead and will think that the farmer sows forbidden seeds. One understands that this is a concern in late fall and early winter, after the old leaves have been shed and the new ones did not grow yet, but in summer when the leaves cover the ripening grapes there should be no problem. The answer is that there is a problem with varieties whose grapes ripen in June and will have lost their leaves by mid-July.</i>, but in summer? There are places where they lose their leaves even in summer.
Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Zeïra: The Mishnah speaks of the space above the shoot<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence in the Mishnah, “even on top of the vine it is forbidden but does not sanctify.” Rebbi Zeïra refers this to the shoot of vine sunk under less than three hand-breadths of earth.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: The Mishnah speaks about the air space of ten<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">If the vines are on a terraced hill and the next terrace is at least ten hand-breadths higher, the wall of the terrace terminates the working space (for vineyard or single vine); planting inside six hand-breadths or four cubits, as the case may be, is forbidden but does not sanctify if the vertical distance is at least ten hand-breadths. {R. Simson thinks that one speaks about plants in flower pots hanging over the vines; Maimonides might agree with this interpretation.}</i>. Rebbi Yose said, we were of the opinion that a vineyard has air space; a single vine has no air space. Since Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: The Mishnah speaks about the air space of ten, that means that even a single vine has air space.
The excess of the circumference of a vineyard is four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The language is elliptic: The excess of the circumference of a vineyard is defined as excess of the distance over and above 4 cubits, as explained in the Notes to the Mishnah. The other statements have a corresponding interpretation.</i>, the excess of a dried-up vineyard is four cubits, the excess of an interrupted trellis is six hand-breadths, and the excess of papyrus is six hand-breadths.
What is the status of the intermediate spaces<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.3.1">Mishnaiot 4:8–9</a>, that if a vineyard is planted in such a way that the distance from one vine to the next in both directions is more than eight but less than 16 cubits, one may not sow there. This situation really should have been dealt with in our Mishnah since in that configuration there are points distant more than four cubits from any vine. Since it is not mentioned in our Mishnah, R. Joḥanan concludes that sowing there must sanctify. {The explanation of this paragraph follows an idea of R. Eliahu Wilna who, however, feels compelled to rewrite the text.}</i>? Rebbi Joḥanan said, they are forbidden and sanctify. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, they are forbidden and do not sanctify. What can you say<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Here follows the argument of R. Joḥanan against R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>? If it is a large vineyard, what about its dry spot, even where it is planted<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since for R. Simeon ben Laqish any spot distant more than eight but less than 16 cubits is forbidden but does not sanctify, why does the Mishnah speak about a dry spot?</i>! If it is a small vineyard, it has no circumference<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.7">Chapter 4, Note 37</a>. The Mishnah cannot speak about a “small” vineyard.</i>. But we must deal with a large vineyard planted in the manner of a small one<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The distances from one vine to the other and between two rows of vines are at least four but definitely less than eight cubits. In that case, the objections do not apply.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, this implies that a large vineyard planted eight by eight has no circumference<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah declares that outside of eight it does not sanctify; dried-up vines cannot make the situation worse.</i>. If you say that the inside is forbidden and sanctifies if it is planted 16 by 16, the outside not so much more<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Simeon ben Laqish’s argument against all the preceding. If inside it sanctifies up to 16 cubits there would be no reason to require only 12 on the outside.</i>?
Rebbi Yose said, one whips only for the main vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the Mishnah establishes the cases in which the new growth sanctifies, the biblical commandment that can be enforced by the criminal court only refers to grain sown in a vineyard.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody draws his vine over somebody else’s grain it sanctifies and he is responsible for its alienation<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The neighbor’s grain has to be burned but the neighbor has a claim enforceable in court against the offender. This position of the anonymous Tanna is accepted as practice in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 15b" href="/Menachot.15b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 15b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Simeon say, nobody can sanctify anything that is not his.<br>Rebbi Yose said, it happened that someone sowed in his vineyard in the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">While the farmer remains the owner of the land in the Sabbatical year, the yield of that year is common property of all people. That means that while the vines remain private property (except for the grapes hanging on them), the grain never was the private property of the person sowing it.</i>. When this came before Rebbi Aqiba, he said nobody can sanctify anything that is not his.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>): “Do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard.” This refers to your own vineyard. From where about another person’s vineyard? The verse says, <i>kilaim</i>, no vineyard and no <i>kilaim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is slightly more clear in the version of <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 230 on the verse: “Do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard, lest the fulness of the seed you are sowing be sanctified together with the yield of the vineyard.” The <i>Sifry</i> reads: “I understand not only a fruit-bearing vineyard, from where a vineyard that is not fruit-bearing? The verse says ‘the vineyard’, any vineyard. I understand not only your own vineyard, from where other persons’ vineyard? The verse says ‘do not sow <i>kilaim</i>’, in any way.” R. S. Adani argues that in contrast to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>, where the direct object precedes the predicate, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>.<i>22:9</i></a> the predicate precedes the direct object and this is always taken as indicating that the prohibition stated at the beginning of the verse covers all situations the verse refers to. Hence, the operative word is not כרמך “your vineyard” but the last word of the sentence, הכרם “any vineyard.”</i> Rebbi Eleazar said, this is Rebbi Meïr’s, for Rebbi Meïr said that a Gentile cannot acquire real estate in the Land of Israel to free it from tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah makes no difference whether the neighbor is Jewish or Gentile, its author must hold that the laws of the Land are not abrogated by Gentile ownership.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it is the opinion of everybody, explain it by a Gentile who sowed his vineyard <i>kilaim</i> and a Jew bought it from him<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Even in the scenario envisaged by R. Eleazar, one cannot infer anything since the Gentile is not required to follow the obligations which in Jewish opinion are a lien on the Land. They are only activated in case a Jew buys the real estate from the Gentile; then the neighbor is a Jew and nobody holds that the obligations are not in force.</i>.
Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose, both of them say that it is Rebbi Meïr’s<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous Tanna of the Mishnah cannot be either R. Yose or R. Simeon; anonymous statements of the Mishnah are attributed to R. Meïr unless an argument can be given to the contrary.</i>. [An animal] which was worshipped: Some Tannaïm state that it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">An animal used for idolatrous practices is unfit as a sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.6.1">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 6:1</a>). The Mishnah states that such an animal is forbidden as a sacrifice but its meat may be eaten by private persons since usufruct of living things cannot become forbidden.</i> whether it is his own or somebody else’s; some Tannaïm state that his own is forbidden, somebody else’s is permitted. They wanted to say, the opinion that it is forbidden whether it is his own or somebody else’s is Rebbi Meïr’s and Rebbi Jehudah’s; the opinion that his own is forbidden, somebody else’s is permitted, is Rebbi Yose’s and Rebbi Simeon’s. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi La: It is everybody’s opinion. Just as you say there that a living thing is forbidden for Heaven even though it is permitted for the private person, so anything that is not yours may be forbidden for Heaven even though it is permitted for the private person<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Yose and R. Simeon do not disagree with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Temurah 6:1" href="/Mishnah_Temurah.6.1">Mishnah <i>Temurah</i> 6:1</a> that an animal used for idolatrous purposes (or for sodomy) is forbidden as sacrifice under all circumstances. The argument about sacrifices is irrelevant for the rules of <i>kilaim</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this is a fine imposed by Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous Tanna does not dispute that in principle nobody can sanctify anything that does not belong to him, parallel to the position of R. Yose in the preceding paragraph. The order to destroy the growth under the vine is purely rabbinical and intended to punish the mischievous vintner. In contrast to the Babli which always accepts R. Meïr’s decrees of fines, the Yerushalmi will accept only a fine imposed by a majority of the Synhedrion.</i>. Did he act on that, “if somebody draws his vine over somebody else’s grain …”? But what did that person do? When it grows, it grows according to his intention<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">What could be the reason to impose the fine? When a person draws a vine over somebody else’s grain, both the vine and the grain are permitted and legitimate. Since later he does not do anything further, of what could he be guilty? The answer is that the grain will continue to grow and that increase under the vine is a necessary consequence of the earlier action and covered by the responsibility for the prior action. In the language of the Babli, nobody can claim missing intent by stating that he wanted only to cut off his victim’s head, not to kill him. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 100b" href="/Bava_Kamma.100b">Babli (<i>Baba Qama</i> 100b</a>) extends the liability to the case when a stone fence between a vineyard and a grain field developed a hole and the owner after being apprised of the problem did nothing to repair it.</i>. One person showed his tetradrachma to Rebbi Eleazar who told him that it was good<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 100a" href="/Bava_Kamma.100a">Babli, <i>Baba Qama</i> 100a</a>, R. Simeon ben Laqish showed the coin to R. Eleazar and told him that he would hold him responsible if he gave the wrong appraisal.</i>. It turned out to be worthless. This case came before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who fined him according to the following: If somebody shows a denar to the money-changer and it turns out to be bad, [the money-changer] has to pay because he takes a fee<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement appears in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 59b" href="/Bava_Kamma.59b">Babli (<i>Baba Qama</i> 59b</a>). There, a distinction is made between a professional money-changer and an amateur, no mention of a difference between giving an opinion for a fee or for free. Some authors (Meïri and R. Asher ben Yeḥiel <i>ad loc</i>.) want to read the ruling of the Yerushalmi into the text of the Babli but their arguments are thoroughly refuted by R. Salomo ben Adrat, <i>ad loc</i>.</i>. Does Rebbi Eleazar take a fee? Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abuna: One who takes it into his hand has the status of one who takes a fee<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this argument is an echo of the rule that a single judge who is shown to be in error has to pay the money he erroneously took from one party if he actually handled the money himself (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:4:2-5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.4.2-5.4"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:4</a>, fol. 47a). The argument is not accepted.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun does not say so, but Rebbi Eleazar says that this fine is imposed by the rabbis; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish says the fine is imposed by Rebbi Meïr. When the case came before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, he asked Rebbi Eleazar, who imposed this fine? He [Rebbi Eleazar] said, the rabbis. He [Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish] answered him, go and pay<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish required R. Eleazar to pay not because the law was that he had to pay, but because R. Eleazar thought the law was that he had to pay. It follows that the Yerushalmi rejects the decrees of R. Meïr not supported by a majority of Sages.<br>In a similar case in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 99b" href="/Bava_Kamma.99b">Babli (<i>Baba Qama</i> 99b</a>), R. Ḥiyya paid up not because he had to, but because, in his opinion, a rabbinic authority is held to higher moral standards.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, everybody agrees that the grapes will be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">They belong to the offending party.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said to him, what makes forbidden is not forbidden and what is not forbidden will be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">If the vine is drawn in a state in which it does not expand any further, only the growth of the grain under it makes the configuration forbidden, as stated earlier. Since the active grain is not forbidden, how can the passive grapes be forbidden?</i>? They disagree if somebody draws his vine over somebody else’s grain. But if somebody draws a neighbor’s vine over his own grain, everybody agrees that what makes forbidden is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The grain of the guilty party.</i>. If somebody draws a neighbor’s vine over his neighbor’s grain we can understand from the following: “Rebbi Yose said, it happened that someone sowed in his vineyard in the Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">While the farmer remains the owner of the land in the Sabbatical year, the yield of that year is common property of all people. That means that while the vines remain private property (except for the grapes hanging on them), the grain never was the private property of the person sowing it.</i>. When this came before Rebbi Aqiba, he said nobody can sanctify anything that is not his.” This was given as answer<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The anonymous first Tanna will hold also in the case under discussion that vines and grain have to be burned and the guilty party charged for all expenses and loss; Rebbis Yose and Simeon hold that nothing has happened.</i>!

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The rapist<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">A robber who terrorizes the farmer and illegally uses his land.</i> who sowed in a vineyard and it was taken from him<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Law enforcement returned the use of the land to its owners.</i>, one cuts it even during a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The intermediate days of Passover or Tabernacles, when agricultural work is permitted only if its omission would cause great harm. In our case, one has to prevent the grain from growing in its owner’s possession to avoid sanctifying the vineyard.</i>. How much does he have to give to the workers? Up to one third<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Since usually agricultural work cannot be done on the intermediate days of a holiday, the workers will know that the farmer is in a bind and raise their wage demands. The Halakhah will discuss what is meant by “a third” for the computation of the upper limit of wages the farmer must be ready to pay. If the workers overcharge, he may do without them and cut the grain after the holiday for regular wages.</i>. More than that he cuts normally, even after the holiday. When is the property called the rapist’s? From the moment it is sunk<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">If the name of the original owners disappears; if local people start referring to the property by the name of the person in possession who now becomes the owner (explanation of R. Simson). If the owner runs away for fear of the rapist (explanation of Maimonides).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Abba bar Jacob in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: So is the Mishnah: One may harvest it even on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">One should not think that the Mishnah prescribes that the illegal grain must be harvested on a holiday if the field reverts to its rightful owner on a holiday. All that is stated is that one may harvest it because it looks badly (if the workers are not too expensive) even though nobody can sanctify anything that is not his and harvesting on the intermediate days of a holiday is forbidden (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sukkah 30b" href="/Tosafot_on_Sukkah.30b"><i>Tosaphot Sukkah</i> 30b</a>).</i>.
“How much does he have to give to the workers? Up to one third.” Rav Ḥuna and Rav Sheshet: One of them says, a third of the wages, the other says, a third of the value<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The value of the crop endangered by the rapist’s action.</i>.
“When is the property called the rapist’s? From the moment it is sunk<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">If the name of the original owners disappears; if local people start referring to the property by the name of the person in possession who now becomes the owner (explanation of R. Simson). If the owner runs away for fear of the rapist (explanation of Maimonides).</i>.” Rebbi Aḥa said: If the owners were forgotten but did not give up hope, the prohibition of the field is biblical<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah declares the property to be the rapist’s once the names of the original owners have been forgotten, he sows on his own and sanctifies the crop. But if the names of the original owners are still known, even if these have given up hope to recover the field through legal action, the rapist is not the owner and should not be able to sanctify anything that is not his. In that case, the prohibition has the status of a rabbinic fine.</i>. If the owners did give up hope but they were not forgotten, the prohibition of the field is rabbinic. But can real estate be robbed<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since real estate is documented, it always should be recoverable once law enforcement has been reestablished, cf. Peah Chapter 2, Note 132. In the opinion of the Babli, one may never legally give up hope on real estate, cf. Tosaphot Sukkah 30b. In Babylonia one never witnessed the prolonged abrogation of property rights by Jewish owners as in Palestine after the war of Bar Kokhba. </i>? Rebbi La said, even though real estate cannot be robbed, one can give up hope on real estate.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah mss. in the Maimonides tradition read יגרוד “shave them off” instead of יגדור “fence them off”. The hurricane did not completely break the vines; now parts of the still living vine lie over grain.</i> If a storm broke vines over grain, one should fix it immediately, but in an emergency it is permitted. Grain leaning under vines, and also vegetables, one turns back and it does not sanctify<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">As long as the roots are outside of the working space of a vine, if the plants turn towards the vine one has to turn them away. Only roots of plants can sanctify.</i>. When does grain start to sanctify? When it is a third grown<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">This may not be the intended meaning, cf. the Halakhah.</i>, and grapes once they have grown to the size of white beans. Completely dry grain and grapes completely ripe do not become sanctified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Following Ben Azai, one should shave them off. One found it stated: Rebbi Aqiba said, one should return them, Ben Azai said, one should shave them off<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This belongs to the second Mishnah, the case when a storm broke branches which now are spread over grain. R. Aqiba says one should adjust the broken branches and fasten them to the vine; Ben Azai said, one should cut them off.</i>.
“When does grain start to sanctify? When it is a third grown.” Some Tannaïm state: When it grew roots. He who says when it is a third grown supports Rebbi Joḥanan. He who says when it grew roots supports Rebbi Hoshaia<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Since real estate is documented, it always should be recoverable once law enforcement has been re-established, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.5.3"><i>Peah</i> Chapter 2, Note 132</a>. In the opinion of the Babli, one may never legally give up hope on real estate, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Sukkah 30b" href="/Tosafot_on_Sukkah.30b"><i>Tosaphot Sukkah</i> 30b</a>. In Babylonia one never witnessed the prolonged abrogation of property rights by Jewish owners as in Palestine after the war of Bar Kokhba.</i>.
“And grapes once they have grown to the size of white beans.” Rebbi Ḥananiah the son of Rebbi Hillel said, because it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>): “And the produce of the vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The verse defines what is sanctified. Since it is called “produce”, some kind of produce must be visible.</i>.” So is the Mishnah: “They do not become sanctified”<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the last sentence in the Mishnah, “completely dry grain and grapes completely ripe do not become sanctified.” The Mishnah is formulated in the passive: completely dry produce cannot itself become sanctified because it no longer grows but produce still growing in its vicinity can become sanctified by its presence (R. S. Adani.)</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A flower pot with a hole sanctifies in a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.6.1">Mishnah 8</a> in the separate Mishnah). A flower pot with a hole is part of the earth, Mishnah <i>Demay</i> 5:10.</i>, but one without a hole does not sanctify. Rebbi Simeon says, both are forbidden but neither one sanctifies<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The Halakhah will explain that R. Simeon does not recognize any difference between flower pots with or without holes, except that he treats food grown in a pot with a hole like food grown on a field with respect to the laws of ritual impurities.</i>. If a flower pot with a hole is temporarily put in a vineyard, if it grew by one twohundreth it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.5.1">Mishnah 5:6</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: “The only difference between a flower pot without a hole and one with a hole regards preparation for impurity.” That is for Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 95" href="/Shabbat.95">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 95a/b</a>, this is a statement explicitly attributed to R. Simeon.</i>, but for the rabbis there are others: A flower pot with a hole sanctifies in a vineyard, one without a hole does not. He who plucks from a flower pot with a hole is guilty, from one without a hole he is free from punishment<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 10:7. Harvesting on the Sabbath is a criminal offense but one cannot harvest plants that do not grow on the earth. Plucking a plant from a pot without a hole is forbidden but cannot be cause for criminal prosecution. The Mishnah states that R. Simeon excludes prosecution in both cases.</i>. A flower pot with a hole cannot prepare plants, one without a hole prepares<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Food can become impure only after the harvest and only after having been wetted, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.2"><i>Demay</i> Chapter 2, Note 141</a>. A plant in a pot with hole is a plant in the earth and nothing can make it prepared for impurity at this stage. A plant in a pot without hole is already harvested since it can be plucked on the Sabbath without fear of prosecution; if it is watered, it becomes subject to possible impurity.</i>. Rebbi Yose referred to it as anonymous statement, Rebbi Ḥanina quoted it in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: The Torah extended the purity of growing plants (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:37" href="/Leviticus.11.37"><i>Lev</i>. 11:37</a>): “If any of their cadavers falls on any sown seed apt to be sown, it is pure<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This explains why R. Simeon agrees with the rabbis that a flower pot with hole is immune from impurity and is not comparable to a pot with hole: The verse insists that anything sown in any way acceptable in agriculture is pure.</i>.”
It was stated: “The tithes from a flower pot without hole are of practice<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbinic practice, not biblical commandment. If symbolic heave from such a pot is mixed with profane food, it does not create <i>dema‘</i> (cf. <i>Demay</i> Chap. 4, Note 27) and if it is eaten in error by a non-Cohen, only 100%, not 125%, has to be returned (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:14" href="/Leviticus.22.14"><i>Lev</i>. 22:14</a>).</i>, its heave does not create <i>dema‘</i> and one does not owe a fifth for it.” Rebbi Yose asked: May one say “He Who created bread from the earth” for it<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">What is the appropriate benediction for bread made from flour from grain not grown “on the earth”?</i>? Rebbi Jonah asked: Is a gourd considered plucked that one may use it as the cover [of a <i>sukkah</i>]<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A hut for the feast of Tabernacles has to be covered by material “not subject to impurity and grown from the earth” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 1:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.1.4.1">Mishnah <i>Sukkah</i> 1:4</a>). As noted in the preceding paragraph, the majority opinion is that plants growing in a pot without a hole are subject to impurity; the question can be asked only according to R. Simeon.</i>? Rebbi Judah bar Pazi asked: If somebody planted five vines in five pots without holes and arranged them two parallel two and one as tail, can you say this is a vineyard? He may rearrange, does there exist a movable vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since the vines can be rearranged, they cannot be considered a vineyard. This is the only question that is answered.</i>?
Samuel said, put under any vine. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Mishnah deals within ten [hand-breadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This starts the discussion of the last sentence in the Mishnah, about a flower pot temporarily put into a vineyard. Samuel said there is a potential for sanctification only if the flower pot is directly under a vine. If the pot is moved from one vine to the other but all the time is under one of them, there is sanctification if the plants in the pot grew by one half of one percent.</i>.
If one put five flower pots without holes under one vine, one has the disagreement between Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:2:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.2.3">Halakhah 4, Notes 56</a> ff. The argument is not as simple as represented here: If the plants in the aggregate of five pots increase by 0.5% then at least in one pot the increase must be ≥ 0.5%. That pot will certainly be forbidden. The only question is whether the five are considered as a unit or not. In the latter case, the grapes will be forbidden but part of the grain will be permitted, still a paradoxical situation.</i>: what makes forbidden is not forbidden and what is not forbidden will be forbidden? But if a single pot was placed temporarily under five different vines, everybody agrees that what makes forbidden will be forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">This starts the discussion of the last sentence in the Mishnah, about a flower pot temporarily put into a vineyard. Samuel said there is a potential for sanctification only if the flower pot is directly under a vine. If the pot is moved from one vine to the other but all the time is under one of them, there is sanctification if the plants in the pot grew by one half of one percent.</i>.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Kilaim</i> in a vineyard are forbidden to be sown or kept and are forbidden for any use. <i>Kilaim</i> of grains are forbidden to be sown or kept but are permitted for eating and certainly for use. <i>Kilaim</i> of textiles are permitted for everything; they are only forbidden to be worn. <i>Kilaim</i> of animals may be raised and kept; they are only forbidden to be mated. <i>Kilaim</i> of animals are forbidden [to be mated] with one another<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Since a mule is part horse and part donkey, mating of two mules implies double mating of parts of horse with parts of donkey and is forbidden for human intervention.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “<i>Kilaim</i> in a vineyard are forbidden,” etc. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>): “Do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard.” Not only sowing, how about keeping? The verse says, vineyard and no <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 7:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.7.3.2">Chapter 7, Note 44</a>. The parallel to this argument is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim"><i>Sifra Qedošim</i> 2(18</a>) [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 2b" href="/Moed_Katan.2b">Babli <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 2b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 64a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.64a"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 64a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 21b" href="/Makkot.21b"><i>Makkot</i> 21b</a>]: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>): “ ‘Your field you should sow no <i>kilaim</i>.’ Not only sowing, how about keeping? The verse says, no <i>kilaim</i>, I told you only because of <i>kilaim</i>.” This argument covers grains, vegetables, and vines. However, the same verse also mentions <i>kilaim of</i> animals and textiles in similar language and these may be kept in the opinion of the Mishnah. It follows that a derivation based on the verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a> has to be rejected and only an argument parallel to that in Chapter 7 is acceptable.</i>. Does this follow Rebbi Aqiba, for Rebbi Aqiba said that he who keeps [<i>kilaim</i>] transgresses a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">“Prohibition” usually refers to a criminal offense. “Forbidden” refers to a moral obligation. Since the statement of R. Aqiba in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 1:15" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).1.15">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 1:15</a> deals with <i>kilaim</i> of seeds (grains and vegetables), he cannot imply that passively keeping <i>kilaim</i> is prosecutable as a criminal offense. However, in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 2b" href="/Moed_Katan.2b">Babli (<i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 2b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 64a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.64a"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 64a</a>), R. Aqiba is quoted in a <i>baraita</i> to the effect that keeping <i>kilaim</i> is a criminal offense. This forces Rashi and the other commentators to conclude that “keeping” here means “fencing in” or a similar activity. (Rashi to <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 2 is lost; the commentary is in <i>Avodah Zarah</i>.) The Yerushalmi has no such difficulty.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, it is the word of everybody. Everybody agrees that what is forbidden is forbidden if he did not preserve through an action, but if he preserved through an action he is whipped, as it is stated: He who covers <i>kilaim</i> is whipped.
From where that it is forbidden for any use? It is said here (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>) “lest you sanctify it” and it is said elsewhere (lest it become sanctified by it)<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">There is no such verse in the entire Hebrew Bible. It is the consensus of the commentators that it should read פן תנקש (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:30" href="/Deuteronomy.12.30"><i>Deut</i>. 12:30</a>) “lest you be caught”, speaking of idolatrous objects that must be destroyed and every use of which is explicitly forbidden. One applies the principle that words in the Pentateuch have one invariable meaning; if that meaning is spelled out at one place, it can be transferred to any other occurrence of the same word.</i>. Since “lest” there means it is forbidden for any use, “lest” here means it is forbidden for all use. Some want to say, it is said here “lest you sanctify it” and it is said at another place “there shall be no <i>qadeš</i> among the Israelites.” Since <i>qdš</i> there means forbidden for any use, so <i>qdš</i> here must mean forbidden for any use. Rebbi Huna said, his sex act is forbidden for all use<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>qadeš</i> is the Semitic male temple prostitute. In the Talmudim, the notion which had become obsolete at that time was extended to all sexual relations of persons who could not legally marry one another, such as the relation between a slave and a free woman, or of a free Jew and a slave girl or a Gentile. Since the woman could not hope to be married, she could have no gain from the sex act; any pleasure she might have is strictly forbidden.</i>. Some want to understand it from the following, as Rebbi Ḥanina said, “lest you sanctify it”, lest fire should be kindled<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The same argument is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 56b" href="/Kiddushin.56b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 56b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 115a" href="/Chullin.115a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 115a</a> in the name of Ḥizqiah. No Tannaitic source proposes to change the text from תקדש to תקד אש.</i>.”
It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:9" href="/Deuteronomy.22.9"><i>Deut</i>. 22:9</a>): “Do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard.” This teaches us that one is not guilty unless he sows two kinds in the vineyard, the words of Rebbi Josia<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew text לא תזרע כרמך כלאים is somewhat ambiguous. One may translate “do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard” or “do not sow your vineyard as <i>kilaim</i>.” R. Josia chooses the first possibility, R. Jonathan the second. There is no question that the verse of <i>Deut</i>. contains the rule of sanctification that does not apply to the situation of the verse in <i>Lev:</i>, the question is why the second verse does not refer to the first.</i>. Rebbi Jonathan said, even one kind. In the opinion of Rebbi Jonathan it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>) “in your field do not sow <i>kilaim</i>”, why does it say “do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard”? The colleagues say, to make it sinful even with one kind. In the opinion of Rebbi Josia, “in your field do not sow <i>kilaim</i>”; why does it say “do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard”? The colleagues say, as a warning. If he was warned about “your field”, he is whipped, if he was warned about “your vineyard”, he is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In talmudic legal theory, a verdict of guilty cannot be pronounced unless criminal intent was shown by the testimony of two witnesses. The standard way of proving intent is the testimony that the perpetrator was warned not to commit such and such an act because it is forbidden in the Torah and that therefore he committed the act in the knowledge of its illegality. The prohibition must be specified. In our case, since sowing something in a vineyard turns the vineyard into a field, either of the verses may be used. This almost impossible standard of proof is needed only in order to convict somebody under biblical law which knows only flogging and the death penalty; it does not have to be strictly adhered to for convictions under police powers for which the punishment is jailtime.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, to give another measure besides six hand-breadths; here it is four cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Conviction for sowing <i>kilaim</i> in an enclosed field (and single vines considered as fields) is possible only if a minimal distance of six hand-breadths was not observed; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:2-5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.2-5">Halakhah 1:9</a>. Four cubits is the minimum distance for an enclosed vineyard. The existence of two different verses is a precondition to the establishment of two different legal standards.</i>. What are we talking about<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">One tries to clarify the statement of R. Abun bar Ḥiyya. If the field is not enclosed, no minimum separation is required but only clear geometric shapes. If the field is enclosed either the enclosure passes through the vineyard, in which case there is no legal vineyard, or it encloses the vineyard, in which case the more stringent rules of the circumference apply (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.4.1.1">Mishnah 4:1</a>). The only possibility is that the field itself is the enclosure, that a spot was cleared inside the field containing a four-by-four square, to be used for planting an illegal vineyard.</i>? If he made a fence on the inside, the vineyard is abolished. If he made a fence on the outside, that is the circumference. But we deal with a field that was a grain field and he cleared four cubits in its midst. This agrees with what Rebbi Ḥanina said, nothing forbidden may form a separation to save. They were of the opinion that what is forbidden does form a separation to prohibit<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is explained in Chapter Four, Note 28.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Jonathan it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>): “In your field do not sow <i>kilaim</i>”, why does it say: “Do not sow <i>kilaim</i> in your vineyard”<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Abun bar Ḥiyya, why does R. Jonathan use the verse to declare that somebody can be convicted of a biblical crime by sowing one foreign kind in a vineyard since the verses are needed to establish two different standards? This question is not a repetition of the one asked at the start of this paragraph.</i>? For that which Rebbi Hila said, if you would say [the Torah] did not say it, what would you say<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse is needed first for the more stringent standard and then for the problem of sanctification, it is impossible to think that it should not have been written.</i>? Or following what Rebbi Zeïra said, “do not sow your vineyard <i>kilaim</i>,” creating your vineyard do not sow <i>kilaim</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">He takes the verse literally, “do not sow your vineyard as <i>kilaim</i>”, not, as we would think, that “do not sow” refers to a preexisting vineyard which usually is planted, not sown. The only transgression that can be punished by biblical standards, i. e., whipping, is creating a vineyard as <i>kilaim</i>, sowing while the planted vines take root.</i>
Rebbi Yudan from Kappadokia asked before Rebbi Yose: There they say that tree seeds are called seeds but here you say that tree seeds are not called seeds. He said to him: There the verse excluded them since usually people do not call them “seeds,” but here the verse added (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:37" href="/Leviticus.11.37"><i>Lev</i>. 11:37</a>) “any sown seed that may be sown.”<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The text is from Tractate <i>Peah</i>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:4:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.4.2-6">Halakhah 1:4</a>, and is explained there in Notes 252–253. “There” is here, Tractate <i>Kilaim</i>, “here” is in <i>Peah</i>. No tree seeds form <i>kilaim</i> with grain and vegetables except grape seeds.</i>
Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai: He who covers <i>kilaim</i> is whipped. Rebbi Joḥanan said to him: Is that not a Mishnah? “<i>Kilaim</i> in a vineyard.” How are <i>kilaim</i> possible by ploughing<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since a standard vineyard is planted with four cubits between vines and rows, there is no space for a plough which needs four cubits on each side. Hence, anything sown has to be sown on the unploughed earth and then covered; the verse calls covering seeds with earth “sowing”.</i>? It must be by covering! Rebbi Yannai praised him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 46:6" href="/Isaiah.46.6"><i>Is</i>. 46:6</a>:) “Those who pour out gold from the wallet, etc.” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 3:21" href="/Proverbs.3.21"><i>Prov</i>. 3:21</a>) “My son, they should not be removed from your eyes,” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 9:9" href="/Proverbs.9.9"><i>Prov</i>. 9:9</a>) “give to the wise that he shall become wiser,” let the wise listen<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In the verse: “inform the just”.</i> “that he increase in knowledge.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, after all these praises I can explain it following Rebbi Aqiba since Rebbi Aqiba said that he who keeps [<i>kilaim</i>] transgresses a prohibition. Rebbi Aqiba said only that he transgresses, did he say to whip? Here we are dealing with whipping. Further, from what we have stated “Sabbatical year<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Eleazar, everybody agrees that ploughing in the Sabbatical year is a criminal offense, while according to R. Joḥanan everybody agrees that ploughing in the Sabbatical year is not a criminal offense. There is no way to single out R. Aqiba’s opinion.</i>.” Can you say that the Sabbatical year follows Rebbi Aqiba? Explain the Sabbatical year following Rebbi Eleazar, as Rebbi Eleazar said, one whips because of ploughing in the Sabbatical. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one does not whip because of ploughing in the Sabbatical. What is the reason of Rebbi Eleazar? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:2" href="/Leviticus.25.2"><i>Lev</i>. 25:2</a>) “The Land should keep a Sabbath for the Eternal,” a general statement. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:4" href="/Leviticus.25.4"><i>Lev</i>. 25:4</a>) “Your field you should not sow, your vineyard you should not prune,” detail. Sowing and trimming were in the general statement, why were they detailed? To trap with them and to tell you that just as sowing and pruning are particular as agricultural work on the earth or on a tree, so everything that is agricultural work on the earth or on a tree [is forbidden]<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian version of this argument is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 3a" href="/Moed_Katan.3a">Babli <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 3a</a>, a tannaïtic version in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar" href="/Sifra,_Behar"><i>Sifra Behar</i> 1</a>. [Cf. Sanhedrin 7:5, reference from Addenda and Corrigenda by Guggenehimer] A comparison with the Babli should use only manuscripts or early editions; the current versions have been corrupted by editors, cf. <i>Diqduqe Sopherim ad loc</i>. The relevant verses are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:2-5" href="/Leviticus.25.2-5"><i>Lev</i>. 25:2–5</a>: “(2) Speak to the Children of Israel and tell them, when you shall come to the Land which I am giving you, the Land shall rest for a Sabbath of the Eternal. (3) Six years you shall sow your field, six years you shall prune your vineyard and eat its yield. (4) But in the Seventh year, the Land shall have a Sabbath of rest; do not sow your field, do not prune your vineyard. (5) Your harvest’s aftergrowth you shall not harvest, the grapes of your wild growth you shall not gather, a year of rest shall be for the Land.” One’s first impulse would be to consider verses (4) and (5) as a unit, following the rule known as כלל ופרט וכלל (the seventh rule of R. Ismael, <i>Sifra</i> introduction) that details flanked by general statements have to be taken as examples, rather than for themselves, and that all activities forbidden are those described by the intersection of the intensional definitions of the examples. This interpretation led the later editors of the Babli astray. The problem is that verse 4 by itself seems to have the structure כלל ופרט, a general statement followed by a particular one, which would imply that the particular statement is to be taken literally; nothing is covered by the general statement that is not covered by the particular one. This forces the Babli to conclude that even R. Eleazar agrees with the principle [anonymous in the Yerushalmi, attributed to R. Ilaï (Hila, La) in the Babli] that a positive general statement and a negative particular one cannot form a unit. {The inverse statement, that a negative general statement and a positive particular one must be considered as unrelated, is an anonymous remark in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 7a" href="/Yevamot.7a">Babli <i>Yebamot</i>7a</a>.}<br>If the first and the second parts of verse 4 have no direct connection, the question arises (in the formulation of <i><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar" href="/Sifra,_Behar">Sifra Behar</a></i>) whether one could think that all work is forbidden in the Seventh Year, including building roads and repairing aqueducts. The second part of the verse describes what is really forbidden. The second rule invoked here by R. Eleazar is <i>heqqēš</i>“trapping”, meaning that a rule exemplified by different cases applies to all cases subsumed under the intersection of the intensional definitions of the examples. Everybody agrees that a rule exemplified by three different examples only applies to these three and nothing else, and a rule exemplified by one example applies to everything similar unless restricted, e. g., by the use of a definite article. A rule exemplified by two examples is problematic; some think these are examples only if they appear at different places and some hold that for illustration of a general rule the examples must appear in the same verse. This is the basis of the following disagreement between R. Eleazar and R. Joḥanan. (For the meaning of all these rules, cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Logical Problems in Jewish Tradition</i>, in: Ph. Longworth, ed., <i>Confrontations with Judaism</i>, London 1966.)</i>. How does Rebbi Joḥanan deal with this? They are two things and two things that are specified from a general notion divide<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The examples are separate; they cannot be used in <i>heqqēš</i>.</i>. Does Rebbi Eleazar not hold that they divide? He holds that they do not divide but instruct. Does Rebbi Joḥanan not hold that they instruct? It is different here because the general statement is a positive commandment and the details are prohibitions; no positive commandment implies anything for a prohibition and no prohibition implies anything for a positive commandment.
According to R. Joḥanan it is obvious that one is permitted to dig in it<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The Sabbatical year. If ploughing is permitted, digging certainly is.</i> cisterns, ditches, and caves. According to R. Eleazar, may one dig in it cisterns, ditches, and caves? If the verses have no implication for prohibition they cannot have implications for permission<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The question is asked the wrong way: If R. Joḥanan asserts that two examples together do not prove anything, they cannot prove that something is permitted. On the other hand, R. Eleazar (following <i><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Behar" href="/Sifra,_Behar">Sifra Behar</a></i>) only prohibits work on the field and in the vineyard, certainly not digging ditches.</i>.
Rebbi Abba from Carthage said, the reason of Rebbi Joḥanan is (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:3" href="/Leviticus.25.3"><i>Lev</i>. 25:3</a>): “Six years you shall sow your field”, not in the Seventh, “six years you shall prune your vineyard,” not in the Seventh. Every prohibition derived from a positive commandment is a positive commandment; he transgresses a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of R. Abba from Carthage, R. Joḥanan only asserted that ploughing is not overstepping a formal prohibition. For him, ploughing is still forbidden but the prohibition is not one of criminal law since it is formulated in a positive way.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said, he transgresses a positive commandment. Rebbi Yasa said, there is not even a positive commandment! Why is it written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:2" href="/Leviticus.25.2"><i>Lev</i>. 25:2</a>): “the Land shall rest for a Sabbath of the Eternal?” For the prohibitions in it<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">He (R.
