Assi) sticks with the prior explanation that two simultaneous examples do not imply <i>heqqeš</i>; “the prohibitions in it” are the two prohibitions spelled out in the verse.</i>.
“I could think one would whip because of the additions.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This is a quote from a <i>baraita</i>; a similar one, also quoted in two separate pieces, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 3b" href="/Moed_Katan.3b">Babli <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 3b</a>. A <i>baraita</i> starting with “I could think that …” always ends with showing that it is not so. The “additions” are prohibitions not explicitly stated in the Pentateuch.</i>” Rebbi Joḥanan explains the <i>baraita</i>, I could think that one would whip because of ploughing in the Sabbatical. Rebbi Eleazar explains the <i>baraita</i>, I could think that one would whip because of the prohibition of the first two terms<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition to work after the harvest of the year preceding the Sabbatical, two different terms, one for fields and one for orchards (<i>Ševi‘it</i> 1:1,2:1). These regulations have purely rabbinical character; the Biblical prohibition extends only to a short time before New Year’s Day. R. Eleazar will agree that even transgression of the latter prohibition is not punishable in court but the expression “two terms” is inappropriate since it refers to rabbinical extensions.</i>. Some Tannaïm state (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:3" href="/Leviticus.25.3"><i>Lev</i>. 25:3</a>): “Six years you shall sow your field, six years you shall prune your vineyard.” Some Tannaïm state (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:4" href="/Leviticus.25.4"><i>Lev</i>. 25:4</a>) “Your field you should not sow, your vineyard you should not prune.” He who states: “Six years you shall sow your field, six years you shall prune your vineyard,” supports Rebbi Joḥanan. He who states: “Your field you should not sow, your vineyard you should not prune,” supports Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The implications of these verses for the positions of the authors have been explained in the previous paragraphs.</i>.
A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> is preserved in <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 70–71, on the verses (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12-13" href="/Deuteronomy.12-13"><i>Deut</i>. 12–13</a>): “<i>Guard yourself</i> not to present your burnt offerings at every place you see. Only at the place which the Eternal will choose from one of your tribes, there <i>you should present</i> your burnt offerings and there <i>you should perform</i> all that I am commanding you.” The first verse contains a prohibition, the second a positive commandment and because of this difference, the verses cannot be taken together in the way R. Eleazar interpreted a similar situation regarding the Sabbatical year. Hence, R. Eleazar seems to contradict tannaïtic attitudes. Since in our sources R. Eleazar’s interpretation of the verses <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:2-5" href="/Leviticus.25.2-5"><i>Lev</i>. 25:2–5</a> is found in <i>Sifra</i>, one may take this as an indication that <i>Sifra</i> was unknown to the compilers of the Yerushalmi, and that <i>Sifra</i> is a Babylonian compilation of tannaïtic material as is traditionally asserted.</i>. “ ‘Guard yourself’ means a prohibition. And it is written: There you should present, there you should perform.” Because that is written, otherwise I would have said it is a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:13" href="/Leviticus.25.13">Verses 13–14</a> show that sacrificing outside the precincts of the Tabernacle or the Temple is a criminal offense (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:13" href="/Leviticus.25.13">v. 13</a>) and a disregard of a positive injunction (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:14" href="/Leviticus.25.14">v. 14</a>). The two verses are parallel, not logically consecutive as R. Eleazar would require.</i>. But that you should not say as you say regarding the Sabbath that if somebody dug a hole, made a ditch, and dug to put in a pole, he is guilty only of one offense, so similarly, if somebody slaughtered, sprinkled the blood, and presented he should be guilty only of one offense<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">An argument is given that R. Eleazar could justify the different treatment of an apparently similar situation.<br>The Sabbath prohibitions are classified under 39 categories of “main works”. An action similar to one of the main works is called a derivative. For example, ploughing as a preparation for sowing and digging a hole for the planting of a tree are main work and derivative respectively. If somebody performed both a main work and one of its derivatives in error, he has to bring one sacrifice of atonement. But if he transgressed prohibitions belonging to two distinct categories, he has to bring two sacrifices. In the case of sacrifices outside the Temple, presenting the sacrifice on the altar is really the last action; it is preceded first by the slaughter of the sacrificial animal and second by the sprinkling of its blood on the walls of the altar. These actions are classified as “performing”. Since the order of the actions is inverted, first “presenting” then “acting”, it follows that they are distinct and that slaughter and sprinkling are not legally derivatives of presenting. {In the <i>Sifry</i>, the interpretation is that the prohibition covers only those actions that are simultaneously a criminal offense and a disregard of a positive injunction.}</i>. Therefore, it is necessary to say that he is guilty for each action separately.
Rebbi Zeïra, Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi in the name of Cahana<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Since Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi was among the older students of Rav, Cahana mentioned here cannot be the one who went to study under R. Joḥanan but must be an older Sage (Cahana I) who already was a recognized authority when Rav returned from Galilee to Babylonia.</i>: He who is planting on the Sabbath is guilty because of sowing<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Sowing is the category, planting a derivative.</i>. Rebbi Zeïra said, he who prunes is like one who plants. If he planted and pruned on the Sabbath, according to Cahana he is guilty on two counts, according to Rebbi Zeïra only on one count. Did not Rebbi Zeïra say the pruner is like the planter, did he say the planter is like the pruner<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Pruning is a subcategory of sowing concerning the Sabbath just as planting is, but planting is not like pruning for the Sabbatical year since planting belongs to sowing.</i>? All was included in the notion of sowing; pruning was singled out for particular stringency<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Following the argument made for the Sabbath, it would not have been necessary to have pruning singled out in the laws of the Sabbatical. Since it is obvious that for the Sabbatical, pruning is a separate offense, pruning can be a subcategory of sowing for the Sabbath only as a stringency, not a leniency.</i>. Because pruning was singled out for particular stringency you want to exempt it because of sowing? There is no difference, both according to Cahana and according to Rebbi Zeïra he is guilty on two counts.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Domestic animal with [different] domestic animal and wild with [different] wild animal, domesticated with wild and wild with domesticated animal, unclean with [different] unclean and clean with [different] clean animal, unclean with clean and clean with unclean animal, all these are [combinations] forbidden for ploughing, drawing, and leading<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The source of this Mishnah is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:10" href="/Deuteronomy.22.10"><i>Deut</i>. 22:10</a>: “Do not plough with an ox and a donkey together.” It is forbidden to take animals of two different species under a common yoke either for work (ploughing or drawing a cart) or to lead them. The Mishnah mss. of the Maimonides tradition start with: “When was this said”, referring to the end of the previous Mishnah that “Kilaim of animals may be raised and kept; they are only forbidden to be mated”; this is only for “domestic animal with domestic animal, wild with wild animal, domesticated with wild and wild with domesticated animal, unclean with unclean and clean with clean animal.” For Maimonides, the prohibition of ploughing, etc., refers only to combinations of unclean (i. e. animals that may not be eaten) and clean (animals that may be eaten) animals. The text of the Halakhah gives some support for such a reading since it starts with a discussion of mating different species.</i>.<br>The driver absorbs the forty<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">This Mishnah deals with a cart (Greek κάρρον, τό, “car, cart”; cf. κάρρος, ὁ, Latin <i>carrus, carrum</i>, “two-wheeled cart”) drawn by two animals of different species. “Absorbing the forty” means being punished by 39 lashes.</i> and the one sitting in the cart absorbs the forty. Rebbi Meïr frees him from prosecution<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is supported by <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 231, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 8b" href="/Bava_Metzia.8b">Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 8b</a>, against the opinion of Samuel who exchanges the positions of R. Meïr and the rabbis.</i>. A third animal tied to their harness is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">If a cart is drawn by two animals of the same species and a third animal is tied not to the cart but to the harness of the other two, it is forbidden but not criminally prosecutable. {According to Maimonides, a <i>qarōn</i> is not a cart but a train of two carts, from Arabic קרן “to bind two things together”. If one sits in the first of two carts one is criminally liable; if a third cart is added at the end one is forbidden to sit there.}</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Domestic animal with domestic animal, etc.” I might think one cannot put a male next to a female nor a female next to a male; the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>) “do not cause your domestic animals to copulate”. You are only forbidden to cause to copulate but you may put a male next to a female or a female next to a male. But what did he actually do<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In order to commit a crime, the perpetrator has to perform a forbidden action. But if the male animal acts by itself, is not put on the female by a human, nothing has been perpetrated. {Explanation by R. Eliahu Fulda, following the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 91a" href="/Bava_Metzia.91a">Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 91a</a>. R. Moses Margalit and R. Eliahu Wilna explain: What is the crime committed by a person who causes animals to copulate by bringing the male bodily onto the female? The answer is that while ejaculation by the male is an automatic reaction, it fulfills the expectations of the human and therefore is induced by the human’s action.}</i>? When it ejaculates it does so by itself.
<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 5:6" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).5.6">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 5:6</a>. The Tosephta rejects Issi’s argument since it is written about David that he did all Eternal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 15:5" href="/I_Kings.15.5"><i>1K</i>. 15:5</a>).</i> Issi ben Aqabia<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the last generation, student of R. Eleazar ben Shamua and R. Yose, who appears also as Issi ben Gur Arieh, Issi ben Jehudah, Issi ben Gamliel, Joseph the Babylonian, Joseph from Hutsal, and maybe Rav Assi.</i> says: It is forbidden to ride on a mule by an argument of analogy. If garments which you may wear one upon the other are forbidden in mixture<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">It might be permitted to wear a woolen coat over a linen garment but it is forbidden to wear any garment containing both wool and linen; cf. Chapter Nine, end of Halakhah 7.</i>, you are certainly forbidden to ride on [a mixture of] animals you may not lead together. But is it not written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 13:29" href="/II_Samuel.13.29"><i>2Sam</i>. 13:29</a>): “Each one rode on his mule and they fled”? One does not learn from a king’s family<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">They will not listen to religious authority.</i>. But is it not written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 1:33" href="/I_Kings.1.33"><i>1K</i>. 1:33</a>): “Let my son Solomon ride on my she-mule”? That was a creature from Creation<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A wild animal, spontaneous offspring of a wild horse and a wild ass.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A student of R. Yose ben Ḥanina and teacher of Rebbis Jeremiah, Jonah, Yose.</i> said in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina, he who leads <i>kilaim</i> by voice will be whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 90b" href="/Bava_Metzia.90b">Babli, <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 90b</a>, this is a disagreement between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish. The argument there is only whether speaking is considered an action, not as in the Yerushalmi that the yoking together of two animals of different species prepares the transgression.</i>. We have stated so: “If he led it, if he made it draw, if he called it and it came, he is responsible to pay like a borrower.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">If a person takes an animal which is not his in any of the ways indicated, he becomes responsible for all damages incurred as if he had borrowed the animal, until he formally returns it to its owner. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 22b" href="/Kiddushin.22b">Babli (<i>Qiddušin</i> 22b</a>) and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kiddushin (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Kiddushin_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta (<i>Qiddušin</i> 1:7</a>), a parallel statement is given referring to acquisition of movables. In talmudic theory, payment alone does not transfer property; the property bought must be acquired by actually taking possession of at least some of it. If somebody bought a herd of cattle and then called one of them and it came, he has acquired the herd.</i>” But did not Samuel state: “If it was standing outside the Sabbath domain, he called it and it came after him, he is free.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If his cattle was standing outside the domain he could walk in on the Sabbath (2000 cubits from the edge of his town) and he called it back, he has not infringed on the laws of the Sabbath since he did not actually step outside his domain. Hence, calling cannot be the same as actually leading.</i>” There it comes of its own volition, here it is forced to walk<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since leading two animals of different species is forbidden only if they are somehow bound together, which restricts the freedom of each animal to walk according to its own volition and makes the human the overpowering influence. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 90b" href="/Bava_Metzia.90b">Babli (<i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 90b</a>) seems to reject this argument by noting that if somebody bound two animals together and then another person came and drove them, or if somebody muzzled the mouth of an ox far away from grain and another person then led it to thresh grain, only the second person is guilty in either case. This rule is accepted in Yerushalmi <i>Terumot</i> 9:4 (fol. 46d); it is considered irrelevant as argument here.</i> But did we not state: “If he clapped before his [a slave’s] eye and blinded it, before his [a slave’s] ear and made it deaf, he [the slave] does not attain freedom.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A slave gains his freedom if he is severely injured by his master (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:26-27" href="/Exodus.21.26-27"><i>Ex</i>. 21:26–27</a>). If the master hits the slave on his body, the slave goes free; near the body only if the slave could not move away. Similarly, for <i>kilaim</i> the rancher is responsible only if he restricts the freedom of movement of his animals.<br>The parallel discussion is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 24b" href="/Kiddushin.24b">Babli <i>Qiddušin</i> 24b</a>.</i>” Rebbi Eliezer ben Rebbi Yose said before Rebbi Yose, there is a difference because he [the slave] could have fled. Know that it is so because we have stated: “If he grabbed him he is guilty.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In the Venice print and Leyden ms. this is still part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.2.2-4">Halakhah 2</a>.</i>: He who sits in a cart, the rabbis say he is a weight, Rebbi Meïr says, he is no weight<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The rabbis assert that the animals have to work harder because of the passenger.</i>.
Originally there were only two as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 41:43" href="/Genesis.41.43"><i>Gen</i>. 41:43</a>): “He made him ride in his two-horse carriage.” Pharao came and made them three, as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 14:7" href="/Exodus.14.7"><i>Ex</i>. 14:7</a>): “three-horse over everything.” The evil kingdom<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A standard name for the Roman government.</i> arose and made it four.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not tie a horse to the sides or the back of a cart, or a Libyan donkey to camels. Rebbi Jehudah says, all animals born from a horse are permitted one with the other even if their father was a donkey, all animals born from a donkey are permitted one with the other even if their father was a horse, but those born from a horse are forbidden with those from a donkey.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">In the Venice text, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:2:5-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.2.5-6">Halakhah 3</a>.</i>: “One does not tie a horse to the sides or the back of a cart.” It is stated<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In both <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 231, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 5:4</a>, it is stated that R. Meïr permits it; in his opinion it is not a non-prosecutable misdemeanor but is unquestionably permitted. The reason is stated in <i>Sifry</i>: Since it says, “do not plough with ox and donkey <i>together</i>”, any use that is not together is permitted.</i>: Rebbi Meïr frees from prosecution. If it supported, whether in ascent or in descent, everybody agrees that it is forbidden. Rebbi Joḥanan said, because either one carries the laziness of the other<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since the ox before the cart and the horse tied to the cart do not walk with the same speed, they both influence the movement of the cart and are “together”. In antiquity, horses were used for riding and drawing chariots but never civilian carts.</i>. Rav says, because the rope is carried between them.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he bound them by their hair<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Is this permitted or forbidden? The question is not formulated clearly.</i>? Where do we hold? In case he bound them together by their hair<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, they cannot draw anything.</i>, should we say that it is forbidden to let one animal ride on another? If he tied them by a rope, we say because they carry the rope between them. Rebbi Jonah said, Rav and Rebbi Joḥanan disagree, Rebbi Joḥanan said, because either one carries the laziness of the other. Rav says, because they carry the rope between them. What is the difference? Both according to Rav as also according to Rebbi Joḥanan, if he bound them by their hair<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If no rope is used, the argument of Rav does not apply. Since the animals will not be able to draw if the cart is attached to their hair, the argument of R. Joḥanan does not apply.</i> he is not punishable!
<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel text is in <i>Šabbat</i> 5:1. A very detailed study of the first six sentences here and in <i>Šabbat</i> was done by J. N. Epstein, מבוא לנוסח המשנה <sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup>, Jerusalem 1964, pp. 97–99, where all variant readings of the names here, in <i>Šabbat</i>, and <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> are collected and commented on. The vocalization of ליבדקס is from the Kaufmann ms. of the Mishnah.</i>“And not a Libyan<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Perhaps the ending דקס refers to Latin <i>equus</i> “horse”, “Libyan animal of the horse family” (E. G.).</i> donkey.” Some Tannaïm state: “Not a <i>nybrqvs</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">An unexplained word that was unknown even to the rabbis of the Yerushalmi. The Arukh reads ניבדקוס, explainable by a change of liquids, <i>l - n</i>. Perhaps also a composite of <i>equus</i>, “Nubian animal of the horse family” (E. G.).</i>.” He who says <i>libdĕqēs</i> on the name of Lybian and (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 11:43" href="/Daniel.11.43"><i>Dan</i>. 11:43</a>) “Lybians and Nubians in His train.” He who says <i>nybrqvs ‘bhṭs</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel in <i>Šabbat</i> reads אמבטס ἀμβάτης, poetic for ἀναβάτης “stallion”. J. N. Epstein points out that because of the nasal pronunciation of מ in Galilean speech, an <i>m</i> can easily be inserted or disappear, so that אמבטס and אב(ה)טס are really one and the same and the text in <i>Kilaim</i> is not necessarily a corruption of the text in <i>Šabbat</i>.</i>, What is <i>‘bḥṭs</i>? A mounting donkey. Rebbi Jonah: Rav Hoshaia<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Amora of the third generation, Babylonian student of Rav Jehudah and Rav Huna, who went to Galilee with his brother and became prominent in the academies of the successors of R. Joḥanan. The two brothers earned their livelihood as shoemakers; their main customers were prostitutes who used to swear by the lives of these holy rabbis who never even looked at them.</i> asked: Do proselytes from Libya have to wait three generations<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:8-9" href="/Deuteronomy.23.8-9"><i>Deut</i>. 23:8–9</a> prescribes that an Egyptian proselyte and his direct offspring may not marry anyone born to Jewish parents. {The discussion is purely theoretical since one holds that the political upheavals already from the time of Sanherib did displace all peoples originally mentioned in the Pentateuch, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yadayim 4:4" href="/Mishnah_Yadayim.4.4">Mishnah <i>Yadayim</i> 4:4</a>; cf. <i>Šulḥan ‘Arukh Even Ha’ezer</i> 4:10.}</i>? Rebbi Jonah from Bostra<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Amora of the fifth generation, mentioned in connection with R. Mana and Rebbi Ḥuna.</i> said, from what we see that they call a green Egyptian bean Libyan<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A bean is لوبيا <i>lubia</i> in Arabic and Farsi; according to N. Brüll, the name is originally Coptic.</i> but a dry one Egyptian, that means a proselyte from Libya has to wait three generations, it means that Libya is identical with Egypt.
Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Hoshaiah: Practice follows the student<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The “student” here is Rebbi Jehudah. It is implied that the opinion of the “Sages”, that all kinds of mules are the same and may be mated with one another, is the opinion of R. Jehudah’s teacher R. Aqiba. The ruling is also reported in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 111b" href="/Ketubot.111b">Babli (<i>Ketubot</i>111b</a>). In the generally accepted opinion of Maimonides, based on the Babli but which can be supported by the position of the “Sages” here, R. Jehudah is restrictive: Only mules of the same kind of mother can be mated, not different kinds of mules nor mules with the pure species of their mother (<i>Kilaim</i> 9:6–7). R. Jehudah holds that among animals the father counts; the Sages take the opposite position.</i>. The words of the Sages are that all kinds of mules are the same.
These<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">An almost identical text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 8:5:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.8.5.6"><i>Berakhot</i> 8:6, Notes 120</a> ff. Since practice follows R. Jehudah, one has to have a practical criterion of how to distinguish between the two kinds of mule that cannot be mated.</i> are its signs: Rebbi Jonah says, if it has small ears, its mother is a horse and its father a donkey; if it has large ears its mother is a donkey and its father a horse. Rebbi Mana ordered those of the patriarch’s estate: If you want to buy a mule, you should buy one with small ears whose mother is a horse and whose father is a donkey.
The white is from the male, from him are brain, bones, and sinews. The red is from the female, from her are skin, flesh, and blood. But breath, life force, and soul are from the Holy One, may He be praised; all three are partners in him<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">“Him” is the child. A much more detailed explanation of the talmudic theory of heredity is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 31a" href="/Niddah.31a">Babli <i>Niddah</i> 31a</a>. Since the text here deals with animals, the paragraph is somewhat out of place since animals are said not to have נשמה “soul” but only נפש “the animal force of life.”</i>.
Rebbi Abba, Rav Jehudah in the name of Rav: The Sages agree with Rebbi Jehudah in the case of a horse from a horse from a donkey with a donkey from a donkey from a horse<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The Sages hold that any two animals half horse and half donkey can be mated, but not an animal ⅔ horse with an animal ⅔ donkey.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai, Rebbi Zeïra in the name of Rebbi Issy<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The otherwise unknown איסת must be איסי (Issy, Assi, Yasa), one of the known teachers of R. Zeïra.</i>: descendants of she-goats and ewes are forbidden one with the other. If it were not so, why do we say “a goat from a goat from a ewe with a ewe from a ewe from a goat.”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Perotiot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">All commentaries depending on gaonic sources declare these to be mules about which it is unknown whether their mother was a horse or a donkey and whose ears do not allow to determine the species of their mother. This implies that the opinion of R. Jehudah in the preceding Mishnah is accepted here as opinion of the anonymous majority and, therefore, practice to be followed. This would make superfluous the statement of R. Isaac bar Naḥman in the preceding Halakhah. Now in Arabic, פֻדַט فُرَط is “a horse which outruns all others” (from the root فرط “to surpass”). It might be that the entire sentence speaks of horses and that thoroughbreds are considered a separate species created by what today would be called genetic engineering, while wild horses are the same as a standard domesticated horse. {Cf. also Medieval Latin <i>paraveredus</i>,<i>parifredus</i> “horse” (E. G.).}</i> are forbidden and wild horses<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Definition of Maimonides.</i> are permitted. A great ape<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides explains by Arabic נסנאס, نَسناَس “a fabled animal having only one foot,” in accordance with the Halakhah here, but in his <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yadayim 1:5" href="/Mishnah_Yadayim.1.5">Commentary to Mishnah <i>Yadayim</i> 1:5</a> he notes that an ape (قِرد) in Egypt is called נסנאס (in modern Farsi, it means Orang Utan). Rebbi Yose holds in <i>Yadaim</i> 1:5 that a great ape is not a human but the Sages there disagree.</i> is a wild animal; Rebbi Yose says they cause impurity in a tent like a human<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Only human corpses cause impurity in a tent, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:8:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.8.7">Chapter 2, Note 180</a>.</i>. The hedgehog<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical קִפֹּד. The Arabic quoted by Maimonides, קנפד, means either “rat” or “hedgehog.”</i> and a squirrel mole<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">חולדה, the Biblical חֹלֶד, means “mole” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 1:5:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.1.5.2-3"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 1:5</a>, fol. 80c). In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:29" href="/Leviticus.11.29"><i>Lev</i>. 11:29</a>, the mole is classified with the “crawling things”. The problem is with the qualifier סנאי (or סניי), which the older commentaries derive from סנה “small bush, tree producing fruit every second year”, but it is difficult to see a mole living in a bush. Maimonides describes the animal as “a kind of fox looking like a mole”. If this animal is classified as “wild animal”, its carcass or parts of it will make impure if touched or carried (even without touching) but only if there is at least the volume of an average olive; if it is classified as a “crawling thing” it does not make impure by being carried, but by touch it makes impure already by the volume of a small lentil.</i> are wild animals. About the squirrel mole, Rebbi Yose says that the House of Shammai say it defiles in the amount of the volume of an olive in carrying, but in the volume of a lentil by touch.<br>The bison is a domesticated animal; Rebbi Yose says a wild animal<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">For the Sages one is permitted to mate bison and cattle and to plough with an ox and a bison yoked together; for R. Yose it is forbidden.</i>. The dog is a wild animal; Rebbi Meïr says a domesticated animal. The pig is a domesticated animal, the wild donkey a wild animal. Elephant and monkey are wild animals. A human is permitted to plough and draw with all of them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “<i>Perotiot</i> are forbidden and wild horses are permitted.” Rebbi Ammi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>Ramak</i> is without χαλινός “bit”. As one says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 8:10" href="/Esther.8.10"><i>Est</i>. 8:10</a>): “the sons of wild horses”<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">While in Arabic, רמך (<i>rimāk</i>,<i>armāk</i>) is a mare, R. Ammi insists that in the verse the masculine ending is appropriate and the horses used by the Persian imperial mail were born to domesticated mares but sired by wild (unbridled) horses.</i>.
“ ‘Field Stones’<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Venice print; the Leyden ms. has two dots over the ב. But also in <i>Sifra Šemini</i> 4|6|5 it is stated on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:27" href="/Leviticus.11.27"><i>Lev</i>. 11:27</a>: “ ‘All that walks on its soles’ that is the monkey, ‘all that walks’ includes hedgehog and squirrel mole, field stones and seals.” אבני השדה for אדני השדה refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 5:23" href="/Job.5.23"><i>Job</i> 5:23</a>: “Your covenant is with ‘field stones’.”</i> is an animal.” Yassi from Arke: It is a mountain man and lives from its navel. If its umbilical cord is cut, it cannot live. Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: The reason of Rebbi Yose (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:16" href="/Numbers.19.16"><i>Num</i>. 19:16</a>): “All that touches [a slain person] on the surface of the field”, when it grows on the field.
<i>Yaror</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 5:8" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).5.8">Tosephta Kilaim 5:8</a> ירורין. From the context, the name must refer to a walking bird, with a voice resembling that of a jackal. The word was used for birds both in Symmachos and the Peshitto, cf. R. S. Lieberman, תוספתא כפשוטה זרעים (New York 1956) p. 652. The determinations here refer to the rules of defilement by dead animals. The rule for the snake is given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:42" href="/Leviticus.11.42"><i>Lev</i>. 11:42</a> which states that “all that walk on their bellies and all that walk on four legs” follow the same rules.</i> and ostrich are like birds in all respects. The snake is like a wild animal.
Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: There are six unresolved doubts. The caper-bush among trees following the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 5:5:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.5.5.2-4">Halakhah 5:6</a>, the House of Shammai make the capeṛbush subject to the restrictions both of vegetables and of trees (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.4.5">Note 92</a>).</i>, clay vessels like potash vessels following the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta <i>Kelim Baba Qama</i> 2:1: “Vessels made of potash, the House of Shammai say they may become impure from the inside and their airspace like clay vessels and from the outside like metal vessels.” Clay vessels cannot become impure if an impurity touches their outside, cf. <i>Berakhot</i> Chapter Eight, Note 51.</i>, the squirrel mole among the crawling things following the House of Shammai<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah here.</i>; the Egyptian bean among seed-vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 3:2:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.3.2.2-4">Halakhah 3:2</a>.</i>, the hermaphrodite among humans<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Whether he is treated as a male or a female. This is detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bikkurim (Lieberman) 2" href="/Tosefta_Bikkurim_(Lieberman).2">Tosephta <i>Bikkurim</i> 2</a>.</i>, the <i>koi</i> among wild animals according to everybody<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta Bikkurim 2:1. Hulin 80a: "Koi is the wild goat; some people say it comes from a male goat and a female deer. Rebbi Yose said, koi is a separate species and the Sages were unable to decide whether it follows the rules of domesticated animals or those of wild animals." The difference between domesticated and wild kasher animals is explained in Chapter 1, Note 104.</i>. Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba said, also the <i>ṭrīqsīn</i> cubit<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The space in the Temple between the holy and the holiest of holies parts. The problem is whether it is part of the Temple hall or of the holiest of holies.</i>. What is the <i>ṭrīqsīn</i> cubit? Rebbi Jonah from Bostra said, confusion<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Jastrow’s explanation is Greek τάραξιν, accusative of τάραξις “confusion”. [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 4:7" href="/Mishnah_Middot.4.7">Maimonides (Commentary to <i>Middot</i> 4:7</a>) declares the word to be the name of the dividing wall in the first Temple.] A Gaonic commentary to <i>Yoma</i> explains טרקסין by an Arabic word בראסתג of unknown meaning. <i>Arukh</i> and Rashi declare טרקסין (spelling of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Middot 4:7" href="/Mishnah_Middot.4.7">Mishnah <i>Middot</i> 4:7</a>) to be a Greek word and “inside-outside” its meaning. Compare Latin <i>intro</i>, adv. “inside” and <i>extra</i>, adv. “outside” for composite <i>inTREXtra</i> (E. G.).</i>, “what is inside-outside?” Rebbi Yose said, since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 6:17" href="/I_Kings.6.17"><i>1K</i>. 6:17</a>) “forty cubits was the House, that is the inner Temple,” it means it is counted inside<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since no provision is made for the wall, the wall is not part of the main part of the main room of the Temple but must be under the rules of the holiest of holies.</i>. Rebbi Mana said to him, but it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 3:8" href="/II_Chronicles.3.8"><i>2Chr</i>. 3:8</a>) “He made the holiest of holies twenty cubits long and ten wide,<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">That verse is badly misquoted: ויעש את בית קדשי הקדשים ארכו על פני רחב הבית אמות עשרים ורחבו אמות עשרים. It makes no difference for the objection.</i>” that means it is counted outside.
“The bison is a domesticated animal.” The rabbis say it was from here and escaped there<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">“Here” is agricultural society, “there” is the wild. The rabbis consider bison as cattle that became wild.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, its roots were from there. Hence an ox and a bison are not <i>kilaim</i>, not following Rebbi Yose, since Rebbi Yose says it is <i>kilaim</i>. But those who translate “wild oxen and antelopes” follow Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Targum Pseudo-Jonathan translates ודישן ותאו in the list of wild animals of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:5" href="/Deuteronomy.14.5"><i>Deut</i>. 14:5</a> as וְרִימְנִין וְתוֹרֵי בַּר.</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rebbi Samuel: Goose and desert goose are <i>kilaim</i> one with the other. About goose and sea goose, Rebbi Judah ben Pazi stated in the name of Bar Dalaiah and we do not know what he stated. It is a conclusion <i>a minori ad majus</i>: If you say they are forbidden when both of them are on dry land, so much more if one is on dry land and one at sea.
“A person is permitted to plough and draw with all of them.” It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:10" href="/Deuteronomy.22.10"><i>Deut</i>. 22:10</a>): “Do not plough with an ox and a donkey together.” (<i>Sifri Deut</i>. 231) You may not plough with an ox and a donkey but you may plough with an ox and a human, a donkey with a human.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Only wool and linen are forbidden as <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">This chapter deals with <i>ša‘aṭnēz</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:11" href="/Deuteronomy.22.11"><i>Deut</i>. 22:11</a>) defined as mixing of linen and wool.</i>. Only wool and linen can become impure by mold disease<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:47-59" href="/Leviticus.13.47-59"><i>Lev</i>. 13:47–59</a>. Wool and linen are the only textiles mentioned there but leather is under the same rules.</i>. Only wool and linen are worn by priests for their service in the temple<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The materials decribed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:5" href="/Exodus.28.5"><i>Ex</i>. 28:5</a> are interpreted to be gold, wool died dark blue, purple, crimson, and fine linen. Gold was used only for the garments of the High Priest.</i>. If one mixed camel wool and sheep wool, if most is camel wool it is permitted, if most is sheep wool it is forbidden, half and half is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the prohibition is biblical, in case of doubt the prohibition prevails.</i>. The same applies if hemp<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>cannabis</i>, also <i>cannabum;</i> Greek κάνναβις, κάνναβος.</i> and linen are mixed.<br>Raw silk and <i>kalak</i> silk are not subject to <i>kilaim</i> but are forbidden because of the bad impression<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Since raw silk looks like flax and <i>kalak</i> silk (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.1.8">Note 33</a>) like wool.</i>. Mattresses and pillows are not subject to <i>kilaim</i> but one’s skin should not touch them. There is no provisional <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">One may not wear <i>kilaim</i> even temporarily as explained in the next sentence.</i>. One may not wear <i>kilaim</i> over ten other garments<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In this case one does not need the <i>kilaim</i> garment as such and derives no bodily comfort from it. Since customs officials are dishonest and charge more than the legal amounts, one does not have to be honest with them, cf. <i>Demay</i> 2:3.</i>, not even to trick the customs.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Only [wool and linen] are forbidden as <i>kilaim</i>”. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:11" href="/Deuteronomy.22.11"><i>Deut</i>. 22:11</a>): “Do not wear <i>ša‘aṭnez</i>, wool and linen together.”<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 232, the text is somewhat more complete: “I would say one is only forbidden to wear it; from where do we know that one may not cover oneself with it (in a blanket)?”</i> I would say one is only forbidden to wear it; the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>): “It shall not come on you.” If it shall not come on you I would say one may not carry a chest on his back<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">A peddler’s chest full of textiles to peddle to Gentiles.</i>; the verse says “do not wear.” Since a garment is specifically useful to the body, [included are] only things useful to the body. Why was it said that “it shall not come on you?” Rebbi Niḥa bar Sava<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A Galilean Amora of the fifth generation, student of R. Jonah. Since R. Zeïra was R. Joḥanan’s student, it is clear that the second name in the list must be “R. Jonah”. In the Babylonian tradition, this is a Tannaïtic statement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 5:13" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).5.13">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 5:13</a>).</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Zeïra, if there was a large piece of cloth, in a part of which was <i>kilaim</i> and that part was lying on the ground, one cannot cover himself with the other part. I would say, it includes even sea flax<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Shell silk, also called “byssus”, from threads, excreted by shells under water, which harden when exposed to air, used already in antiquity.</i>, even hemp; the verse says “wool and linen”; just as wool cannot have an epithet so nothing else can have an epithet. From where that [wool] has no epithet? Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Kings 3:4" href="/II_Kings.3.4"><i>2K</i>. 3:4</a>) “Mesha‘, the king of Moab, was a <i>nôqēd</i>;” what is a <i>nôqēd</i>? A shepherd! “He delivered to the king of Israel 100’000 lambs and 100’000 wool rams.” Only ram’s wool is called “wool”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">All others carry the name of the animal from which they come as an epithet.</i>.
“Only wool and linen can become impure by mold disease,” for it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:47" href="/Leviticus.13.47"><i>Lev</i>. 13:47</a>) “on woolen cloth or on linen cloth.” I could think they can become impure whether dyed or natural; the verse says “woolen cloth or linen cloth.” Just as linen is in its natural state so wool must also be in its natural state<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Braita d'Rabbi Yishmael 13" href="/Sifra,_Braita_d'Rabbi_Yishmael.13"><i>Sifra Paršat Nega‘im</i> 13(3–4</a>). It seems that the name of פשתים was used only for undyed linen cloth; dyed cloth had other names.</i>. I might exclude what was dyed by humans but not what was dyed by nature; the verse says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:48" href="/Leviticus.13.48"><i>Lev</i>. 13:48</a>): “For <i>the</i> linen and for <i>the</i> wool<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The implied definite article restricts this to what is certainly called linen and woolen cloth, not, תכלת, ארגמן, תולעת שני, etc. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 17a" href="/Bekhorot.17a">Babli (<i>Bekhorot</i> 17a</a>) quotes a <i>baraita</i> using the same verse to exclude wool from <i>kilaim</i> of sheep and goats.</i>”, just as linen is white so wool has to be white.
We have stated two rules and they do not resemble one another. We have stated: Only wool and linen are forbidden as <i>kilaim</i>, whether dyed or white. We have stated: Only wool and linen can become impure by mold disease, but only white. Rebbi Jonah from Bostra asked before Rebbi Mana: There, you say “whether dyed or white”, but here you say “but only white”? He said to him, there is a difference because the verse repeated twice “wool, wool”; just as linen is in its natural state so also wool must be in its natural state.
<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the priestly garments mentioned in the Mishnah. A parallel is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 71b" href="/Yoma.71b">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 71b</a>.</i> Rebbi Zeïra said: It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:4" href="/Leviticus.16.4"><i>Lev</i>. 16:4</a>) “He shall wear a garment of holy <i>bad</i>,” growing singly<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since בד can mean (1) single strand, connected with Hebrew בדד, Arabic بدّ “to separate”, or (2) linen cloth. It is not explicit in the text that the garments of the High priest for the service of the Day of Atonement have to be of white linen. The meaning (2) of בד is established in this paragraph. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Acharei Mot" href="/Sifra,_Acharei_Mot"><i>Sifra Aḥare</i> 1(3</a>) בד is taken to mean “double cloth”.</i>. But wool also grows in single strands<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing here proves that בד means linen cloth, not woolen cloth; this shows that the modern acceptation of בד “cloth (of any kind)” was current in the times of the Yerushalmi.</i>. It is explained in tradition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 44:17" href="/Ezekiel.44.17"><i>Ez</i>. 44:17</a>): “There should be no wool on them when they officiate inside the inner doors<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In order to make the point clear, the verse is slightly misquoted, “inner doors” instead of “the gates of the inner courtyard”. Since the courtyard reserved for the priests had no gates, the sentence is taken as referring to the <i>ṭriqsin</i> cubit (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:4:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.4.5">Chapter 8, Note 93</a>).</i>.” Therefore, on the outside wool may be on them. From where that they may wear <i>kilaim</i>? As it says (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 39:28" href="/Exodus.39.28"><i>Ex</i>. 39:28</a>): “The cap of byssus, the hat-turbans of byssus, the cloth trousers of spun byssus, and the belt of spun byssus, blue, purple, and crimson<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">One has to prove that “blue, purple, and crimson” mentioned in the description of the priestly garments and the gobelins of the Tabernacle always refers to blue, purple, and crimson wool.</i>.” And it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 44:18" href="/Ezekiel.44.18"><i>Ez</i>. 44:18</a>): “Linen turbans shall be on their heads, linen trousers shall be on their hips, they shall not belt with sweat.” You infer “byssus” from “byssus”, “byssus” from “turbans”, “turbans” from “turbans”.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This proves that שש means “linen cloth”. שש is defined by its use as material for the priests’ turbans, which are described as made of linen by Ezechiel. [The Babli (Note 14) interprets שש to mean “spun six-fold”; this is not known to the Yerushalmi.]</i> Just as crimson is made from a living being, so the rest is made from a living being<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since the red color of crimson is made from insects [<i>Sifra Meẓora‘</i> (14)], so the material of the series in which crimson appears should be of animal origin. {In Hebrew, the terms חיים and דוח חיים only apply to animals, never to plants.} Therefore, it should be some kind of wool. The next sentence then refers back to the discussion that only sheep’s wool and linen do not need a qualifier in their descriptions.</i>. Just as wool cannot have an epithet so linen cannot have an epithet.
Rebbi Jonah, Ulla bar Ismael in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Blended wool and flax are forbidden. What can one do? One adds more than a pound of camel’s hair and makes it insignificant<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">It is a general principle in Jewish law that one may not neutralize a prohibited substance by mixing it with a large quantity of permitted material. R. Eleazar notes that the Mishnah here decrees a remarkable exception in that wool and flax blended before spinning can be made acceptable by using for every pound of <i>kilaim</i> somewhat more than a pound of neutral material. This does not apply to woolen or linen threads which together in one garment always constitute <i>kilaim</i>.</i>. Abba bar Rav Huna in the name of Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He is Rav Jeremiah in the Babli. Since R. Jeremiah already was an authority in Babylonia before the title “Rav” was introduced, the title “Rebbi” is more correct.</i>: Blended wool and flax are neutral. Rav said, they are forbidden in mixture. Do they disagree? Rav speaks about the case when he made the material itself into a garment, Rebbi Jeremiah speaks about the case where he wants to mix it with other things.
Rebbi Hillel ben Rebbi Vales<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Chapter 1, note 30. Probably Rebbi (Jehudah Nesia) suspected the garment to contain the very expensive Egyptian byssus, used to make transparent garments. The denars here are the worthless money of the military anarchy.</i> had a garment worth 30’000 denar, (he gave it to Rebbi who) found <i>kilaim</i> in it and burnt it. Rebbi Mana had a garment worth 30’000 denar<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Shrouds for the dead may contain <i>kilaim</i>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.1">Mishnah 4</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.2-17">Halakhah 4</a>.<br>Reading of the Rome ms:רִבִּי מָנָא הָיָה לוֹ בֶגֶד בִּשְׁלֹשִׁים רִיבּוֹא דֵינָר וּמָצָא בוֹ כִלְאַיִם וִיהָבֵיהּ לְרִבִּי חִייָה בַּר אָדָא אָמַר לֵיהּ זְבָנִית לְמִית וְקִייֵם עֲלוֹי עַד קָרְחָייָה. “Rebbi Mana had a garment worth 30’000 denar in which he found <i>kilaim</i>. He gave it to Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada and told him that he had bought it for the dead. He kept it until it got threadbare (probably to sit on it).”</i>. He gave it to Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada and told him that he had bought it for the dead. He kept it until he was wrapped in it<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">When he had died.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, Rebbi Samuel bar Isaac brought a garment from the cleaner; there were on it ten threads and he rubbed them off<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The threads might be <i>kilaim</i>. Even though they were only clinging to the garment, were not sown on or otherwise permanently affixed and never could be legal <i>kilaim</i>, he insisted of removing all potential <i>kilaim</i>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, Rebbi Samuel bar Isaac commanded his house not to put a bale of wool next to a bale of flax because of the fibers<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The fibers that might be blown from one bale to the next. Latin <i>fibra</i>,<i>ae</i>, “fiber, filament”, but also cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.5.1">Note 153</a>.</i>.
“Raw silk and silk noil”. Raw silk is μέταξα<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Greek “raw silk”, early Latin form <i>mataxa</i>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 20b" href="/Shabbat.20b">Babli, <i>Šabbat</i> 20b</a> translates כלך by either μέταξα or שירא פרנדא, i. e. sera “silk” (Syriac, Aramaic), پَرَنْد (<i>parand</i>, Arabic <i>firind</i>) “silk” (Persian, Arabic). Also compare <i>sera</i> with Latin <i>sericum</i> “Chinese stuff, silk.”</i>. <i>Kalak</i> silk is imperial <i>‘gbyn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The Rome ms. here has אככין, the mss. in Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i> 2:1 have אנבין. R. Simson and R. Isaac Simponti read אצבין. In <i>Šabbat</i>, Maimonides explains כלך by “silk”; the expression there is usually taken to mean “silk noil”. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel explains that the current trade name in the Eastern Mediterranean was <i>kalka</i>, cf. Greek κάλχη, ἥ “<i>murex</i>, purple dye”. The reading אגבין is possibly a contraction from ארגון “purple”; the determination of כלך as “imperial purple” parallels κάλχη. The Yerushalmi seems to define כלך as purple silk (E. G.).</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, I went around among all sea-faring men and they told me that it was called <i>kalka</i>.
“But they are forbidden because of the bad impression.” Rav said that everything forbidden because of a bad impression is forbidden even in the most private room<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 64b" href="/Shabbat.64b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 64b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 146b" href="/Shabbat.146b">146b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 9a" href="/Beitzah.9a"><i>Beẓah</i>9a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 12a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.12a"><i>Avodah Zarah</i> 12a</a> this is accepted practice while it is rejected in the Yerushalmi, cf. <i>Roš Šabbat</i> 22:9.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> contradicts Rav: “One should not make a visible fringe of linen dyed with soot, but for mattresses and pillows it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Since linen usually is bleached, not dyed, this will look like wool. Nevertheless, such decorations are permitted in the bedroom and only forbidden where others may see them and suspect the owner of wearing <i>kilaim</i>.</i>” A <i>baraita</i> contradicts Rav: “If one’s coins were strewn before an idol he should not bow down to collect them lest he look as if he was prostrating himself before the idol. But if it occurred at a hidden place it is permitted.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This and the following <i>baraita</i> are found also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.7.3">Tosephta <i>Avodah Zarah</i> 6:4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Avodah Zarah 7:3" href="/Tosefta_Avodah_Zarah.7.3">6</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 12a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.12a">Babli <i>Avodah Zarah</i>12a</a>. Instead of “if it occurred at a hidden place it is permitted”, these Babylonian sources read: “If it cannot be seen it is permitted.” This is reinterpreted in the Babli to mean: “If he can arrange it so that he will not be seen bowing down to (or kissing) the idol, it is permitted,” for example by collecting the coins kneeling with his back to the idol. Such a reinterpretation is impossible in the Yerushalmi’s version.</i>” A <i>baraita</i> contradicts Rav: “In a fortified place one should not put his face on gargoyles in the form of faces lest he look as if he was kissing an idol. But if it occurred at a hidden place it is permitted.” A Mishnah contradicts Rav: “One does not slaughter into a pit in the ground but he may dig a pit in his house so that the blood may be collected there. In public, he should not do it so as not to imitate the sectarians.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥulin</i> 2:9, Babli 41a. It is not known to which sect the Mishnah refers. The Babli does not count the Mishnah as contradicting Rav.</i>” A <i>baraita</i> contradicts Rav: “Once he arrived at the outermost courtyard he may spread them out in the sun but not where it is generally visible.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 146b" href="/Shabbat.146b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 146b</a>, speaking of a man whose clothes got wet on the Sabbath who might be suspected of having washed them that day. In the version of the Babli, Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Simeon prohibit spreading out any clothes at all.</i>” A <i>baraita</i> contradicts Rav: “One does not pour water on the Sabbath into a sewage pipe covered the length of four cubits.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 8:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.8.9.1">Mishnah <i>Eruvin</i> 8:10</a>, a <i>baraita</i> permitting to pour the water in wintertime <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 88b" href="/Eruvin.88b">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 88b</a>; a parallel text <i>Eruvin</i> 8:10 (fol. 25b). The situation referred to is a sewage pipe leading away from a courtyard. The water poured out in the courtyard will run into the pipe and flow away into the public domain. While no Sabbath prohibition has been violated by pouring out water in one’s own private courtyard, the water flowing in the pipe will give the impression that it was poured into the public domain, a desecration of the Sabbath. The rabbinic prohibition of pouring water does not apply if either the pipe is covered all the way or if the pipe was carrying water before anything was poured out into the courtyard.</i>” We have stated on this: If water was flowing in it it is permitted, in the winter it is permitted. If aquaeduct pipes spout it is forbidden. Bar Qappara stated that if it occurred at a hidden place it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Against the opinion of Rav. This statement is not in the Babli.</i>. All these statements contradict Rav and he cannot explain them.
“It is permitted for mattresses and pillows.”<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Quote from the <i>baraita</i> in the preceding paragraph. The reasons are explained in the last paragraph of this Halakhah.</i> That means if they are empty but if stuffed they are forbidden. When they are put on a stone bench, but on a bed they are forbidden even empty.
If somebody was walking in public and discovered that he was wearing <i>kilaim</i>. Two Amoraïm, one said he is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">He has to strip off the garment immediately.</i>, the other one said he is permitted. The one who said he is forbidden, because it is a word of the Torah. The one who said he is permitted follows what R. Zeïra said: The dignity of the public is important enough to temporarily override a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 19b" href="/Berakhot.19b">Babli, <i>Berakhot</i> 19b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 3b" href="/Megillah.3b"><i>Megillah</i> 3b</a> this statement is anonymous, possibly tannaïtic, and unopposed. In the Babylonian version, the reason is self-esteem (כבוד הבריות) rather than the dignity of the public.</i>.
It was stated: One does not investigate for a dead person<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Shrouds for the dead may contain <i>kilaim</i>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.1">Mishnah 4</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.2-17">Halakhah 4</a>.<br>Reading of the Rome ms:רִבִּי מָנָא הָיָה לוֹ בֶגֶד בִּשְׁלֹשִׁים רִיבּוֹא דֵינָר וּמָצָא בוֹ כִלְאַיִם וִיהָבֵיהּ לְרִבִּי חִייָה בַּר אָדָא אָמַר לֵיהּ זְבָנִית לְמִית וְקִייֵם עֲלוֹי עַד קָרְחָייָה. “Rebbi Mana had a garment worth 30’000 denar in which he found <i>kilaim</i>. He gave it to Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada and told him that he had bought it for the dead. He kept it until it got threadbare (probably to sit on it).”</i> nor in the house of study<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">A place of public assembly.</i>. Rebbi Yose was sitting and teaching where a dead body was present. He did not say anything either to those who left or to those who remained sitting<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.16"><i>Berakhot</i> Halakhah 3:1 (Notes 68</a>,69). The story is inserted here to emphasize the statement of R. Zeïra, that in the house of study one tolerates not only temporary transgression of the law of <i>kilaim</i> but also of the prohibition of defilement of Cohanim.</i>. Rebbi Immi was sitting and teaching when one said to his neighbor: you are wearing <i>kilaim</i>. Rebbi Immi told him: strip yourself of your garment and give it to him.
It was stated: There is no provisional <i>kilaim</i> in the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since the belt of the Cohen was <i>kilaim</i>, he was not permitted to wear priestly garb outside the Temple precinct even for a second. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tamid 27b" href="/Tamid.27b">Babli, <i>Tamid</i> 27b</a>, there is a dispute whether the belt of a simple priest was <i>kilaim</i>.</i>. But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Yoma (Lieberman) 1:16" href="/Tosefta_Yoma_(Lieberman).1.16">Tosephta <i>Kippurim</i> 1:16</a>, formulated only for the High Priest. A parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 20b" href="/Zevachim.20b">Babli <i>Zebaḥim</i> 20b</a> is formulated for a common priest: “If he stepped outside the wall of the courtyard, if it was for any length of time …” It is explained that he stepped outside to relieve himself. Since the lavatory for priests was subterranean (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Tamid 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Tamid.1.1">Mishnah <i>Tamid</i> 1:1</a>), not holy and not profane, the prohibition of <i>kilaim</i> does not apply there.</i>: A Cohen who stepped outside to talk with somebody, if it was for any length of time he needs immersion, if it was for a moment he needs to wash hands and feet<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">If the Cohen is forbidden to leave the Temple precinct in his priestly garment, how could the case of the <i>baraita</i> ever arise? The answer is that this was possible only for the High Priest on the Day of Atonement when he wore linen garments; he might not leave the precinct if he wore his “golden” garments that were all <i>kilaim</i> except for his hat. It is reported in Mishnah <i>Yoma</i> that sometimes the High Priest spoke to the king (or a visiting foreign king) during the few breaks he had on the day of Atonement.</i>? One for the golden garments, the other for the linen garments.
(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev</i>. 19:19</a>) “[<i>Kilaim</i> cloth] shall not come upon you.” You are permitted to spread it out under you but the Sages said one should not do it lest one thread should be over his flesh<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Kedoshim, Chapter 4 18" href="/Sifra,_Kedoshim,_Chapter_4.18"><i>Sifra Qedošim Pereq</i> 4(18</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 14b" href="/Beitzah.14b">Babli <i>Beẓah</i> 14b</a>. The Babli is much more restrictive and decrees that one may not sit on a <i>kilaim</i> carpet even if the latter is covered by ten layers of unobjectionable material.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Hand towels, scroll wraps<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The covers of Torah scrolls. Wrappers of other book scrolls are not subject to <i>kilaim</i> even for R. Eliezer.</i>, towels of the bath attendant<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The סַפָּג is the bath attendant using the סְפוֹג, Greek σπόγγος “sponge”.</i> are not subject to <i>kilaim</i>. Rebbi Eliezer forbids. Barber’s towels are forbidden for <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">These are put on the client’s shoulders and breast, they are “on him”.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Hand towels, etc.” There<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 24:12" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.24.12">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 24:12</a>. The entire chapter deals with classification of the types of impurity of כלים, i. e., vessels, tools, and garments. Vessels can only acquire original impurity if they are made to be sat on; they become actually impure if a person with gonorrhea sits on them, if they carry his “load”. In all other cases, vessels can only acquire derivative impurity; this is exemplified by a vessel standing in the same tent with a corpse. Hence, “impure by the dead” in these Mishnaiot simply means “may acquire derivative impurity.” Vessels or textiles that are only used to serve other vessels, not humans directly, cannot become impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 16:7" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.16.7">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 16:7</a>). Cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 2:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.2.3.1"><i>Demay</i>, Chapter 2, Note 136</a>, and above <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 2:8:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.2.8.7">Chapter 2, Note 180</a>.</i>, we have stated: “There are three kinds of towels. Hand towels may become impure by load; scroll wraps may become impure by the dead; the wrappings of the Levites’ harps<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The mss. of the Maimonides tradition read “wrappings (of the dead) and covers of Levites’ harps.”</i> are always pure.” Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, because one may put them on the pillow and sleep on them. Rav said, because he puts them under his upper arms<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A reason has to be found why hand towels are considered manufactured for the purpose that either one should sit or lie on them or exert pressure (“load”) on them, for only this can make them susceptible to the impurity of “load”.</i>.
Rav said they<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The three kinds mentioned as not under the laws of <i>kilaim</i>.</i> are forbidden and practice follows him who forbids. Why does he not say one follows Rebbi Eliezer. There are Tannaïm who switch their statements<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 5:17" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).5.17">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 5:17</a>, R. Meïr presents the text of the Mishnah, R. Jehudah switches the names and says that the Sages forbid. In a dispute between R. Meïr and R. Jehudah, practice as a rule follows R. Jehudah.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan put a cloth on his garment. Is that not forbidden because of <i>kilaim</i>? [He did it] so that his garments should not wear out<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">He never wore them as garment.</i>. Rebbi Zeriqan said, they gave Rebbi Avina an egg on a towel containing <i>kilaim</i>, but he did not accept it because of<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.1">Mishnah 5</a>; he did not want to use <i>kilaim</i> to protect his hands from the heat of the egg.</i> “they<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Sellers of <i>kilaim</i> garments.</i> should not intend to use them as protection from the sun in summer and from the rains in winter.”
“Scroll wraps.” Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: Because he makes them into a kind of container<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Greek θήκη. R. Jonathan follows the text of our Mishnah that scroll wraps are not subject to <i>kilaim</i>. However, the reason is not that they are only to serve other vessels (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.2.1">Note 51</a>) because in that case there would not have been any need to specify them in the Mishnah.</i> and puts his Torah scroll on them. Rebbi Abba Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph in the name of Rav: Because he uses it to warm his hands<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 14b" href="/Beitzah.14b">Babli <i>Beẓah</i> 14b</a>, this reason is given for the requirement that curtains may not contain <i>kilaim</i>.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said to Rebbi Abba: Without that would it not be forbidden because of <i>kilaim</i>? He said to him, is not Rav the author? And Rav said they are forbidden and practice follows him who forbids<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Rav gives the reason why he rules following R. Eliezer in the Mishnah.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said to Rebbi Abba, and why did you not say to him that he removes it full<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah scroll is put into its ark or chest wrapped in its wrapper. Since the wrapper is dedicated to a holy object, it should become forbidden for all profane use and the reason given by Rav could not possibly apply. The prohibition of the use of ancillaries to holy objects is explicit in a Babylonian <i>baraita</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 26b" href="/Megillah.26b">Babli <i>Megillah</i>26b</a>); R. Abba points out that adoption of that Babylonian standard would invalidate Rav’s reason to apply the standard of <i>kilaim</i> to wrappers of Torah scrolls.</i>. If he had said to him, would it not be forbidden for usufruct, what could you have said to him?
“Towels of the bath attendant,” sometimes seeing his teacher he wraps himself in them<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, we follow Rav in his ruling that <i>kilaim</i> is forbidden for bath towels.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: women’s bath towels<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan’s statement is a <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 5:16" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).5.16">Tosephta (<i>Kilaim</i> 5:16</a>); there the term is בלארי Latin <i>balnearia</i>,<i>ium</i>, n. “bathing utensils.” The Tosephta adds that these are not subject to <i>kilaim</i> only if they are pieces of cloth, not sewn.</i> are not subject to <i>kilaim</i>. Women’s bath towels are the same as men’s bath towels. What are men’s bath towels? The rabbis of Caesarea say, אנטיטיה<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In Mo'ed Qatan 3:2 (fol. 82a) the reading is אנטינייא. One might derive the word from Syrian Arabic نطا נטא "to be wet"; the meaning would be wet towels. Another possible derivation is from Latin <i>natatorius</i> (adj.) "belonging to a swimmer" (E. G.).</i>. Towels of the bath attendant, that means if he intends them as clothing, but if he does not intend them as clothing, not about that<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of <i>kilaim</i> does not apply. Since the reason for following Rav here applies only to men, even Rav has to agree that women’s towels may contain <i>kilaim</i>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Shrouds of the dead and the pack-saddle of the donkey do not fall under <i>kilaim</i>. One should never carry a pack-saddle on his shoulder even to transport garbage.<br>Sellers of clothing sell in the professional way<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">They may put <i>kilaim</i> cloth over their arms to present it to Gentile buyers. They may not use that cloth to shield themselves from the sun.</i>, but they should not intend in summer because of the sun and in winter because of the rains. But the religious ones<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">For this meaning of צנוע cf. <i>Demay</i> Halakhah 6:8.</i> let them hang over their shoulder on a stick.<br>Tailors sew in the professional way, but they should not intend in summer because of the sun and in winter because of the rains. But the religious ones sew on the ground.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Shrouds of the dead, etc.” It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 88:6" href="/Psalms.88.6"><i>Ps</i>. 88:6</a>): “Among the dead is freedom.” When a person dies he becomes free from the Commandments.
Rebbi commanded three things on his death-bed<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the two Yerushalmi sources mentioned in the previous Note, there is a Babylonian source <i>Ketubot</i> 103. Since the Mishnah mentions shrouds, there follows a lengthy insertion about burials.</i>: My widow should not move from my house; do not eulogize me in small towns; and he who served me during my lifetime should serve me in my death. “My widow should not move from my house,” is that not a Mishnah? “The widow who said, I cannot possibly move from my husband’s house …<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 12:3</a>: “If a widow said, I cannot possibly move from my husband’s house, the heirs may not say, go to your father’s house and we shall support you there, but they support her and give her an apartment corresponding to her standard of living.” The Mishnah does not require that the widow keep the identical apartment she occupied during her marriage, only an equivalent one. In the tradition of the Babli, Rebbi’s widow was not the mother of his children, his heirs.</i>” Rebbi Dositheos<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.2-17"><i>Ketubot</i> 12:3</a> (fol. 35a), ר׳ דוסא. In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 100:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.100.3"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 100(3</a>), ר׳ דוסתאי, Dositheos. The text in <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> seems to be the original text from which the one here is an abbreviation.</i> said, that they should not say, this is the house of the patriarchate and must serve the patriarchate<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>, the argument would have been valid if the house of the patriarch had been built with public money. But since Rebbi built his house with his own money, his successor could not occupy this house.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar bar Yose said, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian version is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 103a" href="/Ketubot.103a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 103a</a> and, in shortened form, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Ketubot (Lieberman) 11:5" href="/Tosefta_Ketubot_(Lieberman).11.5">Tosephta <i>Ketubot</i> 11:5</a>: The woman whose husband died lives in the houses she lived in during her husband’s lifetime, uses slaves and handmaidens, silver and gold vessels she used during her husband’s lifetime, for so he writes to her (in the marriage document): You shall live in my house and be supported by my property as long as you are a widow in my house.</i>: “She lives in the houses just as she lived while her husband was overseas, she uses silver and golden vessels just as she used to while her husband was overseas, she is fed just as she was fed while her husband was overseas.” “Do not eulogize me in small towns” because of the quarrels<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">That there should not be competition among places which were not really able to provide decent eulogizers. The Babli (<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. 103b) explains that in small villages the crowds are too small to give the honor due.</i>. “He who served me during my lifetime should serve me in my death;” Rebbi Ḥananiah from Sepphoris said, for example Yose Ephrati and Joseph from Haifa. Rebbi Ḥizqiah added, do not use many shrouds on me and let my coffin have holes at the bottom<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">(Cf.<i>Šulḥan ‘ārûkh Yôrē Dē‘ā¶</i>362.) In <i>Gen</i>. rabba, the explanation is that the body should decompose rapidly. It is talmudic doctrine that the soul suffers during decomposition of the body and, except for those sinners whose place is in hell, the suffering stops when only the bones are left (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 18b" href="/Berakhot.18b">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 18b</a>).</i>. These words say that Rebbi was buried in a single shroud since Rebbi said, not as a man left, he will come<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">At Resurrection.</i>. But the rabbis say, just as a man left, he will come. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Nathan<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The quote in <i>Yalquṭ Sim‘oni Job</i> 924 reads: R. Jonathan.</i>: The garment that went with a person to the grave will come back with him. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 38:14" href="/Job.38.14">Job 38:14</a>) “The seal<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The “seal” of a person are the characteristics differentiating him from all others: voice, looks, and thinking [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:12:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.12.2-4"><i>Sanhedrin</i> Yerushalmi 3:13</a> (fol. 22b), Babli 38a].</i> turns itself around like clay, they appear in dress.” Antoninus<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">A Roman emperor of the Severan dynasty who reputedly was a crypto-Jew.
