Since all emperors of that dynasty adopted the name of Antoninus together with that of Augustus, speculations about the identity of this Antoninus are rather futile.</i> asked Rebbi, what is the meaning of “The seal turns itself around like clay?” He said to him, He Who brings the generation clothes it<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">God will not only create the flesh as described in Ezechiel’s vision of the dry bones but the clothing with it, as He did for Adam. An echo of this discussion is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 90b" href="/Sanhedrin.90b">Babli <i>Sanhedrin</i> 90b</a>, bottom.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 114a" href="/Shabbat.114a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 114a</a>, R. Yannai. The reading here is confirmed by the second sentence, since R. Joshia was a student of R. Joḥanan.</i> commanded: Dress me in beige<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.2-17">Ketubot 12:3</a> (fol.35a), בורידיקא. This cannot mean “pink” since “rose colored” in the Yerushalmi is ורדינון, Greek ῥόδινος. Since יריר means pus, יריריקא is taken to mean “pus colored”.</i>, not white and not black. If I rise among the just I shall not be ashamed, if I rise among the wicked I shall not be ashamed. Rebbi Joshia commanded: Dress me in clean white. They said to him, in what are you better than your teacher? He said to them, why should I be ashamed of my deeds? Rebbi Jeremiah commanded: Dress me in clean white, dress me in my socks<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The word נרס is not in the dictionaries. The Rome ms. has here לא לבשון לי דרסי “do not dress me in wornout (shoes)” but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 100:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.100.3"><i>Gen</i>. rabba 100(3</a>) has אלבשוני דרדסאי “dress me in my socks,” which may be the best reading. If נרס is a genuine word, it should be Arabic مَرَسَة מדסה “cord, rope”.<br>The messianic idea of bodily resurrection with the dead fully clothed and equipped is not part of actual Jewish burial customs. (But a strange echo of these discussions appears in Heine’s poem about a dying veteran of Napoleon’s failed Russian campaign, who wants to be buried in his native France, wearing his military decorations, gun in hand, sword by his side. He plans to rise from his grave fully armed, ready to protect his Emperor, who at the time was widely regarded as a messianic figure:<br>Das Ehrenkreuz am rothen Band<br>Sollst du aufs Herz mir legen;<br>Die Flinte gieb mir in die Hand,<br>Und gürt mir um den Degen.<br>Dann steig’ ich gewaffnet hervor aus dem Grab,<br>Den Kaiser, den Kaiser zu schützen! (H. Heine, Romanzen, <i>Die Grenadiere</i>). (E. G.)}</i>, put my shoes on my feet and a walking stick in my hand, lay me on my side, when the Messiah comes I shall be ready.
The<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Parallels <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.2-17"><i>Ketubot</i> Yerushalmi 12:3</a> (fol. 35a), Babli 104a.</i> people from Sepphoris said: We shall kill anybody who tells us that Rebbi died. Bar Qappara associated with them, his head covered and his garments torn. He said to them: Earthlings and angels held the Tablets of the Covenant; the angels were stronger and they took away the Tablets. They said to him: Rebbi died. He said to them: You said it. They tore their clothes and the sound of the tearing was heard at Goffta<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">An unidentified place which had a synagogue (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:1:2-22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.1.2-22"><i>Nazir</i> 7:1</a>).</i> at a distance of three <i>mil</i>.
Rebbi Nahman in the name of Rebbi Mana: Miracles happened that day. It was Sabbath eve and all the surrounding towns came together to eulogize him. They made him [Rebbi] rest in eighteen synagogues until they brought him to his resting place but the day extended for them until each one had time to get to his house, fill an amphora of water, and light the candles. When the sun set, the rooster crowed<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">To announce the following morning. This clause is missing in the Rome ms. In the parallel in the Babli, only the last two sentences of this paragraph appear.</i>. They were anxious and said, maybe we did desecrate the Sabbath? There came a voice and said to them, every one who was not lazy for the eulogies of Rebbi shall be announced for the life of the Future World, except the fuller<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli it is explained that during Rebbi’s stay in Sepphoris, the fuller appeared every day in his court but on the day Rebbi died, the fuller was absent for some reason.</i>. When the latter heard this, he ascended the roof, threw himself down, and died. There came a voice and said, including the fuller.
Rebbi dwelt in Sepphoris seventeen years and applied to himself (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 47:28" href="/Genesis.47.28"><i>Gen</i>. 47:28</a>): “Jacob lived in Egypt seventeen years,” Jehudah lived in Sepphoris seventeen years. Of these he suffered from toothache for thirteen years. {Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, all these thirteen years no woman lying-in died in the Land of Israel and no pregnant woman had a miscarriage in the Land of Israel.} Why did he have a toothache<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Parallel in Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 85a. There, the calf ran to hide itself in Rebbi’s garment but did not talk to Rebbi. In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 33:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.33.3"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 33(3</a>), the calf is said to have been bellowing “as if to say, save me!”</i>? Once he saw a calf to be slaughtered when it bellowed and said to him, Rebbi, save me! He said to it, for that you were created. At the end, how was he healed? He saw them killing a burrow of rats. He said to them, let them live, it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 145:9" href="/Psalms.145.9"><i>Ps</i>. 145:9</a>) “His mercy extends to all His creatures.”
Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bereishit Rabbah 33:3" href="/Bereishit_Rabbah.33.3"><i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 33(3</a>).</i> was very meek and said, all a man might ask from me I am ready to do, except what the elders of Bathyra did for my ancestor: they divested themselves of their presidency and appointed him<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The ancestor is Hillel, <i>Pesaḥim</i>6:1, Babli 66a; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 85a" href="/Bava_Metzia.85a">Babli <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 85a</a>. The family of Hillel traces its origins to a woman of Davidic origin. The Heads of the Diaspora are the descendants of King Joiachin.</i>. If Rav Huna, the Head of the Diaspora, would come here I would let him sit higher than myself since he is from the tribe of Judah and I am from Benjamin, he is from the male line and I am from the female. Once, the elder Rebbi Ḥiyya visited him and said, Rav Huna is outside. The face of Rebbi became saffron-colored. He [R. Ḥiyya] said to him, his coffin arrived. He [Rebbi] said to him, go outside and look who wants you there. He stepped outside and did not find anybody; he understood that he [Rebbi] was angry with him<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 16b" href="/Moed_Katan.16b">Babli, <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> 16b</a>, the reason for R. Ḥiyya’s disgrace is that he disregarded Rebbi’s injunction not to teach Torah in a public place (market or street). As noted in the next paragraph, R. Ḥiyya (and his sons) spent most of their time in outreach programs to the unlearned.</i>. He did not visit him again for thirty days. {Rebbi Yose bar Abun said, in those thirty days did Rav learn from him [R. Ḥiyya] the principles of Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">In<i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>, it is added that he taught Rav all the rules in which Babylonian tradition differs from the Galilean, things he could not teach in Rebbi’s academy. In Yerushalmi sources, Rav is always called “son of R. Ḥiyya’s sister.” In Babylonian sources, Rav is called “son of R. Ḥiyya’s half-brother and half-sister.” In <i>Mo‘ed Qaṭan</i> it is added that R. Ḥiyya taught not only Rav but also R. Abba bar Ḥana, nephew from a full brother.</i>.} At the end of thirteen years and thirty days<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i>: At the end of these thirty days (when R. Ḥiyya could appear again before Rebbi following the rules of temporary bans).</i>, Elijah visited him [Rebbi] in the likeness of the older Rebbi Ḥiyya. He said to him, how does my lord feel? He said to him, one tooth hurts me. He said to him, show it to me. He showed it to him, he put his finger on it and it was healed. The next day, the older Rebbi Ḥiyya came to him and asked him, how does Rebbi feel, what is with that tooth? He said to him, from the moment that you put your finger on it, it was healed. At that moment, he said, woe on you, lying-in women of the Land of Israel, woe on you, pregnant women of the Land of Israel! He [R. Ḥiyya] said to him, that was not me. From that moment on, he [Rebbi] treated him with honor. When he [Rebbi] went to the assembly hall, he said, may the older Rebbi Ḥiyya come inside. Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose asked, closer than me? He said, beware! The older Rebbi Ḥiyya inside, and Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose innermost. Rebbi used to state the praises of the older Rebbi Ḥiyya before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose. Once, he [R. Ismael ben R. Yose] saw him [R. Ḥiyya] in the bathhouse and he did not show him reverence. He [R. Ismael ben R. Yose] said to him [Rebbi], is that the one of whom you state praises? He said, what did he do to you? He said, I saw him in the bathhouse and he did not show me reverence. He [Rebbi] said to him [R. Ḥiyya], why did you do that? He said to him, so much should come over me if I realized that I was washing myself, at that time my eyes were occupied with the allegorical interpretation of the entire book of Psalms. From that moment on, he [Rebbi] appointed two students to accompany him [R. Ḥiyya] to watch out for him.
Rebbi Assi<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is a student of R. Joḥanan, he must be the Amora usually called Yasa, Assi in the Babli.</i> fasted eighty days to see the old Rebbi Ḥiyya. In the end, he saw him, his hands trembled and his eyes were dimmed<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.2-17"><i>Ketubot</i> 12:3</a> (fol. 35a), “he saw one of his court, and a Cohen was lying next to him.”</i>. If you say that Rebbi Assi was an insignificant person, one weaver came to Rebbi Joḥanan and said, I saw the sky falling but one of your students supported it. He asked him, could you recognize him? He said, if I would see him, I would recognize him. He made all his students pass before him [the weaver], and he recognized Rebbi Assi. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish fasted three hundred fasts to see the old Rebbi Ḥiyya but did not see him. In the end, he stopped feeling bad and said, maybe he did study Torah more than I did. They said to him, he spread Torah more than you did<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 85b" href="/Bava_Metzia.85b">Babli, <i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 85b</a>, R. Simeon ben Laqish was not able to find the cave in which R. Ḥiyya was buried; there it is explicit that “they” who argue with him are heavenly voices.</i> and in addition he went into exile. He said to them, did I not also go into exile? They said to him, you went into exile to study, he went into exile to teach<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">He was a Babylonian, emigrating from rich Babylonia to poor Galilee. He reorganized elementary education in Galilee (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 85b" href="/Bava_Metzia.85b"><i>Baba Meẓi‘a</i> 85b</a>).</i>.
When Rav Huna, the Head of the Diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">He obviously cannot be identical with the Rav Huna in the story of Rebbi. The Babylonian traditions about the Heads of the Diaspora are quite confusing. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Moed Katan 25a" href="/Moed_Katan.25a">Babli, <i>Mo‘ed</i> <i>Qaṭan</i> 25a</a>, the story is told only about “Rav Huna” without a title, but in all versions of <i>Seder Olam zuta</i>, the person whose coffin (i. e., the ossuary) was buried next to Rebbi Ḥiyya is Rav Huna, Head of the Diaspora. In one of the versions, this Rav Huna has a great-great-grandfather Rav Huna. In R. Sherira Gaon’s letter (ed. Neubauer, p. 30), the second Rav Huna buried in Israel is called “from the family of the heads of the Diaspora”; he died in 608 Sel. = 296 C. E.</i>, died they brought him here. They said, where shall we put him? They said, let us put him next to Rebbi Ḥiyya the elder for he is one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ḥiyya was related to the Davidic dynasty.</i>. They said, who might want to bring him there? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, I shall go up to put him there. They said to him, you are looking for a pretext, for you are an old man and you want to go to rest there yourself. He said to them, put some string on my feet and when I shall be weak<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.2-17"><i>Ketubot</i> 12:3</a> (fol. 35a), עמית “if I become blind”, cf. Arabic עמי “to be blind”.</i> you pull. He went in and found the three arguing. “Jehudah my son, after you, and no more. Ḥizqiah my son, after you, and no more. Joseph ben Israel, after you, and no more.<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This implies that R. Ḥiyya had been buried in the burial cave of Joseph, near Sichem. They seem to imply that there is no place for Rav Huna there.</i>” He [R. Ḥaggai] lifted his eyes to look, it was said to him turn around, turn around. He heard the voice of Rebbi Ḥiyya the elder who said to his son Rav<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The sons of R. Ḥiyya, Jehudah and Ḥizqiah, while leaders of their generation, usually are mentioned without title. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 103b" href="/Eruvin.103b">Babli, <i>Eruvin</i>103b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 19a" href="/Zevachim.19a"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 19a</a>, many mss. give Jehudah the title “Rebbi”.</i> Jehudah, make space for Rav Ḥuna that he may rest there. But [Rav Ḥuna] did not accept this. They said, since he did not accept that one made space for him, so his descendants will never end. He [R. Ḥaggai] exited from there and his years were doubled<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">In the version of the Babli, R. Ḥaggai (Ḥagga) ran into a wall of fire and left the ossuary of R. Huna standing at the entrance to the cave.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">There are many parallel treatments of the topic of this paragraph. Uncharacteristically, the different sources of the Yerushalmi tradition all have their own formulation; they borrow the idea but not the language: <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 96(7), <i>Tanḥuma Wayeḥi</i> 3, <i>Tanḥuma Buber Wayeḥi</i> 6, <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> 1, fol. 2b. Only <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.2-17"><i>Ketubot</i> 12:3</a> (fol. 35b) has the same language as here. Babylonian sources <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 111a" href="/Ketubot.111a"><i>Ketubot</i> 111a</a>, Y<i>oma</i> 71a.</i> It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 47:30" href="/Genesis.47.30"><i>Gen</i>. 47:30</a>): “You shall carry me from Egypt and bury me in their burial place.” What would Jacob lose at any place he would be? Rebbi Eleazar gave an inner reason, Rebbi Ḥanina gave an inner reason, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi gave an inner reason<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Note that the chronological order is inverted. In most other sources, the argument of R. Simeon ben Laqish is attributed to R. Eleazar.</i>. What is the inner reason? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116:9" href="/Psalms.116.9"><i>Ps</i>. 116:9</a>) “I will walk before the Eternal in the Land of Life.” But the lands of life are only Tyre and its surroundings and Caesarea (maritima) and its surroundings<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">These are not part of the Land of Israel, at least not in the geography of the Second Commonwealth. Hence, the verse cannot apply to this world but must apply to the Future World which in the Yerushalmi is identified with the Days of the Messiah.</i>; there is cheap (food), there is satiety. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish in the name of Bar Qappara, the Land whose dead will live again in the days of the Messiah. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 42:9" href="/Isaiah.42.9"><i>Is</i>. 42:9</a>) “He gives soul to the people on it.” That would mean that our teachers in the diaspora would lose out! Rebbi Simai said, the Holy One, praised be He, makes the earth erode before them and they roll like wine barrels<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Gen</i>. <i>rabba</i>: “He makes cavities in which they move”.</i>. When they arrive at the Land of Israel their souls return to them. What is the reason? (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 37:12" href="/Ezekiel.37.12"><i>Ez</i>. 37:12</a>) “I shall deposit you on the territory of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">In the verse: “I shall bring you to the territory of Israel.” The return of the body precedes the return of life by two verses.</i>,” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezekiel 37:14" href="/Ezekiel.37.14">v. 14</a>) “give My spirit into you, and you will live.”
Rebbi Berekhiah asked Rebbi Ḥelbo. Rebbi Ḥelbo asked Rebbi Immi. Rebbi Immi asked Rebbi Eleazar. Rebbi Eleazar asked Rebbi Ḥanina, some say Rebbi Ḥanina asked Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Even those like Jeroboam ben Nabat and his kind? He said to him, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 29:22" href="/Deuteronomy.29.22"><i>Deut</i>. 29:22</a>) “Sulphur and salt, conflagration throughout the country.” Rebbi Berekhiah said, one asked the other and we understand nothing from it. What does it mean? Since the Land of Israel was burned, judgment was executed on it<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Once judgment is executed, the sinner is free from his sin. This opinion, spelled out in detail in <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> (cf. Note 105), contradicts <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.2.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:2</a> which excludes three kings (Jeroboam, Ahab, Manasseh) from the Future World; it is in line with the Babli’s conclusion in the name of “those looking for hints”, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 104b–105a.</i>. We have stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 54a" href="/Yoma.54a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 54a</a> (<i>Seder Olam</i> 27), this statement is attributed to R. Yose. In the old Yerushalmi source, <i>Ekhah rabbati</i>,<i>Petiḥah</i> (towards the end), the name is R. Jehudah. In <i>Pesiqta deRav Cahana</i> (Buber) p. 114a, it is R. Simeon bar Ioḥai.</i>: Seven years was the Land of Israel burned; that is what is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 9:27" href="/Daniel.9.27"><i>Dan</i>. 9:27</a>) “The Covenant overtook the public for one week.” What did the Samaritans in it do<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Samaritans were settled in Samaria by the Assyrian kings and were there when the Jews returned from Babylonia, they must somehow have survived in the meantime. The answer is shortened here [and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 12:3:2-17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.12.3.2-17"><i>Ketubot</i> 12:3</a> (fol. 35b)] to incomprehensibility. The full text is preserved in <i>Ekhah rabbati</i> and <i>Pesiqta deRav Cahana</i>: זורעין אותה מטליות מטליות זורעין כאן והיא נשרפת זורעין כאן והיא נשרפת. “They sow it pieces by pieces. They were sowing here and it was burned, they were sowing there and it was burned.”</i>? Pieces, pieces and it was burned.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 54a" href="/Yoma.54a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 54a</a> (<i>Seder Olam</i> 27), this statement is attributed to R. Yose. In the old Yerushalmi source, <i>Ekhah rabbati</i>,<i>Petiḥah</i> (towards the end), the name is R. Jehudah. In <i>Pesiqta deRav Cahana</i> (Buber) p. 114a, it is R. Simeon bar Ioḥai.</i> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 20:6-7" href="/Jeremiah.20.6-7"><i>Jer</i>. 20:6–7</a>): “But you, Pashḥur, and all inhabitants of your house will go into captivity. You will come to Babylon, die there, and be buried there<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">Since the passage is a curse upon Pashḥur, dying in Babylon and being buried there are two separate curses.</i>.” Rebbi Abba bar Zemina said, Rebbi Ḥelbo and Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina. One of them said, if somebody dies there and is buried there, he has two [detriments] in hand. If he dies there and is buried here, he has one [detriment] in hand. The other one said, burial here atones for death there.
Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina: A human’s feet guarantee to put him where he is wanted<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 53a" href="/Sukkah.53a">Babli <i>Sukkah</i> 53a</a> quotes the statement in the name of R. Joḥanan.</i>. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 22:20" href="/I_Kings.22.20"><i>1K</i>. 22:20</a>) “Who will seduce Ahab that he should go and fall in Ramot Gilead?” Might he not die in his house rather than there? Eliḥoref and Aḥiah were two of Solomon’s secretaries<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">Eliḥoref and Aḥiah the sons of Shisha were <i>Soferim</i> (<i>1K</i>. 4:4). In rabbinic Hebrew, סופר means “scribe”; late Latin <i>secretarius</i>.</i>. He [Solomon] saw the Angel of Death looking at them and grinding his teeth. He said a word and put them into space<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel in the Babli, Solomon sends them to the mythical city of Luz whose inhabitants never die, but the Angel of Death takes them at the entrance gate.</i>. He [the Angel of Death] went and took them from there, then he came laughing before him [Solomon]. He said to him, just before you were grinding your teeth and now you are laughing at us? He said to him, the Merciful told me to take Eliḥoref and Aḥiah from space and I was wondering who would put them there where I was sent to take them; He put it into your mind to do so that I could complete my mission. I went and dealt with them there. The two sons of Rebbi Reuben ben Strobilos<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">A Tanna of the fourth generation, reputed to speak Latin like a Roman. Late Hellenistic Greek στροβιλᾶς, ᾶ, ὁ “dealer in pine cones” but, since pine cones were used in pagan worship, it might be better to derive the name from Greek ἀστραβηλάτης, ου, ὁ “muleteer”.</i> were students of Rebbi. He [Rebbi] saw the Angel of Death looking at them and grinding his teeth and said, let us exile them to the South, maybe the exile atones<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The doctrine that exile atones is found in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 56a" href="/Berakhot.56a">Babli <i>Berakhot</i> 56a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 37b" href="/Sanhedrin.37b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 37b</a>. It is based on the biblical law of exile for the homicide.</i>. He [the angel of death] went and took them from there.
Ulla was an emigrant. He was dying there and started to cry. They said to him, why are you crying? We shall bring you to the Land of Israel. He said to them, how does this help me? I am losing my pearl in an impure land. One cannot compare one who expires in the bosom of his mother to one who expires in the bosom of a strange woman<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Ulla seems to hold that dying outside the Land of Israel is detrimental only for an inhabitant of the Land.</i>. Rebbi Meïr was dying in Assos. He said, tell the people of the Land of Israel, there is your Messiah<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what the sentence means. The consensus of the commentators is that he sent there so a delegation should come and return the bones of the most famous teacher of his time to the Land, and that his burial on the sea shore was only a temporary measure.</i>. With all that, he said to put his bier on the sea-cape<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Since Assos (Behramkoi) is on a high cliff, he probably wanted to be buried in a place from which the sea could be seen. גיף is the same as Syriac כיף “rock.” The verse given as the reason leads to the digression about the seas and lakes of the Land of Israel.</i> since it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 24:2" href="/Psalms.24.2"><i>Ps</i>. 24:2</a>): “For He founded it on seas, based it on rivers.”
“Seven seas surround the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The list is not only in the parallel in <i>Ketubot</i> but also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 74b" href="/Bava_Batra.74b">Babli <i>Baba Batra</i> 74b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 24" href="/Psalms.24"><i>Midrash Psalms</i> 24</a>, and, as a secondary source, <i>YalquṭPsalms</i> 24. The latter source and the Munich, Rome and Hamburg mss. of <i>Baba Batra</i> have “lake Paneas” (Banias) instead of “lake of Apamea”. The pool of the Jordan source at Banias is in the Land of Israel, Apamea is in Northern Syria (but Ḥomṣ is also in Syria). The ocean is the Mediterranean, Lake Tiberias is Lake Genezareth, the salt sea is the Dead Sea. Lake Ḥuleh in Greek sources is lake Samokhonites. What is missing in the list is Birket Ram on the Golan heights and possibly a pool at Ḥamat Gader, mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 109a" href="/Shabbat.109a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 109a</a>. Since lake Samkho is lake Ḥuleh, ימא דחולתא “the lake of the dunes” cannot be Lake Ḥuleh but might have been a swamp in the plain between Haifa and Acco since in <i>Yalquṭ</i> it is called “the lake of Acco”.</i>: The ocean, lake Tiberias, lake <i>Samkho</i>, the salt sea, lake Ḥolata, lake Sheliat, lake Apamea.” But does there not also exist the lake of Ḥomṣ? Diocletian dammed up rivers and created it. It is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 21:20" href="/Numbers.21.20"><i>Num</i>. 21:20</a>): “Looking down on the desert.” Rebbi Ḥiyya from Biria<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">An otherwise unknown Amora.</i> said, if one ascends the desert mountain one finds the likeness of a sieve in lake Tiberias, that is the well of Miryam<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The well accompanying the tribes of Israel during their forty years in the desert (<i>Seder Olam</i>, Chap. 5). The version בארה in the Rome ms., instead of בורה, is an intrusion of Babylonian traditions. The parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 35a" href="/Shabbat.35a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 35a</a> identifies the mountain as Mount Carmel. As noted by the editor of <i>Kaftor waPeraḥ</i> (p. קלט), this is an intrusion from the preceding mention of Mt. Carmel (cf. Yerushalmi <i>Berakhot</i> p. 54) since the Munich ms. of the Babli identifies the sea as Lake Tiberias. The original name of the mountain in the Babylonian tradition is lost.<br>The place of Balaam’s vision must have been near the Dead Sea, not Lake Tiberias.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the rabbis estimated it and it is assessed opposite the middle gate of the old synagogue of <i>Sarongin</i><sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Ketubot</i>, יסרוטגין. The place is unidentified.</i>.
Rebbi Bar Qiria<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">An otherwise unknown Amora. The name tradition of this paragraph is thoroughly unsatisfactory. In <i>Gen</i>.<i>rabba</i> 97 as well as <i>Tanḥuma Wayeḥi</i> it is Rebbi (R. Jehuda II Nesia) and R. Eleazar. In <i>Pesiqta rabbati</i> 1 it is (a likewise unknown) R. Beroqia and R. Eleazar.</i> and Rebbi Eleazar were talking a walk on the road when they saw coffins being brought into the Land from abroad. Rebbi Bar Qiria said to Rebbi Eleazar: What good is that going to do them? I am reading for them (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 2:7" href="/Jeremiah.2.7"><i>Jer</i>. 2:7</a>) “My inheritance your considered an abomination<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Since you did not live in the Land.</i>” during your lifetime, “you came and made My Land impure<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">By the impurity of the dead.</i>” in your death. He said to him, when they arrive in the Land, one takes a lump of earth and puts it on the coffin, as it is written (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:43" href="/Deuteronomy.32.43"><i>Deut</i>. 32:43</a>) “His earth atones for His people.<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, one puts earth from the Holy Land into or on a coffin even for burial outside the Land. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116" href="/Psalms.116"><i>Yalquṭ</i> <i>Psalms</i> 116</a> directly connects <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:43" href="/Deuteronomy.32.43"><i>Deut</i>. 32:43</a> with the earlier statement that even Jeroboam and people like him have a part in the Future World.</i>”

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <i>Birsīm</i><sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">In the Leyden ms. הבדסים, the Rome ms. היכרסין, the Cambridge ms. of the Yerushalmi Mishnah הבידסין, some Genizah fragments הבידסין. In <i>Sifry Deut</i>. 234 the readings are בורסים in most sources, but also בירסין, בידסרין, in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kelim Batra 5:3" href="/Tosefta_Kelim_Batra.5.3">Tosephta <i>Kelim Baba batra</i> 5:11</a> בורסין. <i>Arukh</i> reads בדס. Since the word itself was no longer understood by the Amoraïm, the explanation is given in the Halakhah.</i>, <i>Burdesīm</i><sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of most Mishnah mss.; the only variant is הברדיסין. In <i>Sifry</i> בורדסים, ברדסין, בירדסין, כרדסין, בררסין. In <i>Tosephta</i> ברתסין.</i>, and Dalmatian garb as well as <i>pinon</i><sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah readings פונון, פיבון and Rome ms. פיטין. The common explanation is from Greek πῖλος, ὁ, “felt (as lining of helmets, shoes, etc.); felt cloth”, but felt is commonly called לבד in Mishnah and Tosephta; the Halakhah defines these “shoes” as socks.</i> shoes one should not wear unless one checks them. Rebbi Yose says, those coming from the sea shore in maritime countries do not have to be checked because usually they are made from hemp. Padded<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah mss. of the Maimonides tradition, the Rome ms., and the Leyden text in the Halakhah, all read זדב, this has been taken as the basis of the translation.</i> shoes have no problem with <i>kilaim</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <i>Bursīn</i> are <i>birri</i><sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>birrus</i>, <i>i</i>,<i>m</i>., also <i>birrum</i>, <i>i</i>,<i>n</i>. raincoat of silk or wool, mentioned in <i>Ed</i>.<i>Diocl;</i> garments in the style of the Gallic tribe of the Burri. In <i>Sifry</i>(<i>loc</i>.<i>cit</i>. 137) the garments are described as “not rectangular”; the Gallic tribes wore trousers and jackets, rather than togas.</i>. <i>Burdesīn</i> are <i>delma</i><sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">The Rome ms. has דלמא but this is the Rome scribe’s usual style for the rhetorical דֵילֹמָא. The dictionaries explain the word from from Byzantine δουλαμά “loose garment, wrapping garment”; this would fit the context but H. L. Fleischer has shown that δουλαμά is derived from Turkish <i>dolamak</i>, “to wrap around”, cf. Turk. <i>dolman</i> “furtrimmed military jacket”; the dictionary explanation looks like an anachronism. R. Isaac Simponti reads הולמא.</i>. Dalmatian garb are short sleeved tunics and overcoats. <i>Pinon</i> shoes are socks<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides, while declaring his ignorance as to the meaning of these words, declares all four pieces of garment to be woolen <i>calze</i> “stockings”.</i>.
“Rebbi Yose says, those coming from the sea shore in maritime countries.” Does this mean “from the sea shore and from overseas countries” or “from the seashore of maritime countries”? Since it was stated: For example, Tyre and its surroundings, Caesarea (maritima) and its surroundings, it means “from the seashore of maritime countries”.
“They do not have to be checked.” That means in earlier times when flax was not found everywhere. But today, when flax is found everywhere, they need to be checked.
“Padded shoes”. There are places where they are padded from the outside<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">So that it should be clear that the shoes are not wholly made of leather.</i>. As Rebbi Zeïra said to Rebbi Abba bar Zemina to tell Bar Rishon<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Probably the shoemaker’s father was called בכור, Primus, or Protos.</i> not to sew his shoes with flax but with [leather] strips. Rebbi Zeïra agrees that if one bound a woolen belt over a linen garment that would be permitted, for if he makes himself thin, it will fall down.
They asked before Rebbi Illaï: May one sew one’s shoes with flax? He said to them, because of Rebbi Zeïra, for Rebbi Zeïra said it is forbidden. Rebbi Illaï will agree that it is forbidden to wear woolen socks over linen socks since he cannot take off the inner ones unless he first takes off the outer ones. The father of Rav Safra asked Rebbi Zeïra: May one put coins in a woolen bag and tie it with a linen thread, or put coins on a linen cloth and tie it with a woolen thread? He said to him, does the rabbi not know Rav Huna? For Rav Huna said, it is forbidden. Rebbi Avina said, it is forbidden. Samuel said, it is permitted. Rebbi Jacob bar Ada in the name of Rebbi Yasa said, it is permitted.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Only spun and woven cloth is forbidden because of <i>kilaim</i> since it is said (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:11" href="/Deuteronomy.22.11"><i>Deut</i>. 22:11</a>): “Do not wear <i>šaʻaṭnez</i>”, anything carded, spun, or woven<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">The word שעטנז of unknown etymology is taken as an abbreviation and split into three: שע ט נז. Maimonides declares נוז “to weave” as Armenian. The Halakhah will show that the conjunction ו has to be understood as “or”, not as “and”.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar said, he has turned aside and turned his Father in Heaven against him<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">This homily takes נז as shorthand for נלוז.</i>.<br>Felt is forbidden because it is carded. A woolen border<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">This meaning of פיף is attested to by all Gaonic sources and the commentaries dependent on them; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.1.7">Note 28</a>. Musaphia’s derivation from the Greek (derived from R. Simson’s interpretation of פיף as “fringes”) ἐφυφή “woof” and accepted by the dictionaries makes no sense, as recognized by I. Löw.</i> on a linen garment is forbidden because it goes around like the woven part. Rebbi Yose says, woven ropes are forbidden because he stitches them before he ties them<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote">One may not make a belt out of two strips, one of wool and one of linen, even if they are not permanently sewn together since one will loosely stitch them together so that they should not separate when worn. About stitching see Mishnah 10.</i>. One should not bind a strip of wool on one of linen to gird his hips even if a leather belt is in between<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">One may not permanently sew wool on one side and linen on the other side of a leather belt since the stitcheseven when made of a neutral material like cotton, will bind wool and linen together.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of “it”, i. e., the Torah, one might translate “He”, God as Giver of the Torah.<br>The five letter root שעטנז is declared non-Semitic. One investigates whether some three-letter combination would do. Since it does not, one concludes that the word is shorthand, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.5.1">Note 151</a>.</i> could have stated שעו and not טוי. If it had stated שעז but not טוי we would have said that spinning is permitted. But the Mishnah does not say so, but “only spun and woven cloth is forbidden because of <i>kilaim</i>.” It could have stated שעט and not נוז. If it had stated שעט but not נוז we would have said that weaving is permitted. But the Mishnah does not say so, but “a woolen border on a linen garment is forbidden because it goes around like the woven part.” It could have stated טנז and not שע. If it had stated טנז but not שע we would have said that carding is permitted. But the Mishnah does not say so, but “felt is forbidden because it is carded.”
Ropes of purple wool are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">To be used on a linen garment.</i>. Some Tannaïm state, they are permitted. He who says they are forbidden, for example those of embroiderers who tie them on<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">They use wool to embroider a linen garment. The next sentence states that a woolen belt is forbidden on linen trousers if the belt passes through waistband loops.</i>. He who says they are permitted, for if he makes himself thin, it will fall down.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Tags of weavers and washermen<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Little bits of cloth with the name of the client stitched to cloth or garment. They may not be from a material making <i>kilaim</i> with cloth or garment.</i> are forbidden because of <i>kilaim</i>. If one stiches one pin-stitch there is no connection, it does not fall under <i>kilaim</i>, and if he removes it on the Sabbath he is not prosecutable<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">If a woolen and a linen garment are stitched together by one needle-stitch only, there is no connection because the thread can be removed by simply drawing it. But if there are at least two needle-stitches, so that the two ends of the thread are on the same side, this is a connection as for <i>kilaim</i>, impurity, and the Sabbath.</i>. If the two ends appear on the same side it is a connection, it falls under <i>kilaim</i>, and if he removes it on the Sabbath he is guilty. Rebbi Jehudah says, only if there are three pin-stitches. A sack or a chest bring together for <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">One may not carry a sack or a chest containing both woolen and linen clothing. All <i>Sifry</i> mss. (<i>Deut</i>. 232) as well as the Mishnah and Maimonides mss. in the family of Rabbi Qafeḥ read מצרפין instead of מצטרפין, but there can be no doubt that in the language of the Mishnah, the <i>t</i>-passive מצטרפין has the meaning of active פצרפין. The <i>Sifry</i>, in the understanding of Maimonides, reads: “ ‘Together’ in any way, this forces you to state that sack and chest bring together.”</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Ḥanina said, not until it comes down an entire side<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">For him, woolen and linen cloth create <i>kilaim</i> only if a full seam was sown. This clearly contradicts the Mishnah which requires only two or three needle-stitches. The Halakhah does not decide whether one follows the anonymous Tanna or R. Jehudah. [This paragraph and the next are also in <i>Šabbat</i> 7 (fol. 10c)].</i>. Rebbi Yannai said, they said to Rebbi Ḥanina, get out and read! Did we not state, “if the two ends appear on the same side?” That means, only if it goes down and up. And did we not state, “Rebbi Jehudah says, only if there are three needle-stitches.” That means [the thread] goes down, up, and down. But so and so<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Either one follows the rabbis of R. Jehudah; in no case does one need more than three stitches.</i>.
A thread drawn through by means of a needle, even it if has a knot on each side, is no connection for cloth<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">If one stitch has been made and now the needle is sticking in the cloth, this does not count since the needle will eventually be removed. In order to create <i>kilaim</i>, the thread alone must cross the cloth two times, for one regular stitch. The next sentence is the justification for the rule.</i>. The thread is a connection for cloth but not for the needle. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both say, only if it is knotted on both sides. The words of the rabbis disagree since Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Jeremiah<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Jeremiah in the Babli.</i> said in the name of Rav: He who straightens out the sides on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">According to Maimonides (<i>Šabbat</i> 10:9), it is the regular procedure in sewing a garment that when a seam is sewn the two sides are stretched to be equal before the thread is knotted. According to Rashi in Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 95a, it refers to a garment ready to wear or already worn, of which a seam became loose. The explanation of Maimonides is the only one compatible with the Yerushalmi.</i> is guilty because of sewing<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">The parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 95a" href="/Shabbat.95a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 95a</a> simply says, “is guilty” without stating the reason. Then the argument of the Yerushalmi is impossible. The Babli supports R. Jonah and R. Yose in the name of R. Joḥanan, that a double stitch is counted as permanent connection only if knotted at both ends.</i>. He should have said “because of sewing and making knots.”
Rebbi Simon said, the reason of Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this should be “R. Jehudah”. Maybe the manuscript before the scribes simply had ר״י.</i> is that by the third his work becomes permanent. Does Rebbi Jehudah hold with Rebbi Eliezer? As we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 13:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.13.1.1"><i>Šabbat</i> Mishnah 13:1</a>. The parallel in Halakhah 13:1 is all in the name of R. Ulla and has the names of R. Eliezer and R. Jehudah switched. Maybe “R. Simon” here is an intrusion from the next paragraph.</i>, “Rebbi Eliezer says that he who weaves three threads at the start or one thread on a piece of weaving already begun is guilty.” Rebbi Ulla said, the reason of Rebbi Eliezer is that by the third his work becomes permanent. But here, might less than that be undone<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">If the weaver made three passes with his shuttle, he can leave his work for the next day without fear that it will unravel by itself. That does not mean that in the case of the Mishnah R. Eliezer requires three needle-stitches; maybe two needle-stitches only, and R. Eliezer may disagree with R. Jehudah about sewing. The question cannot be asked in the Babli since two stitches knotted at both ends are at least as good as three unsecured ones.</i>?
Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: They stated only “sack”. Hence not a basket. It was found stated: The basket, the sack, and the chest bring together for <i>kilaim</i>. Tents have nothing to do with <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Tent cloth may contain linen and wool; linen and woolen garments stored in the same tent do not produce <i>kilaim</i>.</i>.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked, can he and his son combine for <i>kilaim</i>? How may this be? He wears woolen clothes but his son wears linen clothes. He takes a woolen belt and winds it around both of them. Rebbi Yose said, where is it bitten<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">“Biting” means a direct connection, that one piece of cloth is inside the other or a thread is physically in both. Mere touching means nothing. נו is a contraction of ניהו “he, it”.</i>? Rebbi Ḥaggai asked, can he himself bring together for <i>kilaim</i>? How may this be? He wears a woolen sock on one foot and a linen one on the other foot. Rebbi Yose said, where is it bitten? No, it is needed if he had the ends of wounds, put a plaster<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>splenium</i>, Greek σπλήνιον. The word usually appears as אַסְפְלִנִית in both Galilean and Babylonian texts.</i> of a woolen patch on one end and a linen patch on the other end. Rebbi Yose said, where is it bitten? Nothing is forbidden unless there is biting.
